PREFACE
This Master Thesis is prepared at the Department of Electrical Engineering of the Technical
University of Denmark (DTU) in fulﬁlment of the requirements for acquiring a Master of
Science in Electrical Engineering at DTU.
The project work have been carried out between the 25th of January and the 25th of
June 2021 under supervision of Mattia Marinelli (Associate Professor at DTU), Tatiana
Gabderakhmanova (Postdoctoral researcher at DTU), Jan Engelhardt (PhD student at
DTU), Mirko Ledro (Research assistant at DTU), Zoltan Mark Pinter (Research assistant
at DTU), Gunnar Rohde (Chief Technical Consultant at Danish Center for Energy Storage),
and Martin Knappmann (Test Engineer at Nerve Smart Systems ApS).
The Master Thesis counts for 30 ECTS.

==PAGE:7==
ABSTRACT
The global energy sector is going through a major transition from fossil-based towards
renewables, following the 7th Sustainable Development Goal "Aﬀordable and clean energy".
The amount of energy produced by renewable energy sources (RES) increases, as well as the
number of electric vehicles (EVs). This growth presents new challenges for controlling and
maintaining stability in power systems. As part of a joint activity between the EU-funded
H2020 research project INSULAE and the EUDP-funded research project TOPchargE, a
multi-purpose battery energy storage system (BESS) from Nerve Smart Systems ApS will
be deployed on the Danish Island of Bornholm. The BESS allows for reconﬁguring of its
battery cell topology during operation and serves as a link between the local distribution
grid, a PV system, and two EV fast-charging stations.
As part of the projects, this thesis provides a comprehensive and thorough investigation
of the BESS through both experimental and simulation results, with particular focus on
the unique capability of the BESS to adapt its cell conﬁguration in a real-time fashion,
enabling converterless operation. A single cell- and a battery system model is developed by
conducting thirteen experiments to capture the performance characteristics and dynamic
behaviour of a battery cell at three temperatures. Each cell of the BESS is modelled
individually in MATLAB & Simulink® and validated against experimental data. The
simulation results show that the model is able to accurately capture and predict the
dynamic cell switching and voltage. However, the cell model experienced worse accuracy
above 90 % and below 10 % State of Charge (SOC). The eﬃciency of the BESS is estimated
to be 95 % during a simple charge/discharge simulation at 40 A. The losses caused by the
reconﬁgurable battery design amount to 33 %, while the remaining losses are due to the
peculiarities of the lithium iron phosphate (LFP) battery chemistry.
A complete thermal characterization at both module and cell level is performed as well.
This included the thermal time constant which is experimentally estimated to be 10.21 and
1.7 hours for the module and single cell, respectively. The simulation results show that the
thermal module model can describe the thermal dynamics with suﬃcient accuracy.
Finally, the ability of the BESS system to perform maximum power point tracking (MPPT)
of the photovoltaic (PV) production is investigated through simulation studies. The PV
system is built from scratch in MATLAB & Simulink® to represent a PV installation
located on Bornholm. To utilise the reconﬁgurable capabilities of the BESS, a MPPT
algorithm is proposed. The MPPT eﬃciency of the BESS is compared to the inverter
delivered at Bornholm. The inverter model is made as a static characterization, which
include an RMS representation and the eﬃciency as a function of power. The simulation
results show that the BESS achieves a MPPT eﬃciency of 99.36 %, whereas the inverter
has a total eﬃciency of 98 %. It is concluded that the proposed control algorithm design is
better than a traditional inverter performing MPPT.

==PAGE:23==
1
INTRODUCTION
The growth in renewable energy and carbon-neutral consumers have brought with new
challenges regarding the worldwide requirement of providing a secure and aﬀordable energy,
while ensuring everyone has access to energy.
A short introduction about the energy transition, including the global energy consumption,
the growth of renewable power capacity and the progression of the electricity prices is
included in the ﬁrst section. Then a brief introduction to the smart grids and how it can
be realised. To conclude, two projects are described that work with several innovative
technologies to realize the smart grid concept, with particular focus on a new smart battery
system.
1.1
The green energy transition
Energy has changed the course of humanity and improved the lifestyle of the society over
the past centuries. This is observed in the energy being both produced and consumed.
Since the Industrial Revolution in the 18th century, the energy system has undergone
several transformations. For example, the energy demand has experienced an exponential
growth, since countries are getting richer and their populations increase.
Figure 1.1: Global primary energy consumption by source [1].
Nowadays, the total energy consumption is mainly composed by fossil fuels, which are the
primary producers of greenhouse gasses, as shown in Figure 1.1. Therefore, if this growing
1
==PAGE:24==
1. Introduction
demand is not reduced, the global energy consumption will continue to increase, making
the green transition of the energy system more diﬃcult, away from fossil fuels and towards
more sustainable low-carbon renewable sources.
However, the world has shown awareness to the green transition during the last decades, as
the carbon neutral energy production has experienced a large growth. At the end of 2020,
the global renewable generation capacity was 2799 GW, with hydro-power accountable for
1211 GW. Nonetheless, solar and wind power are expanding fast, as shown in Figure 1.2
[16]. The trend underlines that there is a tangible interest for renewable energy, and that
the shown numbers will keep on increasing. For example, looking at a small country such
as Denmark, its goal is to become independent from fossil fuels in 2050, while producing
enough energy from renewables in order to satisfy the total Danish energy consumption
[17].
Figure 1.2: Renewable power capacity growth [2].
Even though considerable improvements have been made, the world is a long way from
reaching the 7th Sustainable Development Goal, "Aﬀordable and Clean Energy" [18]. For
its achievement, it is of fundamental importance to develop cheap, eﬃcient and reliable
renewable energy technologies, which can compete with current prices of fossil fuels and
therefore attract more investors. Some of the renewable energy sources (RES) have become
already competitive and cost-eﬀective. In the UK, in Europe and in some states of the USA,
the electricity price based on wind and solar power has become cheaper than traditional
high-carbon energy resources [3], as shown in Figure 1.3. As costs continue to fall and
wind and solar become mainstream, the renewable energy sector will only keep growing.
2
==PAGE:25==
1. Introduction
Figure 1.3: Electricity prices in [Euro/MWh] in Northwestern Europe (left) and in the
USA (right) in 2018 [3].
The growth in renewable sources and in carbon-neutral consumers increases the importance
of a solid power system infrastructure. Indeed, it is becoming more diﬃcult for grid
operators to determine how much energy will be produced at any given moment, as well
as the power demand of barely predictable consumers, such as EVs. These two combined
components pose new challenges to properly balance the electricity supply and demand. A
way to solve the problem is to shift from the traditional power system, where energy ﬂows
in one direction (from producer to consumer), to a"smarter" power system, where energy
ﬂows in both directions (from/to producer to/from consumer) to better accommodate the
electricity produced from RES.
1.2
Smart grids - the future of the Danish electrical grid
The main goal of a smart grid is to adapt and transform the existing electrical grid by adding
the necessary equipment to handle the increasing amount of digital and computerised
technology, renewable energy sources and ﬁnally the carbon-neutral consumers (e.g. EVs)
[19]. The purpose is to move the grid into a new era of reliability, availability, and eﬃciency
to provide beneﬁts for both the economy and the environmental health. The main beneﬁts
that come with a smart grid are the following [20]:
• More eﬃcient transmission and distribution of electricity.
• Faster restoration of grid after a disturbance.
• Reduced cost for operation and management of utilities.
• Lower electricity prices for consumers.
• Reduced peak demand.
• Increased integration of large scale RES.
An important aspect in smart grids is to allow energy to ﬂow in both directions (from
producers to consumers, and vice versa). To realize so, passive consumers with a more/less
predictable energy consumption are replaced with active participants that interact with
the grid by the use of automatic and smart control systems. Currently, there are diﬀerent
methods to control active consumers. Firstly, the power consumption can be shifted to a
speciﬁc part of the day where it is more proﬁtable by providing ﬁnancial incentives. Indeed,
3
==PAGE:26==
1. Introduction
by using equipment such as smart meters at each consumer, it enables the grid operators to
give a sudden activation request in order to manage and control consumers’ behaviour [21].
Secondly, the centralised electricity market which often is built as zones (mostly national) is
converted into a more decentralised market, by ignoring boundaries and other constraints,
also called nodal-pricing [22]. This conversion would enable both the consumers to be
more ﬂexible and allow suppliers to expand their strategies by incentivising investments
in generation in areas with high prices[23]. Thirdly, the traditional energy system, where
the energy production is concentrated in a few and large power plants, is turned into
a decentralised energy system, where many small power producers such as wind farms,
solar farms and other small generators are placed. However, the increasing amount of
unpredictable and ﬂuctuating power sources (such as wind and solar farms) are often the
reason behind excess in power injection into the grid during periods with low demand, or
in congested areas. Therefore, one of the main topics about today’s technology is about to
store the excess energy that is often produced by renewable sources, which can be used
later when the grid experiences very high demand while low renewable production. A
technology that can satisfy the mention request is a battery energy storage system (BESS),
which is the main actor in this project.
Figure 1.4: Traditional- and Smart Grid concepts [4].
1.3
H2020 INSULAE and TOPChargE
The H2020 INSULAE Project aims to decarbonize European islands. In the EU over 15
million people live on islands. The energy systems on islands are often a small isolated
microgrid, that are not as eﬀective, reliable, and economic friendly as the main grid. Small
4
==PAGE:27==
1. Introduction
micro-grids often has an electricity price that is 3 to 4 times more expensive than the
mainland. It has 2 to 3 times higher polluting, much higher losses and it is very dependent
on external supply, as over 50% of the power generation relies on fossil fuel imports [24, 25].
The purpose of the INSULAE project is to demonstrate real operation of several innovative
technologies based on RES, and based on the gathered experience to generate action plans
for several European islands, also called Lighthouse Islands. Among the islands is the
Danish Island of Bornholm.
The goal of the TOPChargE project is to provide a 350 kW high-power charger with an
integrated battery-buﬀer. This solution is independent of battery voltage and is going
to be the electricity supply solution for EVs, as it is able to diminish grid peak loads.
Approximately half a million chargers will be integrated in Europe in the years 2020-
2025 [26]. Furthermore, the charger is equipped with a unique feature that has an easy
modular design and low connection fees which combined makes it a fast and aﬀordable
solution.
TOPChargE is developing a high-power electric vehicles supply equipment
(EVSE) that can be put into service without any need for large investments into the electric
grid infrastructure. And with an integrated battery-buﬀer, realised by the world’s ﬁrst
reconﬁgurable battery with variable topology, most challenges of the conventional battery
management system (BMS) can be removed and thereby make DC/DC power conversion
a much more smooth process[26].
The new reconﬁgurable battery system at Bornholm
As part of the joint activity between the EU-funded H2020 research project INSULAE and
the EUDP-funded research project TOPchargE, a multi-purpose smart battery system will
be deployed on the Danish Island of Bornholm. As shown in Figure 1.5 the core of the
energy system consists of a battery energy storage system that serves as a link between
the local distribution grid, a PV system, and two EV fast-charging stations. The purpose
of the BESS is to serve as a buﬀer in the grid to decrease the impact of EV charging on
the grid and to provide ﬂexibility for integrating renewable energy sources, thus providing
a better coordination between the diﬀerent components in the energy system.
Figure 1.5: Overview of the deployed system in Bornholm.
5
==PAGE:28==
1. Introduction
The BESS is provided by Nerve Smart Systems ApS (NSS) and it allows for active
reconﬁguration of its cell topology.
By doing this it is possible to operate each cell
according to their state and health. This help to reduce the ageing and unbalance issues
that follows an original BESS. The reconﬁgurable BESS is able to utilize the weak cells
by constantly monitoring the state of the cell, in contrast to a traditional system where
it would have to be permanently bypassed, since the weakest cell determines the overall
performance of an original battery system.
Furthermore, the battery voltage of the
reconﬁgurable battery system can actively be adjusted by changing the cell topology. This
should allow the reconﬁgurable battery system to oﬀer new options as opposed to not
needing a DC-DC converter to connect the batteries and a DC component. This could be
to provide fast-charging of EVs[27] or to replace a DC-DC converter preforming MPPT.
The BESS that will be used at Bornholm is able to provide 312 kWh of capacity. This
means that there will be 3 dual strings with 6 modules per string, resulting in a total of
972 cells.
However, this thesis will focus on the cell engagement logic in the new reconﬁgurable
battery concept and the direct coupling of the strings with other components meaning
that the investigations will look at a single dual-string and one component at a time, and
not at the coordination of the strings or the energy management of the complete system.
A dual-string consists of 6 modules per string, where each module have 27 cells. The cell
is based on lithium iron phosphate (LFP) and they have a nominal capacity and voltage
of 100 Ah and 3.2 V respectively.
The scope of this project is to develop a single-cell model based on the LFP cell and then
scale it up to represent the whole reconﬁgurable battery on system level. In addition, the
cell- and the battery system model will include a complete characterization of the thermal
dynamics.
The model will then be used to investigate the battery model’s ﬁdelity when interacting
with other components of the energy system. As shown in Figure 1.5 the components of
interest are the inverter, the PV system, and the EVs. However, as the interaction between
the EV and the battery have been investigated in [28, 27], the focus will be on the PV
system and the inverter.
6
==PAGE:29==
1. Introduction
1.4
Thesis objective
The purpose of this thesis is to provide an investigation of the newly developed multi-
purpose smart energy battery system which is to be deployed at Bornholm. By building
a model of the battery, it is possible to get familiar with the performance characteristics
and dynamics of the battery, hence making it possible to explore the features of the
reconﬁgurable battery system. The designed model will be validated against experimental
data. The validated model will help give a better understanding of the interactions between
the battery and other components of the energy systems, more speciﬁcally the inverter
and PV system, which will help maximize the overall eﬃciency of the energy system. The
objective is to develop a static characterization of the inverter, and to investigate whether
the reconﬁgurable battery system can provide MPPT functionality by developing a new
decision making-algorithm that utilizes the reconﬁgurable cell topology. Finally, the model
will be used to investigate thermal characteristics of the battery system, which will help
understand the battery limits and which improvements that can be made to increase the
performance, safety and lifetime expectancy.
During the thesis, the following questions will be investigated:
• What components are necessary to consider when developing an electric dynamic
equivalent model on cell- and battery level, and how does the model perform against
experimental results?
• How does the battery perform on the system level and how well does the model
predict cell switching dynamics?
• What is the battery system eﬃciency and the loss distribution?
• How can a static characterization of the inverter be made?
• What is the thermal time constant of a battery module and how would it be
determined?
• What are the limits of the developed battery model regarding the thermal
characteristics?
• How can a decision-making algorithm be designed to provide PV MPPT functionality
by using the Nerve Switch to engage and bypass individual cells?
• How eﬃcient is the battery at performing direct MPPT on PV panels compared to
the standard method of using an inverter?
• When is it more convenient to use a reconﬁgurable battery system instead of a
traditional system?
7
==PAGE:30==
1. Introduction
1.5
Thesis outline
This thesis is based on the above-mentioned objectives, arranged as seven chapters. Starting
with the necessary theoretical background knowledge, and then progressing towards the
development of the various models, the models are then validated against experimental
results, where the main results are summed up and discussed. The validated models are
then used for diﬀerent simulative studies. Finally, the thesis results are wrapped up in a
conclusion. A short description of the chapters can be read below:
• Chapter 2 begins with introducing the fundamental background knowledge needed
to understand the project. This includes an introduction to battery cells, as well as the
reconﬁgurable battery system, a short description of an inverter and thermodynamics,
followed ﬁnally a detailed description of PV systems.
• Chapter 3 describes the modelling approach for each individual model.
This
includes a description of the assumptions that were made, a model overview, the
necessary experiments to characterize the models, and ﬁnally the implementation
and realisation of the models.
• Chapter 4 focuses on validating the cell- and battery system model. This involves the
designed test procedures which is applied on the actual system to collect experimental
measurements. The measurements are then used in the models to recreate the
experiment. Finally, the results are evaluated and discussed.
• Chapter 5 focuses on the simulative studies.
The validated battery system
model is used to evaluate its eﬃciency and performance when interacting with
other components of the energy system. The studies are divided into three parts;
investigation of the BESS system eﬃciency, the thermal model behaviour, and the
MPPT capabilities of the reconﬁgurable battery system when directly coupled to a
PV system.
• Chapter 6 concludes and summarise the results found throughout the thesis.
• Chapter 7 suggests future work for the developed models.
Chapter 2 & 3 is further divided into the models developed throughout the thesis. These
models are presented in the following order: cell-, BESS-, inverter-, thermal-, and PV-
model.

2
THEORETICAL BACKGROUND
This chapter will provide the theoretical background necessary to get a good understanding
of the investigations that have been performed during this project.
The theoretical
background will include:
• An introduction to battery cells (2.1).
• An explanation of reconﬁgurable BESS and the Nerve Switch (2.2.3).
• Short description of inverters (2.3).
• Introduction to thermal dynamics (2.4).
• Detailed description of the PV system (2.5).
2.1
Introduction to battery cells
2.1.1
Basic cell
Cells are the smallest building blocks of a battery and is the smallest individual
electrochemical structure capable of holding charge. The voltage delivery from a cell
is dependent on the type of materials, as well as a few other factors which will be
investigated during modelling.
Cells are often categorised as either primary or secondary cells. The main diﬀerence
between these two types is their capabilities to reverse the chemical reaction. Primary cells
are non-rechargeable, and the chemical reaction can therefore not be reversed by imposing
voltage onto the cell. This means a primary cell can be discharged once in its lifetime
before deconstruction. Secondary cells can reverse the chemical reaction and be re-charged.
Thereby allowing it to cycle multiple times during its lifetime.
In a single battery cell, the voltage, charge, and current capabilities are limited by the type
of cell. With currently known material combinations, this will be limited to a few volts,
which often is insuﬃcient. To leverage the limited capabilities of a single cell, multiple cells
may be connected in series or parallel. This will be described further in the section 2.2.
2.1.2
How cells work
Cells in their most basic explanation operates by exploiting the potential in a chemical
reaction by using a separation membrane preventing the reaction taking place without an
electrical load. As seen in Figure 2.1, the cell’s primary components are the electrodes
housing the material for the chemical reaction. The separation membrane prevents electron
transfer, and thereby the chemical reaction and the current collectors which the electric
load is attached to.
9
==PAGE:32==
2. Theoretical background
Figure 2.1: Lithium-ion cell equivalent schematic [5].
Chemical reaction
The chemical reaction used in cells is called a "Redox"-reaction. Which is an abbreviation
of "reduction" and "oxidisation". The naming originates from the two reactions taking place
when putting two materials which would naturally exchange electrons due to their diﬀerent
potential. For the material releasing an electron, the atom oxidisation level will increase
and is therefore called oxidation. Meanwhile the material receiving the released electron
will decrease the oxidisation level, thereby experiencing a reduction reaction, therefore the
name "Redox"-reaction [5].
Electrodes
The chemical potential created by placing two materials next to each other, such that
one material would naturally release an electron to the other material, which translates
directly to electrical potential, attempting to transport electrons through a circuit. The
two materials are called electrodes with the material releasing the electron named "negative
electrode" or "anode". The electron receiving material is labelled "positive electrode" or
"Cathode" during discharging. Note that the positive/negative electrodes is deﬁned by the
direction of the electron transfer and therefore switches name during charging[5].
Separator
To prevent the natural exchange of electrons due to the diﬀerence in electrical potential, a
separating layer called "separator" is placed between the materials. See Figure 2.1. This
layer prevents electrons from passing between the two electrodes, but not the positive
ions. This layer separates the material electronically and prevent the chemical Redox
reaction from taking place. Instead, the electrodes is connected to a rod or plate called
"Current collector". In some cells, the current collectors can be of the same material as
the corresponding electrode. These connects the electrode to the terminals of the cells,
allowing electrons to move from the electrode into the outside world. Due to the current
collectors connecting the electrode and terminals, the terminals potential will match the
10
==PAGE:33==
2. Theoretical background
electrode it is connected to during open circuit. Thereby generating the positive (plus)
terminal for cathode and negative (minus) terminal for anode.
Connecting these two terminals creates a current path for the electrons to travel from the
anode to the cathode as seen in Figure 2.1 as discharging. These electrons are moved by
the electric ﬁeld generated due to diﬀerence in potential. In a similar manor, the positive
ions travel the much shorter path through the separator from the Cathode to the anode.
This is assuming the cell is connected to a load. If the external circuit to the cell terminals
imposed opposite potential, it can "force" the electrons to move from the cathode to the
anode. This will reverse the chemical reaction pushing the positive ions back.
Electrolyte
In some cells an electrolyte is used. An electrolyte is the medium which aid the ionic
charge transfer between the electrodes. An electrolyte is often a solvent in which ions and
some electrons can ﬂoat freely. This aids the transferring process and decrease the internal
resistance, but the electrolyte does not take an active part in the chemical reaction or
operation of the cell. The choice of solvent can limit the terminal voltage capabilities of a
cell. Using a water based solvent is for exampled limited to 2V, as this is the electrical
potential where Hydrogen and Oxygen binds together [5].
2.1.3
Material selection
There exist many types of cells, all constructed of diﬀerent materials. The most common
cell chemistries that are used today is the Lead-Acid, the Nickel Cadmium/Hydride and
the lithium-ion [29].
Lead-acid
A Lead-Acid battery consists of Lead peroxide, Sponge lead and diluted Sulfuric Acid[30].
They are cheap, rechargeable, widely available, and highly tolerant to overcharging. They
are used in many conﬁgurations such as UPS’s, machinery and other systems that requires
a lot of power. One battery cell typically has a voltage rating of 2 V which makes it easy
to design batteries with an even number of voltage. Some of the disadvantages are that
they are very heavy, have low speciﬁc power and energy and the self-discharge [31].
Nickel
The two most common types of Nickel based batteries are the Nickel Metal Hydride (NiMH)
and the Nickel-Cadmium (NiCd). The positive electrodes are made from nickel-hydroxide
and the negative electrode consists of Metal Hydride when looking at NiMH or cadmium-
hydroxide in case of NiCd. They are one of the most used battery types due to their
small compact size, high energy density, long cycle life, rapid recharge capabilities and a
moderate tolerance for overcharging. All this makes them very attractive for commercially
available applications such as toys, calculators, small dc-motors etc. [32, 33]. One cell
typically has a voltage rating of 1.25 V. However, one of the major disadvantages are that
they have a high self-discharge, the highest of the three types[34].
11
==PAGE:34==
2. Theoretical background
Lithium-ion
Lithium-ion based batteries are quickly becoming the most promising cell chemistry. The
most common lithium-based batteries is typically made such as the cathode is typically
made from lithium cobalt oxide (LiCoO2) or lithium manganese oxide (LiMn2O4) on an
aluminum current collector, while the anode consist of graphite carbon on a copper current
collector. A single cell are typically rated between 3.2 V and 3.6 V [34].
Figure 2.2: LFP cell used in the
NSS’s reconﬁgurable battery system.
Lithium-ion seems to be promising, due to low
maintenance, long cycle life, high speciﬁc energy
and charge, high eﬃciency and low self-discharge [35].
However, lithium-ion batteries require an exceptional
safety system in order to keep the voltages and
currents within the safety limits. Otherwise, there
is the risk of exposing the battery to permanent
damage. Moreover, a lithium-ion can be expensive
to produce as well and ﬁnally, lithium is a limited
material [36].
There exists a plethora of cell types with diﬀerent
materials. This is resulting in high variation in cell
performance, voltage and behaviour. This project
will focus on the LFP (LiFePO4) cell as this is used
in the Nerve Smart System’s reconﬁgurable battery
system. One of these cells is shown in Figure 2.2.
2.1.4
Modelling approaches
There are two common methods to model a cell[5]. The ﬁrst, simpler approach to modelling
cell operation is to use electrical-circuits to deﬁne the approximation of the static- and
dynamic behaviour of how a real cell’s voltage responds to diﬀerent input-currents. The
second approach is based on the actual physics that undergo the cell’s operation. Diﬀerent
physical modelling approaches is shown in Figure 2.3.
A proper cell model should be able to simulate both the cell storage capability information,
and the voltages response to a load.
Physical modelling
Physical modelling unlike the equivalent models is based on physical and chemical equations.
They allow for a much more accurate monitoring and prediction of the individual processes
happening internally in the cell. It is possible to get very accurate predictions with only
a few physical parameters, which makes it possible to extrapolate from data instead of
interpolating [5]. However, they are diﬃcult to formulate and the process of creating and
using the physical-based model is shown in Figure 2.3. Firstly, there is the molecular
scale, where the parameters for these equations can be measured directly in the lab. The
molecular scale models are often used for predicting the diﬀusion rates of the lithium within
crystalline structures of the solid particles that comprise the electrode active materials,
and when predicting the open circuit voltage. However, since there exist methods to
12
==PAGE:35==
2. Theoretical background
Figure 2.3: Diﬀerent approaches to making models of lithium-ion cells [5].
directly measure the diﬀusivity and open circuit potential, the molecular scale is often
not used. Next step is the micro-scale, where homogeneous material is looked at. This
means that the models will predict what happens inside the solid electrode and in the
electrolyte by considering them separately. It is a volume average where impurities and
imperfections are also considered, to create the homogeneous material. Then there is the
continuum scale, where the electrolyte and electrodes are considered connected. This is a
simpliﬁed micro-scale model, where each sample volume contains a portion of the electrode
and electrolyte materials. The equations take interactions between the two regions into
consideration. Final scale is taking the equations from the previous scales and putting
them into ordinary diﬀerence equations (ODE), which is very useful for fast simulations
time and easier control [5].
Electrical equivalent-circuit model
Electrical Equivalent-Circuit Models (EECM) applies knowledge of all basic/common
electronic components to build a circuit that mimics the behaviour of the observed battery
cell. A typical EECM is constructed of a voltage source, a series resistance representing
the cell’s internal resistance and one or more RC branches connected in series, which
represents the diﬀusion voltages and charge transfer processes. The model may contain
parasitic components, which include self-discharge and charging losses. However, in this
project the parasitic elements are neglected due to the high coulombic eﬃciency and low
self-discharge during normal operating conditions in lithium-ion cells [37]. The EECM can
be seen in ﬁgure Figure 2.4.
13
==PAGE:36==
2. Theoretical background
Figure 2.4: Generic EECM.
Previous modelling approaches based on EECMs, compared in [38], all have diﬀerent
degrees of complexity and accuracy. The most relevant are the EECMs developed using
one RC branch [39] and the EECMs developed using multiple RC branches [40]. According
to [38], it is proved that adding more complexity by including more than two branches
doesn’t justify the increased accuracy compared with the increased need for computational
eﬀort.
Internal resistance:
The internal resistance, also called an equivalent series resistance,
is modeled by using a single resistor. The value of this parameter is depending on both
temperature and SOC. The instantaneous voltage drop/rise at the beginning of discharging
or charging a cell, is due to the presence of this resistor.
Open circuit voltage:
Cells will supply a voltage to a load, but when the cell is
unloaded and has an open circuit, the voltage of the cell is somewhat predictable, therefore
it is called open circuit voltage (OCV). The values of the OCV depends on the SOC and
temperature of the cell. With LFP cells, the OCV varies non-linearly with SOC, where
below 10% and above 90% SOC it varies exponentially while between 10-90 % SOC it
varies close to linearly, and the voltage is almost constant.
Dynamic RC branches:
The parallel RC branches in the EECM are used to describe
the dynamic behaviour of the cell and to model the transient response. The resistance part
describes the charge transfer resistance, while the capacitor, also called surface capacitor,
is representing the internal chemical kinetics of the cell. All the parameters will vary with
changes in SOC and temperature.
Modelling methods such as the EECM, are simple systems of the cell-scale which is
relatively easy to implement. Furthermore, they are easily used in battery state estimations
and control algorithms for the BMS. The diﬀerent equations used for this model can predict
output voltage of the cell based on previous measurements of cell current. However, the
EECM is limited in regards to internal dynamics in the cell, which is critical if the objective
is to determine cell-ageing. Furthermore, in order to determine the parameters, empirical
data is required.
14
==PAGE:37==
2. Theoretical background
Since the objective of this project isn’t to build a cell-model that takes ageing into
consideration and requires a very detailed model of the internal processes, it has been
decided to use the EECM as the Modelling approach in order to simplify the process of
modelling the cell and the battery at system level.
2.1.5
Challenges associated with cell modelling
When modelling the cells, multiple eﬀects must be considered to achieve the best accuracy
possible. However, including all of the eﬀects would make the model complex, diﬃcult to
estimate and computationally heavy.
This section will provide a description of the diﬀerent eﬀects that can be considered when
modelling a battery cell.
State of health
As the cells age, their capabilities to accept charge decrease. This is a result of deformation
of the anode/cathode as they dissolve and reform through chemical reactions.
Each
reconstruction results in a slight change in the structure and thereby deformation.
The second major part of an ageing cell is the decreased voltage output from a cell. In a
new cell, the separator ﬁlm will be very thin and allow easy transfer of positive ions. The
exposed separator ﬁlm will however oxidize, creating internal resistance in the transfer path.
This oxidation will limit further oxidation slowing the ﬁlm growth, but it will never stop.
This change of the separator over time, results in increased internal resistance, limiting the
open circuit voltage.
This degradation of cell health is therefore primarily a result of the chemical reactions not
being completely reversible (perfect) and the ﬁlm thickening from oxidation.
The state of health (SOH) can be estimated by fully charging the cell and compare it to a
new cell rating. This can be calculated as seen in Equation 2.1. The SOH is then used to
estimate the increase of R0. The relationship between cell ageing and R0 is diﬃcult to
estimate without experimental data.
SOH = Cmax
Crated
· 100 %
(2.1)
State of charge
A way to describe how much capacity there is left in a cell is to use the state of charge
(SOC). The SOC is one of the most important cell parameters since other parameters, such
as the open circuit voltage, the resistances etc. relies on it. The SOC is usually deﬁned as
the ratio of the amount of energy that has been used to the cell’s nominal capacity. The
cells nominal capacity is always given by the manufacturer.
SOC = Creleased
Crated
· 100 %
(2.2)
15
==PAGE:38==
2. Theoretical background
There exist multiple methods to determine the SOC, which include: Direct measurements,
theoretical estimation, artiﬁcial intelligence [41].
• Direct measurements: This uses measurements on the physical cell such as the voltage
and the impedance. One method could be the impedance spectroscopy.
• Theoretical estimation: This could be the coulomb counting method. The coulomb
counting method measures the discharging current of the cell and then integrate the
current over time in order to determine the SOC 2.3.
• Artiﬁcial intelligence-based methods: These are the most accurate and complex
methods. These methods are adaptive methods, meaning they can automatically
adjust the SOC for diﬀerent discharging and charging conditions. An example is
neural networks, which creates/trains a model based on huge amount of data.
SOC(t) =
1
Crating
·
Z
−I(t)dt
(2.3)
Some of the methods are very complex while others are simple. However, the trade-oﬀ
will be the SOC estimation accuracy. It depends on what system is used and what the
requirements are. If the SOC calculation is needed for an BMS that controls an actual EV,
the SOC has more requirements since the SOH must be considered. While in other tasks
the SOC could be calculated by using the coulomb counting method.
OCV-SOC dependency
Figure 2.5: Open circuit voltage and SOC relationship for a typical LFP [6].
The voltage is highly dependent on state of charge. A fully charged cell will have a higher
voltage compared to a depleted cell. This relation is nonlinear for all cells, but the exact
dependency varies on the type of cell. The model therefore needs to know the state of
charge to determine the voltage output. An example of a typical voltage proﬁle for a
LFP can be seen in Figure 2.5. The diﬀerence between the curves is primarily due to the
hysteresis eﬀect.
In general, the voltage increases with an increase in charge. This is a result of more charges
are usable in the chemical reaction. The voltage will experience an exponential increase
16
==PAGE:39==
2. Theoretical background
in the low and high end of SOC% while in the middle the voltage increase can be linear
interpolated. This is due to the chemical structure preventing chemical reactions of the
inner layers until the outer layers have reacted and released their ions. As the ﬁrst layer is
fully build, the increase in voltage is limited. This create a high voltage increase at the
extreme ends. For LFP the middle range of SOC is almost constant voltage as seen in
Figure 2.5.
Hysteresis
Figure 2.6: OCV-SOC hysteresis curve of a lithium cell from [5].
As shown in the Figure 2.5 and Figure 2.6 the voltage is higher during charging compared
to discharging at the same SOC level. While the behaviour of this phenomenon are not
well understood [5], it will be referred to as hysteresis.
This phenomena result in a slight deviation in the rest voltage at same SOC, depending
on history of the change in SOC. Unlike the voltage depression described in section 2.1.5,
the time passed since last change have no inﬂuence on the hysteresis eﬀect, it is only the
change in SOC which have an impact [5].
The hysteresis eﬀect is signiﬁcant in LFP [42], and it is therefore important to consider
this eﬀect during modelling.
As shown in ﬁgure 2.6, the switch between discharge and charge is not instant and instead
has an exponential relationship. This eﬀect is larger at lower currents, as it is dependent
on SOC [5].
Self-discharge & leakage
As with most physical systems, it will naturally seek equilibrium. For a cell, this eﬀect
takes the shape of "self-discharge". A cell will naturally lose charge over time as an attempt
to equalize the potential between the two terminals, even if there are no physical connection
between these terminals. This self-discharge is a slow process and can be minimised by
17
==PAGE:40==
2. Theoretical background
proper storage. It will however deplete over time if not maintained and destroy a cell if
left unattended.
A similar eﬀect is the leakage, which is lost energy due to ineﬃciency in the chemical
to electrical energy conversion. These two forms of losses result in a diﬀerence between
injected power and extracted power. This can be a problematic when doing Coulomb
counting as it relies on measuring the actual energy transfer from the current alone and
will therefore not include these two eﬀects. The oﬀset in Coulomb counting as a result of
slight inaccuracy can be counteracted by periodically re-calibrating the SOC. This is most
commonly done by fully discharge the cell to its minimum voltage.
Temperature
It is known that batteries behave diﬀerently at diﬀerent temperatures. A few examples can
be read in [43]. Both internal losses and open circuit voltage have been observed changing
as a function of temperature.
Load current
According to [44], the cell parameters is current dependent as long as the temperature is
below 5 °C. Whenever the temperature is above 5 °C the parameters are becoming less
and less depending on the current rating applied to the cell. When working above 25 °C
the current no longer has any inﬂuence.
Cell design
The internal design of a cell has an inﬂuence on whether the cell have any inductive or
capacitive eﬀects. This is a result of how the anode and cathode is built in relation to
each other. Most textbooks explaining cells, looks at a 2D version with the anode and
cathode opposite each other with a transfer liquid and a separator. In reality, this would
be a poor use of space and would result in a large, ﬂat cell with a low energy and poor
physical packaging which is diﬃcult to manufacture. Instead, most newer cells are made
using multiple layers, in order to achieve higher energy capacities without stretching the
cell.
There are usually two ways of achieving a compact cell; layering or rolling. Rolling the
layers often results in cylindrical cells with one continues anode/cathode, while layering
them result in rectangular shapes often referred to as box or pouch cells [5].
In addition to the shape of the packaging, the spinning nature of "rolling" the cell creates
an inductive eﬀect while "plating" the anode/cathode result in capacitive parasitic eﬀect[5].
Memory eﬀect
The memory eﬀect is typically occurring in nickel-cadmium and nickel-metal hydride
rechargeable batteries. The memory eﬀect causes the battery cell to hold less charge
and it occurs when battery cells are repeatedly recharged after being partially discharged.
According to [45] the memory eﬀect is deﬁned as: A phenomenon in which a cell, operated
18
==PAGE:41==
2. Theoretical background
in successive cycles to the same, but less than a full, depth of discharge experiences a
depression of its discharge voltage and temporarily loses the rest of its capacity at normal
voltage levels.
Voltage depression
Voltage depression is the voltage drop when battery cells is constantly overcharged.
Overcharging will create small crystals of electrolytes on the plates increasing the internal
resistance until it is dissolved. This result in a lower output voltage when in use, compared
to a healthy battery (hence the voltage "depression"). In addition, this eﬀect lower the
voltage during discharge, but returns to the expected voltage level if it is allowed to rest
[46].
Deep discharge and overcharge
Discharging the battery below its allowed minimum voltage can deconstruct the
anode/cathode and permanently deform the atom structure.
This will permanently
decrease the cell capacity.
Similarly, overcharging can lead to permanent damage due to increased decomposition of
water into gas which might create too high pressure and change the cell characteristics [45].
2.2
Battery energy storage systems
This chapter will explain the important components of battery energy storage systems
(BESS), which include the battery itself and the battery management system (BMS).
2.2.1
Battery
A battery is a collection of multiple cells placed in series and in parallel to achieve a speciﬁc
voltage and capacity rating. As mentioned previously, the cell chemistry decides a ﬁxed
voltage range, therefore, to have high-voltage, a speciﬁc amount of cells must be placed
in series. The battery packs voltage is then the sum of all the individual cell voltages,
VBatt = Ns · Vcell, if cell voltages are assumed to be equal. Furthermore, each type of cell
has a speciﬁc current limit that the cell has been designed to sustain. To achieve higher
current-rates (c-rate) the cells must be connected in parallel, in which the battery packs’
c-rate is the sum of all the individual cell currents, Ibatt = Np · Icell. The choice between
how many cells to wire in series versus in parallel to achieve the required maximum power,
primarily comes down to two factors: economy and safety.
Multiple modules can then be wired in series to achieve an even higher voltage. Higher
voltages are often preferred compared to higher currents due to safety reasons, and to
avoid high losses due to Ploss = I2 · R.
19
==PAGE:42==
2. Theoretical background
2.2.2
Battery management system
Figure 2.7: General illustration of BMS and
interconnected components [7].
In this project the battery used are based
on lithium-ion cells.
These type of cells
are very sensitive to temperature, current
and voltage. If the cells are not managed
eﬃciently the operation of a lithium-ion
battery could be destructive for each
individual cell. Therefore, a good battery
management system (BMS) is especially
important when operating a lithium-ion
battery system.
A typical BMS and its interconnection with all the diﬀerent components of a battery pack
can be seen in Figure 2.7.
Among the components, there are the battery comprising the modules and the cells, the
measurement and control devices and the thermal management system.
The primary objectives of the BMS are [7]:
• To ensure the safety of operation. The BMS should be able to detect unsafe operating
conditions and respond accordingly.
• To protect the cells of the battery pack from cases of abuse or failure. This could
be active intervention under software control and special electronics that can detect
failures and isolate the failing components from the rest of the battery pack and the
load.
• To balance the cells in order to extend the lifetime of the battery under normal
operating conditions. This is achieved by having the controller communicating with
the load, advising it of dynamic limits on power that can be injected or withdrawn
over some short future interval. This is done to ensure the battery cells are not
getting overcharged or deep discharged, and that the battery is operating within its
operational-temperature range.
• To always ensure that the battery pack is in a state in which it can still fulﬁl its design
requirements. This could be to only operate the battery at a SOC of 10 % −90 %.
2.2.3
Nerve Smart Systems - reconﬁgurable battery system
The BESS used in this project is based on a battery system with a reconﬁgurable topology.
This means that the number of cells that are active can be changed during operation.
By doing this it is possible to dynamically adjust the voltage the battery system is able
to deliver. This ﬂexibility is achieved by switches that either bypass or engage battery
cells. Thus, the entire system is no longer limited by the weakest cells, since all the cells
can be controlled individually. The device which is responsible for opening and closing
the switches, as well as measure the temperature, voltage etc. of each cell is called the
Nerve Switch®(NS) and is shown in Figure 2.10. There is one BMS controller per module
(module controller).
20
==PAGE:43==
2. Theoretical background
Figure 2.8: The BESS structure. Including the Nerve Switch, the LFP cell, a battery
module, and ﬁnally a cabinet containing the dual string consisting of 12 modules
The overall structure of the complete BESS is seen in Figure 2.8 and is constructed as
follow:
• The controller board is placed on top of each cell, above the terminals.
• The battery module is composed by 27 cells connected in series.
• 6 modules are stacked up top each other creating a sub-string.
• A dual-string is made of 2 sub-strings.
Nerve Switch®
Figure 2.9: Dynamic depletion of cells within the battery over time
Figure 2.9 illustrates the idea of the dynamic battery cell engagement over time. As seen
in the ﬁgure, only a fraction of the cells is engaged at all times to achieve a set voltage. As
time passes the active cells deplete in SOC and are substituted by other cells. This allows
for a dynamic voltage range which is not found in a traditional battery pack. Notice how
not all cells are fully charged at the beginning of the timeline 2.9. This symbolises the
imbalance found between cells either due to health or charging behaviors. Normally the
battery is only as good as its weakest cell, but this is not the case with the Nerve Switch
21
==PAGE:44==
2. Theoretical background
as the weak cell E (top row) is only partially used and bypassed to allow longer battery
operation with the healthier cells.
Figure 2.10: Nerve Switch PCB.
Figure 2.10 shows the NS controller, which controls
if a cell should be engaged or bypassed by opening or
closing switch 1 or switch 2 without disconnecting the
current path through the battery. The two switches
consist of four MOSFETs connected in parallel to
reduce the current going through them and thereby
reducing the losses. The diagram shown in ﬁgure
2.11 shows the current ﬂow which is controlled by
the NS when two cells are charging. The diagram on
the ﬁgure shows the current ﬂow when two cells are
charging which is controlled by the NS. First of all,
each MOSFET such as Q12 and Q11 is an equivalent
of the four MOSFETs as seen on the controller board.
When the two cells are engaged the current will ﬂow as shown in Figure 2.11a. The current
will ﬂow through cell 1 and 2 by closing MOSFET Q12 and Q22, which corresponds
to "Switch 1" on the NS PCB shown in Figure 2.10. Now when bypassing cell 1, both
MOSFET Q11 and Q12 will open leaving both gates open for a short moment, such that
a short circuit can be prevented. The current will ﬂow through the diode (D12) of the
MOSFET as shown in Figure 2.11b. This process happens rapidly, and the diode can only
conduct such a large current for a short duration, and therefore this process requires logic
switch control. Finally, cell 1 has been completely bypassed by closing Q11. This means
the current will bypass cell 1 through Q11 but still charge cell 2 through Q22 as seen in
Figure 2.11c. When engaging a cell, the procedure is reversed. Discharging the cells follow
same procedure, but the opposite diode is used when both gates are open.
(a) Both cells engaged.
(b) bypassing cell 1
(c) cell 1 bypassed.

from the Nerve Switch [8].

2. Theoretical background
2.3
Introduction to the inverter
The inverter is deﬁned as a device that can convert direct current voltage to alternating
current voltage and vice versa. The inverters are often used in systems that include a
photovoltaic system, battery or other DC producing devices that must be converted to an
AC. This AC voltage can be supplied directly to the power system, homes, industries and
more. Modern inverters have a very high (more than 95 %) power conversion eﬃciency
and they are generally more reliable and economically feasible [47].
Figure 2.12: Full-bridge inverter [9].
An inverter typically uses power electronic
switches, called IGBT or MOSFET. The number
of switches varies depending on the type of
inverter.
A simple single phase full-bridge
inverter is shown in Figure 2.12. This inverter
has four switches, a DC source, and a load. To
convert DC to AC, the switches are opened and
closed in a speciﬁc pattern. When the switches
S1 and S2 are closed (ON) and S3 and S4 are
open (OFF), the direction of the current through the load will be positive, thereby creating
a positive half cycle. As S1 and S2 open (OFF) and S3 and S4 close (ON) the current
through the load will ﬂow in the opposite direction, creating the negative half cycle. A
full sinusoidal AC signal is then produced. The amount of ON and OFF time decides the
frequency and is often controlled by a PWM signal. The output of this inverter will be a
controllable wave that can have be a sinusoidal wave, a square wave etc.
Several types of inverters exist and three common types of inverters for powering AC loads
by converting a DC voltage to AC voltage include:
• Pure sine wave inverters - they are used for general applications.
• Modiﬁed square wave inverters - used for inductive and capacitive loads.
• Square wave inverters resistive loads - this could be solar panels which require MPPT
functionality.
2.4
Thermal dynamics
Thermal behaviour is known to be dictated by the following three laws:
• The ﬁrst law of thermodynamics; Law of Conservation of Energy, states that energy
can neither be created nor destroyed, energy can only be transferred or changed from
one form to another.
• The second law of thermodynamics states that the entropy of any isolated
system always increases. Isolated systems spontaneously evolve towards thermal
equilibrium—the state of maximum entropy of the system.
• The third law of thermodynamics states that the entropy of a system approaches a
constant value as the temperature approaches absolute zero.
The ﬁrst law dictates that the energy can not be created or removed from a closed system
and a system’s total energy can only change when energy is transferred over the system
23
==PAGE:46==
2. Theoretical background
border. Assuming the battery cabinet as a closed system, any internal electrical power
loss must therefore remain in the system as heat. The heat that is generated by electrical
losses can be describe by the following expression:
Qin = Ploss = I2
R
(2.4)
As losses always generate heat, independently of the direction of the current, the thermal
energy in the system will increase, and the heat will spread throughout the battery as
stated in the second law. As heat spreads, it will eventually be carried out beyond the
battery system border, either through the surrounding cabinet or by air.
Thermal dynamics is often simpliﬁed to an electrical equivalent, with heat ﬂow P modelled
as current and temperature diﬀerence ∆T modelled as voltage. This modelling approach,
makes it possible to identify all major paths in which the heat can transfer. The thermal
electrical equivalent uses common series and parallel equivalents to simplify the equations.
Heat transfer can only happen through three phenomena: conduction, convection, and
radiation.
2.4.1
Radiation
Radiation is based on electromagnetic waves and is the only method that allows to transfer
thermal energy in vacuum. Thermal radiation is generated by kinetic energy and is found
in any object above absolute zero. The energy is absorbed when the waves hit and heat up
an object [48].
2.4.2
Conduction
Conduction is the process where thermal energy is transmitted through collisions of
neighbouring molecules/particles. The rate of energy transfer by conduction is depending
on the temperature diﬀerence ∆T between the objects that are in contact and their thermal
resistances. Materials such as metal have a low thermal resistance, which makes it very
eﬃcient at conducting thermal energy. The conductive heat transfer can be expressed as
[10]:
Pcond = KL · A
L · ∆T
(2.5)
Where KL is the conductivity of the material, A is the cross-sectional area and L is the
length of the thermal path as shown in Figure 2.13.
24
==PAGE:47==
2. Theoretical background
Figure 2.13: Illustration of thermal conduction through a material [10].
2.4.3
Convection
Convection is the transfer of heat between a material and ﬂuids (free moving molecules).
Diﬀerent types of convection exist, such as natural convection, forced convection etc.
Natural convection can further be split into free- and limited convection [10].
Convection is calculated using Equation 2.6
Pconv = Kk · Cg · A
L0.25 · ∆T 1.25
(2.6)
where L and A is the length and cross-sectional area similar to conduction. L and Cg is
dependent on the geometry due to the natural movement of hot-cold air and is therefore
called the geometry constants. The heated molecules tend to rise while cold molecules fall
thereby generating a natural heat ﬂow. As the heat rises, the orientation and shape of the
surface is important.
By designing the object such that the aerodynamics allows for easier removal of the warm
air, it is possible to have a more eﬃcient cooling of the object of interest.
The heat transfer coeﬃcient is calculated as:
h = Kk ·
Cg
L0.25
(2.7)
where KK is the convection constant.
Free convection occurs when there is plenty of space between the heated material and
the next object, as it allows the air molecules to easily move. Convection is deﬁned free
for distances larger than 25mm [10]. For free convection, the constant Kk is deﬁned at
Kk = 2.5. Any distance smaller than 25mm such that the movement of the molecules may
be hindered by nearby objects is deﬁned as limited convection resulting in Kk < 2.5.
The constant Kk as a function of distance is shown in Figure 2.14.
Diﬀerent methods for calculating Cg and L based on multiple shapes are shown in Table 2.1
[10].
Forced convection is when the airﬂow is artiﬁcially created using a fan or a pump. This is
often seen with cooling ﬁns that have limited distance between them which makes natural
25
==PAGE:48==
2. Theoretical background
airﬂow impossible. Hence, by forcing the air through the fans the transfer of thermal
energy is increased. Some studies have found an improvement of 21% in the heat transfer
coeﬃcient for aluminium when increasing the wind speed by 1 m/s [49].

2.4.4
Thermal time constant
For an object with temperature T1 placed in an environment with an ambient temperature
T2, the temperature decay can be described by the following expression:

τth + T1
(2.8)
The thermal time constant τth is a measure for the time required for the object to respond
to a change in the ambient temperature. To determine the thermal time constant, equation
2.8 can be simpliﬁed by t = τth:
T(τth) = ∆T · e−1 + T1 = ∆T · 0.368 + T1
(2.9)
This means that at time t = τth the temperature diﬀerence between the object and the
ambient is 36.8 %. Hence, the time constant is deﬁned as the time required for the object
to change 63.2 % of the total diﬀerence between the initial and ﬁnal temperature [11]. The
thermal time constant is then calculated as:
τth = T −T1

2.5
Photovoltaic system
Solar cells are photovoltaic devices that use semiconducting materials to absorb photons
from solar radiation and convert it to electrical energy. The absorbed photons are used to
release electrons which then ﬂow through the material to generates a DC-current. The
output from the PV panel is given by the amount of irradiance that the cell is absorbing,
which depends on dust, shades, placement of PV module etc. To improve the amount
of absorbed irradiation, some PV modules can adjust the altitude angle and change its
direction such that it matches the solar azimuth angle. Furthermore, other factors such
as eﬃciency, degradation, and temperature have a signiﬁcant impact on the performance
of a PV. In this project the I-V curve for the PV system (See Figure 2.18) is of interest
since it deﬁnes the combinations of voltage and current that are achievable under given
conditions. The I-V curve does not by itself provide any information on what point the
system actually will be operating, as this is determined by the load that is connected to
the PV. The voltage across the load and the PV is the same, and the current that runs
through both are equal. Therefore, by adjusting the load voltage it is possible to adjust
the power output from the PV.
For this chapter the basic electrical equivalent, the output characteristics, the PV
conﬁguration, and the maximum power point tracking method will be explained.
2.5.1
Basic electrical equivalent and output characteristics
A basic electrical equivalent diagram of a PV cell can be represented by an ideal DC-current
source, a parallel leakage resistance Rp, a series resistance Rs, and a diode in parallel with
the current source, as shown in Figure 2.16. Two conditions are especially of interest when
modelling the actual PV and the equivalent circuit, which is the short circuit current ISC
(2.16b) and the open circuit voltage VOC (2.16c). During a short-circuit no current ﬂows
in the diode since Vd = 0, so all the current from the ideal current source will ﬂow through
the shorted leads. Since that short circuit current must be equal to ISC, the magnitude of
the ideal current source must be equal to ISC.
Therefore, an equation for the output current of the PV cell can be written as:
29
==PAGE:52==
2. Theoretical background
I = IL −Id −V + I · RS
Rp
(2.20)

2.5.2
PV conﬁguration
The described electrical circuit is a single PV cell. An individual cell is only capable of
producing around 0.5 V [12]. This means that in almost every case a single cell is not
suﬃcient for any task. Instead, a basic block for PV applications is a module consisting of
a number of series-connected PV cells.
30
==PAGE:53==
2. Theoretical background
(a) Complete diagram.
(b) When short circuit.
(c) When open circuit.
Figure 2.16: Basic PV equivalent diagram (a), PV equivalent when considering short-circuit
current (b) and PV diagram when considering the open-circuit voltage (c) [12].
If multiple modules are wired in series or parallel, they become an array.
By wiring modules in series the voltage output will increase, while wiring in parallel will
increase the current output, as shown in Figure 2.17.
Figure 2.17: Illustration of multiple series and parallel connected PV modules and the
impact on the current and voltage output.
2.5.3
Maximum power point
When a load is connected to the PV module, a current and a voltage will be produced that
result in a speciﬁc power delivered. To determine the amount of power that the PV is able
to deliver, the I-V characteristics must be investigated. A generic I-V curve is shown in
Figure 2.18, which can be used to identify parameters such as VOC, ISC and the maximum
power point (MPP) [12]. Furthermore, the power output from the curve can be seen as the
product of voltage and current. There exists two points where the power output is zero,
when the current is zero or when the voltage is zero. The maximum power point is located
at the “knee-point” at which the product of voltage and current reaches its maximum
possible value given the ambient conditions.
A PV’s I-V characteristics changes constantly as the amount of irradiance changes and
as the temperature of each cell varies. Thus, standard test conditions (STC) have been
made to have a fair comparison of module to module. Those tests include an irradiance of
1000 w/m2 and a standard cell temperature of 25 °C [12]. Figure 2.19 shows the inﬂuence
31
==PAGE:54==
2. Theoretical background
Figure 2.18: I-V curve of an arbitrary PV with MPP [12].
diﬀerent cell temperatures and irradiance levels have on the total power output of the
PV. From the ﬁgure it is observed that as temperature decreases so does the maximum
current while the maximum voltage increases. However, as irradiance changes it is observed
that the maximum current is aﬀected a lot, while the voltage only has slight changes. To
describe the PV’s performance with respect to the temperature, an indicator called NOCT
is used [12]. NOCT stands for nominal operating cell temperature. The NOCT is the cell
temperature in a module when ambient temperature is 20 °C, solar irradiation is 0.8 W/m2
and wind speed is 1 m/s.
Figure 2.19: I-V curves with diﬀerent temperatures and levels of irradiance [12].
A major problem for PVs, is that the output can be reduced signiﬁcantly when a small
portion of the cell is shaded. If no eﬀort has been made to account for this, a single shaded
cell in a string of cells can easily cut the output by half. If one cell is shaded, its current
source gets reduced to zero. This means that the voltage drops across Rp as the current
ﬂow causes the diode to be reversed, such that the diode current is also zero. Hence, the
current must travel through RP and Rs in the shaded cell into the load. Thus, instead of
increasing the voltage it is reducing it, while the added losses will increase the temperature
32
==PAGE:55==
2. Theoretical background
in the cell. To avoid this problem, special diodes are made. These are called bypass diodes
and they are typically added in parallel with modules or within blocks of cells, since adding
them across each cell would be extremely expensive. The diodes assist the current by
letting it move around a shaded or malfunctioning module within a string.
2.5.4
Maximum power point tracking
As mentioned previously, the power output from the I-V curve is highly ﬂuctuating as solar
irradiance and temperature changes. Therefore, signiﬁcant eﬃciency improvements could
be realized if the operating points for the PV could be kept near the "knee-point" of the
I-V curve throughout the daily conditions. This process is called maximum power point
tracking (MPPT). A MPPT is usually done through a DC/DC converter. By dynamically
changing the duty cycle of the converter it is possible to adjust the voltage level across
the PV, and thereby change the operating point. There exist many techniques that can
perform MPPT such as perturb and observe (P&O) and incremental conductance (INC).
Both techniques adjust the voltage and observe whether that change increased or decreased
the power delivered from the PV.
Perturb and observe
The perturb and observe method is one of the commonly used MPPT algorithms, mainly
because it is easy to implement [13]. This method only requires a voltage and a current
measurement from the PV. However, a disadvantage is that the tracking can get confused
and track the wrong way during rapid irradiance change, this is referred to as the hill-
climbing eﬀect.
At the beginning of the algorithm, the initial voltage will start at some pre-set level Vinit.
In addition, a voltage step Vstep must be chosen. Vstep must be carefully considered since
it has a large impact on how the overall algorithm will perform. If a large Vstep is chosen,
the response time to a change in irradiance will be fast while the accuracy will be low
with large oscillations around the optimal point. However, when choosing a small Vstep the
response time will be slow, but the accuracy will be much better with small oscillations.
One problem with the basic P&O method is that these oscillations can never be avoided.
From Figure 2.20 the P&O is shown. It starts by measuring the voltage and current,
and then calculate the power. It then compares if the power is lower or greater than the
previous power. Thereafter, the voltage is compared with the previous voltage, and a
decision to either decrease or increase the voltage by Vstep is made.
33
==PAGE:56==
2. Theoretical background
Figure 2.20: Flowchart of basic perturb and observe algorithm [13].
Incremental conductance
Like the P&O method, the IC method is also a commonly used MPPT method [13]. Similar
to the P&O the IC only requires a measured voltage and current from the PV to work.
This method compares the incremental conductance to the instantaneous conductance
in a given PV system. Depending on the result of the comparison the voltage either
increases or decreases until the maximum power point (MPP) is reached. From Figure 2.21
it is observed that when the incremental voltage and current is zero, ∆I
∆V = 0, then the
maximum power point has been reached. If the sum of the instantaneous conductance I
V
and the incremental conductance ∆I
∆V is zero, the output voltage is the MPP voltage. If
these two conditions are not met then based on comparisons between either ∆I > 0 or
I + ∆I
∆V V > 0 the Vref is increased or decreased by Vstep.
34
==PAGE:57==
2. Theoretical background
Figure 2.21: Flowchart of basic incremental conductance algorithm [13].
35
==PAGE:58==
