DEVELOPMENT OF SIMULINK MODELS
This chapter will focus on the development of the Simulink models of the BESS, PV system
and inverter and their respective control systems.
Figure 3.1 shows the components that will be modelled in order to evaluate the BESS
performance in diﬀerent operating scenarios. Such as direct coupling of PV or in traditional
grid operation.
Figure 3.1: Overview of modelled system components.
There are 5 diﬀerent models in this chapter followed by a summary section:
1. The cell model (3.1).
2. The BESS model (3.2).
3. The inverter (3.3).
4. The PV system (3.5).
5. The thermal system (3.4).
6. Summary of the above models (3.6).
This project will focus on modelling a single dual-string based on the BESS introduced
in section 1.3 on page 5. Each sub-string consists of 6 modules with 27 cells each. This
sums up to a total of 324 cells, which can provide a total capacity of around 104 kWh and
a maximum voltage of approximately 1040 V. The BESS model is built from the ground
up starting on cell level, which means the LFP cells, have been modelled and validated
individually to characterize the performance and the dynamic response. The cell has then
been scaled up to represent the whole battery at system level.
The inverter model is based on a static characterization of the inverter that is used together
with the BESS. The characterization includes losses as a function of power and an RMS
representation.
36
==PAGE:59==
3. Development of Simulink Models
The PV system model will represent the PV system that is installed at Bornholm. This
means that there will be 10 strings in parallel with 23 PV modules in series per string. The
PV panel is included in appendix E.1. The goal has been to develop a PV system model
and a decision-making algorithm. The algorithm makes it possible for the BESS to provide
MPPT functionality, such that it automatically engages and bypasses the individual battery
cells to maximize the power output of the PV system.
Finally, the thermal dynamics will be investigated by developing two diﬀerent thermal
models. The ﬁrst model considers a module as a single thermal mass. The second model
considers a single cell as thermal mass, which then will be scaled up to represent a complete
module consisting of 27 cells.
To create the diﬀerent models, MATLAB & Simulink® will be used. Simulink is a software
program used to model, simulate and analyze diﬀerent systems. Primarily, Simulink is a
graphical block diagram tool and consists of various custom-sets of block libraries. Simulink
works close together with MATLAB as well. MATLAB is a programming language and
a numeric computing environment developed by MathWorks. It allows for easy matrix
manipulation, creation of algorithms and plotting of functions and data [51].
3.1
Battery cell model
This section provides a detailed description of the process of modelling a single lithium-ion
cell. This includes the following:
• Description of the assumptions that have been made.
• Model overview
• Description of the experimental data needed to start designing an EECM model.
• Description of how the cell model has been implemented in Simulink.
• Characterization of of the cell model parameters.
• Discussion of the ﬁnal estimated parameters.
3.1.1
Assumptions
Included eﬀects
Based on the described eﬀects in section 2.1.5 it has been decided to include the following
when modelling the single cell:
• SOC inﬂuence on cell behaviour.
• Hysteresis eﬀect.
• OCV-SOC dependency.
• OCV-Temperature dependency.
• Load current: Dynamic responds to current.
• Cell design: Anode layering.
37
==PAGE:60==
3. Development of Simulink Models
Neglected eﬀects
Cell design Since the cell under investigation is of the box type, the inductance will have
a minor impact compared to the capacitive eﬀects. Therefore, only the capacitive eﬀects
will be represented as part of the capacitances included in the RC branches.
State of Health The inﬂuence of ageing will be neglected and there are several reasons
for this assumption. First, all cells implemented in the battery are new. Secondly, there is
a lack of data. Thirdly, LFP cells have a long lifetime due to slow aging. The inﬂuence of
ageing on state of health would therefore have to be based on theoretical estimations.
Self-discharge & Leakage Since this thesis investigates new cells over a short period with
protection schemes to ensure the cells stay above the minimum voltage, the self-discharge
will be neglected. Likewise, a new cell will have a small initial leakage, which can be
neglected in the bigger picture of operating the battery system.
The second reason for neglecting these eﬀects is the diﬃculty in which they are observed
and modelled. Both are happening within the cell and can therefore not be measured
directly and diﬀerentiated from the modelled parameters. The best way to observe these
phenomena is indirectly through the diﬀerence between charged and discharged power.
Finally as mentioned in [37] when working with lithium electrochemistry, the parasitic
eﬀects are often neglected due to high coulombic eﬃciency and low self-discharge.
Load Current Based on the description of the dependency between the parameters and
the applied load current in section 2.1.5, it is decided that the parameters in the cell model
will not be current dependent based on the assumption that the temperature of the BESS
will be kept at approximately 25 °C.
Moreover, since the cells will not experience high current rates during normal daily
operation, there should not be a signiﬁcant impact on accuracy due to high currents.
Secondly, the possible deviation during high current rates can be a result of internal heating
of the cell during testing. While the cells will be in a controlled temperature chamber
during the discharge experiment, a local internal heating is unavoidable.
Memory eﬀect As mentioned in section 2.1.5 the memory eﬀect typically occurs in
nickel-cadmium and nickel-metal hydride rechargeable batteries and therefore the memory
eﬀect will not be included in this model.
Voltage depression This phenomenon is generally present in consumer electronics, which
are often trickle charged over nominal capacity. This will not be present in the reconﬁgurable
battery system as the included BMS will prevent overcharging by limiting the allowed
voltage levels. Thus, voltage depressions will not be considered.
Deep discharge and overcharge The BMS should prevent any deep discharge or
overcharging. This is achieved by limiting the operation range of the cell to be within
speciﬁcations. The second reason is the new cells is usually oversized as a way to guarantee
nominal operation throughout the cell’s lifetime. This phenomenon will therefore not be
investigated in this thesis.
38
==PAGE:61==
3. Development of Simulink Models
3.1.2
Model overview
An overview of a complete cell model to be simulated in Simulink is shown in Figure 3.2.
The model consist of the electrical equivalent circuit model (EECM), SOC calculation,
hysteresis model and a lookup-table circuit containing the parameters for the EECM model
found in section 3.1.3.
Figure 3.2: Draft of cell model in Simulink.
3.1.3
Experimental cell characterization
Figure 3.3: Experimental setup during the OCV and pulse test used to characterize the
cells.
In order to characterize the parameters of a cell model, diﬀerent kinds of experiments are
needed. According to [5], two experiments are suﬃcient for characterizing the parameters.
Both experiment setups are identical, placing the cell under test in a thermal chamber to
keep a ﬁxed temperature. A current source is used to feed a current through the cell. As
output of the test, the cell terminal voltage is measured with high accuracy using sensor
wires. The setup is shown in Figure 3.3. Both experiments has been performed by NSS at
5 °C, 25 °C and 45 °C.
39
==PAGE:62==
3. Development of Simulink Models
Experiment to determine OCV
Regarding the experiment to determine the OCV vs. SOC relationship, the initial state
is to have the cell fully charged. Then, the cells are slowly discharged to the minimum
operating voltage (0% SOC). Thereafter, the cell is very slowly charged to the maximum
operating voltage (100%) while monitoring the cell voltage and accumulated ampere-hours
charged. A c-rate of c/30 is often used to minimize the inﬂuence of other phenomena, such
as voltage drop over internal resistance. Ideally, the cell should rest at each SOC to gather
the correct OCV-SOC relationship. This is however not feasible in practice as the cell
often needs 2 hours to stabilise. A continues c/30 current is commonly accepted as an
alternative, as the low current result in neglectable disturbances from other sources.
Figure 3.4: Experimental OCV for test temperatures: 5 °C, 25 °C and 45 °C. For both
charging and discharging.
The data collected from cycling the cell is then used to create the OCV vs. SOC plot as
seen in Figure 3.4. The diﬀerence between charging and discharging curves at the same
temperature can be deﬁned as the hysteresis eﬀect mentioned in section 2.1.5. This is
common for LFP cells which OCV tends to be aﬀected a lot by the hysteresis eﬀect [52].
In addition it should be mentioned that since the current rate used for the experiment is
so low, the heat-generation in the cell is negligible and all the collected data points are
therefore assumed to be at the test temperature.
The test temperature itself is however inﬂuential. As seen in in ﬁgure 3.4 the lower
temperature increases the voltage faster during charging at the high end of the SOC, while
decreasing the voltage at lower SOC during discharging. This indicates that the cell voltage
is limited during lower temperatures and prevent the cell from using the complete nominal
capacity range. This change is however not linear and the OCV at 25 °C and 45 °C is
identical within margin of error. The colder temperature is therefore a limitation to be
considered during battery operation.
40
==PAGE:63==
3. Development of Simulink Models
Experiment to determine dynamic characteristics
Regarding the experiment for determining the series resistance and the RC-parameters
for the EECM model, a current interrupt/pulse test must be performed. The experiment
should be initiated whenever the cell is either fully charged or discharged. The temperature
should be kept constant through the test. Once the cell is at either 100 % or 0 % SOC it
will be exposed to a current pulse, usually equal to 10 % of the cell capacity. With 100 Ah
nominal capacity rating, the current pulse is 100 A for 6 minutes, followed by a resting
period of 1.5 hours. By monitoring the voltage, each pulse will provide information for the
parameter estimation with breakpoint intervals of 10 % SOC. The results from the current
interrupt experiment at 25 °C discharge is seen in Figure 3.5. From this it is observed that
there was a total of 12 current pulses. The reason for the 12 pulses instead of the expected
10 is because the cells are new and therefore can have more charge than the guaranteed
100 Ah rating. This is also seen in the previous experiment,Figure 2.5, where the total
charged capacity in the cells was higher than 100 Ah.
Figure 3.5: Current Interrupt experiment performed as discharge at 25℃. Using the
current as input to the cell and voltage is the measured output.
Selecting the optimal number of RC branches
As mentioned in section 2.1.4, the RC branches are describing the dynamic behaviour
of the cell. The more RC branches that are included in the model the more accurate
it becomes, but at the cost of being more complex. In this project the plan is to use
Simulink® to build the model and the build-in optimizer to later estimate the parameters.
According to [37], LFP cells exhibit complex dynamics. Therefore, having a too simple
model could mean that the ﬁnal results will not be accurate enough, or a too complex a
model could cause the optimizer to ﬁnd a local minima and thereby get stuck. Thus, to
determine the optimal number of RC branches, the data from the pulse tests is examined
during the relaxation phase. As the pulse current stops, the transient response of the cell
41
==PAGE:64==
3. Development of Simulink Models
is directly related to the series resistance and the RC branches. A pulse at around 50%
SOC has been chosen, as seen in Figure 3.6. This ﬁgure shows the relaxation period after
the ﬁfth pulse, as the voltage converges towards a new steady state.
Three exponential equations with one, two, and three terms is then ﬁtted to the data. The
amount of terms is directly related to the number of RC branches.
When looking at the faster part of the transient, it clearly shows that by increasing the
number of terms in the exponential ﬁt, that the overall ﬁt becomes better but at the risk of
overﬁtting. It is therefore determined that one RC branch will not produce a satisfactory
match to the data, while three RC branches has the lowest residual error, two RC branches
is chosen since it is the best compromise between complexity and accuracy.
Figure 3.6: Zoom on the relaxation period after the ﬁfth pulse in the discharge pulse
experiment. The data is plotted with exponential ﬁts corresponding to 1, 2, and 3 RC
branches.
3.1.4
Matlab modelling
The following section will describe the subsystems from model overview (Figure 3.2) in
detail and how it is implemented into Matlab.
The model was built with a voltage source, a series resistance and two RC branches based
on result from section 3.1.3. Moreover, a hysteresis has been developed to consider the
large diﬀerence between the rest cell OCV which depends on whether the cell has been
charged or discharged beforehand without any resting time. This model is created such
that each circuit element contains lookup tables to make it easier to estimate values while
also increasing the computational eﬃciency.
EECM
In Figure 3.7a the EECM is shown. As mentioned before the electrical circuit consists of 5
components; the Voc voltage source and two RC branches connected in series with the series

resistance R0. The parameter values are determined as a function of SOC, temperature
and whether the cell is charging or discharging. See Figure 3.9 on parameter lookup tables.

(b) EECM in Simulink.

==PAGE:66==
3. Development of Simulink Models
SOC estimation - Coulomb counting
Figure 3.8: Storage circuit "Coulomb counting" implementation in Simulink.
Coulomb counting was chosen for the SOC estimation. The coulomb counting method
measures the discharging current of the cell and then integrate the current over time in
order to determine the SOC.
When using the Coulomb counting method there are several factors, such as battery history,
cycle life etc., which are not taken into consideration[53]. This aﬀects the accuracy of the
SOC estimation, especially if it is implemented in systems that are tasked to estimate the
SOC over a long period of time.
The Coulomb counting method is chosen for three reasons. Firstly, the cells are new and
therefore far above ratings which means that battery life and history are not as relevant yet.
Secondly, this method is simple and takes little computational power. Thirdly, Coulomb
counting loses accuracy over time, as the error is accumulating. But since the simulation
runs within short durations, this error should not be signiﬁcant. This method can be used
in a real system over long time, if re-calibrations is performed regularly.
Figure 3.8 shows the subsystem containing the storage system, using the coulomb counting
equation 2.3.
Since the simulation is running in seconds, the capacity rating is multiplied with 3600
to convert ampere-hours to ampere-seconds. The signing convention is designed such
that negative current means current ﬂowing IN (cell is charging) and when the current is
positive the current is ﬂowing OUT of the cell (cell is discharging).
Next is the deﬁnition of the SOC range.
It can be deﬁned either by rated capacity
[SOC = ζreleasable/ζrated] or maximum releasable charge [SOC = ζreleasable/ζMax].
Following is a discussion of reasons for picking one or the other. Note that SOC and SOH
is interconnected and deﬁning one limits the deﬁnition of the other.
SOC based on ζrated
Using ζrated to calculate the SOC is easy since the rated capacity remains the same for all
cells, especially for the 100Ah cells since SOC = 1% = 1Ah. By doing this, the dependency
on the cell condition is removed and there is therefore no need for updating the operating
SOC if weaker cells are connected.
Finally it provides a better accuracy compared to modifying the SOC to whichever cell is
the weakest. A disadvantage is the possibility that new cells is able to have a SOC above
100%. This is due to the cells being designed with a margin (due to warranty) in order to
ensure that the cell will have the rated capacity when the guarantee expires. In the end
this means that this method of calculating the SOC leaves capacity unused.
44
==PAGE:67==
3. Development of Simulink Models
Furthermore, the degradation can be a problem. It is important for the BMS to monitor
and operate cells using the voltage limits since at some point the cells can no longer reach
100% SOC.
SOC based on ζMax
The second method is to deﬁne the SOC to always be between 0 % −100 % SOC. This
method makes it easier to provide a general idea of the current available capacity and is
more intuitive due to the general idea of 100 % meaning the cell is unable to receive any
more charges. Furthermore, it makes it easier to compare all cells across the diﬀerent cell
types, manufactures, and capacity since they are all normalized. However, since all cells
have diﬀerent total capacities, 100 % SOC means diﬀerent actual capacity from each cell.
Usually, the BMS balance the battery based on the weakest cell which means that the
weakest cell is used very eﬃciently, while the stronger cell will not be fully utilized. In the
end this make the diﬀerence between the strong and weak cell even greater.
If the cell which has been used to characterise the cell model, proves to be a weak cell. Then
as the cell model is scaled up to represent the entire battery on system level, the results of
the Simulink model could end up underpredicting the battery performance compared to
the actual system.
Reason for choosing ζrated over ζMax
A traditional system with the rigid cell structure would be limited by the weakest cell.
Hence, by choosing to calculate the SOC based on the maximum releasable charge would
provide a better comparison with other systems. The battery system from NSS with its
reconﬁgurable design can gain more advantages of using the rated capacity to calculate
the SOC, since it can bypass depleted cells. Having the rating system means the SOC
correspond directly to the current available charge in each individual cell allowing the
system to better utilize the healthier and weaker cells at the same time. The static
deﬁnition also decreases the need for constant updates of the SOC due to degradation or if
a new weak cell is added.
Lookup tables
Figure 3.9: Subsystem with lookup tables from Simulink model.
From Figure 3.9 the subsystem containing all the lookup tables for the EECM model
parameters can be seen. As shown, the lookup tables are 4 dimensional, taking 3 inputs;
45
==PAGE:68==
3. Development of Simulink Models
the SOC, temperature and whether the cell is discharging [κhyst = 1] or charging [κhyst = 0]
to determine the actual parameter for the corresponding circuit element.
The lookup-table takes the three inputs and then return the value found in the 3d-matrix.
If the inputs do not have a corresponding output, the lookup-table will do a linear
interpolation.
When creating the look-up tables, it is important to keep in mind the amount of ”Knee-
points”/Breakpoints (SOC levels) to include in the lookup table. More points in the
lookup table allows the parameters to be captured at a higher resolution. However, this
leads to an increased computational time during the parameter optimization. Each point
corresponds to a variable which the solver must approximate. As the look-up tables
uses linear approximation between the data points, the necessary number of observations
included needs to be taken into consideration.
Regarding the parameters for the cell, it would be preferable to create few breakpoints
between 30%-80% SOC, as this region tends to be linear by itself for lithium-ion cells.
Then increase the number of breakpoints during the higher and lower levels of SOC where
the voltage becomes highly nonlinear.
For this model, the SOC is chosen with 10 % intervals from 0 % to 110 %. The 10 % was
chosen due to the pulsing experiments that was performed in the laboratories of Nerve
Smart Systems ApS. They have chosen to perform the test with 6 minutes 100 A discharge
pulses which corresponds to 10 % SOC per pulse. The reason for choosing 110 % SOC as
the maximum level of SOC, is due to the new cells that tends to allow more than 100 %
SOC = 100 Ah charge.
Regarding the temperature, three breakpoints have been chosen; 5 °C, 25 °C and 45 °C.
3.1.5
Modelling the OCV vs. SOC hysteresis eﬀect
Regarding the OCV vs. SOC relationship notice how the charging and discharging curve
is slightly oﬀset due to the hysteresis eﬀect discussed in section 2.1.5. There are multiple
ways to model this behaviour, and three of these methods are:
• 1. Method: Approximation of the charge and discharge curve described in [5].
• 2. Method: Determine OCV based on discharge experiments with diﬀerent c-rates.
• 3. Method: Determine OCV based on a ratio constant which is a function of change
in SOC [5].
1. Method: Approximation of the OCV
This method is based on the discharge and charging curve which can be seen in Figure 3.4.
From this ﬁgure it is assumed that the OCV can be approximated to be the average between
the discharge and the charge curve. From Figure 3.10 it is shown how the approximation
have been made. The approximation is not made as a complete average between the
discharge and the charge curve, namely because there are no discharge voltages at the low
SOC due to the test encountering the cut oﬀvoltage Vmin before 0 % SOC was reached.
Similar at a high SOC the charging test encountered the maximum cut oﬀvoltage Vmax
before 100 % SOC, which forced the approximation to be based on the discharge curve.
46
==PAGE:69==
3. Development of Simulink Models
Figure 3.10: Approximation of the OCV vs. SOC based on the discharge and charging
curve.
The approximation is based on the following equation:
Voc = (1 −SOC/100) · Voc,charge + (SOC/100) · Voc,discharge
(3.4)
The advantage of this method is its simplicity due to an approximation of the voltage
which is arranged in one single lookup table.
2. Method: Determining the OCV from diﬀerent c-rate discharge
experiments
This method is done by performing a series of constant discharge experiments at diﬀerent
c-rates. The results of these experiments is shown as cell voltage vs. SOC in Figure 3.11.
Originally the measurements were taken in voltage vs. capacity discharged, but was later
converted to voltage vs. SOC where the SOC is normalized to be between 0% and 100%.
For each of the experiments it is assumed that the diﬀerence in the measured voltage is
caused by a total resistance in the cell and the c-rate. Therefore, for each combination of
two of the voltages, a resistance can be calculated using the following equation:
V = OCV −I · R
(3.5)
This equation is then used to calculate the resistance that determines the diﬀerence in
voltage at two diﬀerent c-rates. By looking at the plot with c/3 and c/5, the following
expression is created.
Vc/3 −Vc/5 = OCV −Ic/3 · R −OCV −Ic/5 · R
(3.6)
47
==PAGE:70==
3. Development of Simulink Models
Figure 3.11: Results from diﬀerent c-rates during discharge. The following current rates
have been used: 3c, 2c, 1c c/2 and c/3.
It is assumed that the OCV is the same for all c-rates which gives:
Vc/3 −Vc/5 = (Ic/5 −Ic/3) · R
(3.7)
Then the resistance is calculated as follows:
R = Vc/3 −Vc/5
Ic/5 −Ic/3
(3.8)
This is done for all the experiments which then provides a ﬁgure showing all the diﬀerent
resistances related to the diﬀerent c-rates. The ﬁgure is seen in Figure 3.12.
48
==PAGE:71==
3. Development of Simulink Models
Figure 3.12: Estimated resistance values for diﬀerent c-rates.
From Figure 3.12 it is observed that there are three resistances close to each other, which
include 100, 50 and 33 A. An average between these three resistances are then taken, and
a model is then ﬁtted on the average curve in order to determine the total resistance of
the cell for each level of SOC.
Finally, the ﬁtted resistance model can be used to provide an estimation for the OCV,
which is shown in Figure 3.13.
Figure 3.13: Estimated OCV based on the resistance model.
49
==PAGE:72==
3. Development of Simulink Models
3. Method: Determining the OCV by including the hysteresis
The third method is using the same equation 3.4 from the 1 st method and the plots
from Figure 3.4. However, instead of making an approximation of the two curves, an
interpolation between the charge and discharge parameter will be made using a variable
ratio. This ratio will be called the "hysteresis ratio" and is labelled κHyst.
κHyst will be dependent on changes in the SOC and limited to be between 0 and 1 such
that it represents the ratio between the discharge and charge OCV curves.
A κHyst = 0 implies that the discharge curve is fully utilised, while the charge curve is
fully used when κHyst = 1.
By doing this, the history of the SOC change can be modelled by switching dynamically
between the two OCV curves as seen in ﬁgure Figure 3.10.
One way to describe this mathematically is shown in Equation 3.9, where κHyst is the
weighting of the two curves. By converting the current to charge, it allows κHyst to control
the speed at which the hysteresis comes into eﬀect.
Voc = κHyst · Voc,charge + (1 −κHyst) · Voc,discharge
(3.9)
The chosen method
Based on the discussed literature that mentions that the hysteresis eﬀect is especially
important when modelling LFP cells. The 3rd method is chosen since it considers the
dynamic behaviour and the hysteresis eﬀect.
Experiment to determine hysteresis ratio constant
To determine the change in ratio as a function of SOC, a dedicated experiment is performed.
This experiment is a modiﬁcation of the original OCV-experiment described in 3.1.3.
Normally when determining the OCV, the cell is fully discharged and charged to capture
the overall OCV of the cell. Regarding the hysteresis eﬀect, the OCV test should be
repeated in steps of 5 % less charge and discharge, meaning the cell is cycled between 5 %
and 95 % SOC, then 10 % and 90 % SOC etc. This method is however very time consuming,
as the OCV runs at c/30 current.
Instead, a modiﬁed version of the OCV characterization experiment is going to be applied
to the cell. The designed load proﬁle is shown in Figure 3.14. The experiment is performed
such that the initial SOC of the single cell corresponds to SOCinit,cell = 0 %. The cell is then
charged at c/30 until the SOC corresponds to SOCcell = 55 % and then discharged until
the SOC corresponds to SOCcell = 45 %. The cell is then fully charged to SOCcell = 100 %.
Next the cell is discharged to 45 % SOC and then charged back to 55 % SOC and ﬁnally
fully discharged to SOCcell = 0 %.
50
==PAGE:73==
3. Development of Simulink Models
Figure 3.14: Load proﬁle for determining the hysteresis ratio constant κHyst.

(a) Measured voltage.
(b) New OCV-SOC vs. OCV-SOC at 25℃.
Figure 3.15: Results from extended OCV experiment to determine the hysteresis ratio
constant.

the measured voltage against the calculated SOC (based on Coulomb counting).
Most notably from the OCV-SOC curve in Figure 3.15a is the beginning of the discharge
which is directly coupled to the end of the charging curve at 100% SOC. The reason behind
this is the lacking 2 hours rest period. This creates a slight shift (to the left) for the

discharge curve. However, this does not inﬂuence the exponential ﬁt of κHyst, as the ﬁtting
is performed on the change in SOC and not on SOC itself.
First step in ﬁtting the hysteresis response, is to cut out the relevant data. The cut begins
the moment when the current is reversed and stops when the current is changed back to
the original direction. Since only one cycle is performed around 50 % ± 5 SOC two sets of
data points are created, One for charging and one for discharging. The ﬁrst cut of data is
marked red in ﬁgure 3.16a, while the second set is marked orange. Figure 3.16a, shows the
red circle zoom on the data from ﬁgure 3.15b.
Now the data must be ﬁtted between 0 and 1, with 0 indicating that the discharge OCV
curve is fully utilised and 1 indicating that the charging OCV curve is fully used, where the
discharge and charge OCV curve is marked as purple and green respectively in Figure 3.16b.
The ﬁt is achieved by ﬁrst subtracting the discharge OCV curve from the cut data (Figure
3.16c) such that the cut data converges towards zero. The data cut (Red) is then divided by
the charging curve OCV (Green in ﬁgure 3.16c) resulting in Figure 3.16d. It is important
to divide the data point with the SOC corresponding OCV curve. Note that the 3.5A
assumption compensation discussed in section 3.1.7 is applied between ﬁgure 3.16a and
3.16c.
This procedure is repeated for the reversed current during discharge (cut2). The cut2 is
marked as orange in ﬁgure 3.16a and the resulting plot after the data preparation is shown
in ﬁgure 3.16f.
Finally plotting these data points as a function of change in SOC, ∆SOC, the resulting
plots in ﬁgure 3.16e can be made.
Figure 3.17: Results of the hysteresis ratio constant, κHyst, as a function of ∆SOC. From
charge to discharge (left), from discharge to charge (right)
The red line in ﬁgure 3.17 is a second order exponential ﬁt of equation 3.10. By default,
53
==PAGE:76==
3. Development of Simulink Models
the exponential starts at 1 and approaches 0. For charging to go the opposite way, the
exponential is subtracted from 1. The variables from the two exponential ﬁts are shown in
table 3.1. As observed in the variables B/C and the ﬁt in ﬁgure 3.17, there is almost no
diﬀerence between going from charging to discharging or the vice versa. In other words,
the rate of change remains the same around 50 % no matter the direction.
Fκ = A · (expB·∆SOC + expC·∆SOC)
(3.10)

The last variable A is the starting point of the exponential and has so far been 1. This
is not always the case, as the current might switch direction faster than κHyst can reach
0. Therefore, during a switch in the current direction, the constant A is set to the last
recorded value of the hysteresis ratio constant; "A = κHyst". Similarly the SOC change is
set to zero; ∆SOC = 0.
3.1.6
Manual and automatic characterization of cell parameters
For determining the parameters R0, R1, R2, C1 and C2 for the EECM model, the results
from the current interrupt experiment is used to calculate the theoretical values by hand
and later using the Simulink built-in "Parameter Optimizer".
Theoretical estimation of the cell parameters
Figure 3.18: Method to estimate parameter values for R0, R1, R2, C1 & C2 [14, 15].
54
==PAGE:77==
3. Development of Simulink Models
First the EECM parameters is estimated by manually considering each individual pulse in
the pulse experiment described in section 3.1.3.
Figure 3.18 shows how to determine the diﬀerent parameters describing the instantaneous
response, charge transfer and the diﬀusion voltages.
The instantaneous response is determined by the series resistance R0, which can be
calculated by using the voltage V0 and discharge c-rate of 100 A:
V0 = I · R0 →R0 = V0
I
(3.11)
The charge transfer is determined by the ﬁrst RC branch while the diﬀusion voltages
are described by the second RC branch. The resistances of the 2 RC branches is calculated
similarly:
RRC,1 = V1
I
&
RRC,2 = V2
I
(3.12)
The time constants for the ﬁrst and the second RC branch are determined by measuring
the time period for the charge transfer process and the diﬀusion process respectively, as
seen on Figure 3.18. The capacitance is then calculated from the time constants and the
calculated resistance:
CRC,1 = τRC,1
RRC,1
&
CRC,2 = τRC,2
RRC,2
(3.13)
The resistances and capacitance is determined for every pulse. The steady state after each
pulse is associated with a speciﬁc SOC. The SOC breakpoint for the estimated parameter
values is rounded to the nearest tenth.

Automatic estimation of parameters
An alternative way of estimating the EECM parameters is by using an optimizer to
automatically ﬁt the parameters. In this case the built-in optimizer in Simulink called
"Parameter Estimator" is chosen. This estimator injects an input into the attached Simulink
model and compares the simulated output with the measured output. The Parameter
Estimator then adjust the parameters to improve the accuracy between the simulation
output and measured output.
The EECM model described in section 3.1.4 is used together with a current source between
the terminals, thereby replicating the "Pulse-experiment" setup as seen in ﬁgure 3.3.
55
==PAGE:78==
3. Development of Simulink Models
The current interrupt experiment described in section 3.1.3 is used, since the goal of
the optimizer is to estimate the parameters describing the dynamic cell behaviour. The
experimental current is used as input to the current source, then selected as input in
Parameter Optimizer. As for the output in the Parameter Optimizer, the simulated cell
voltage is chosen. The output is then compared to the measured cell voltage. Finally the
Parameter Optimizer needs to know which parameters it is allowed to adjust and their
initial values.
The "nonlinear least-square" error method is chosen as the solver-conﬁguration for the
optimizer.
Figure 3.19 shows the results for R0, R1, R2, C1 and C2 from the Parameter Optimizer. The
bottom plot shows the experimental current which has been fed into the simulated current
source. The top plot shows the simulated cell voltage (blue) against the experimental
measured cell voltage (orange). The average error in the voltage during the complete
Parker proﬁle is 20.01 mV.
Figure 3.19: Optimizer results with calculated initial guesses and no control of voltage -
discharge pulse test.
The most important decisions to take when setting up the optimizer is determining which
variables the Parameter Optimizer is allowed to adjust and their initial state.
Starting with picking the right amount of parameters to optimize.
All resistors and
capacitors in the EECM circuit is both temperature dependent and SOC dependent. This
is taken care of by giving the Parameter Optimizer a matrix instead of a constant, which
contains a column for each temperature breakpoint and rows for each SOC breakpoint at
which the Parameter Optimizer should ﬁnd the parameters. The total values the Parameter
Optimizer can adjust is 3 · 11 · 5 = 165. Unfortunately, each added variable will result in
signiﬁcantly longer simulation times.
It is therefore decided to run the optimizer for the pulse experiment at one individual
temperature at a time. This resulted in 5 · 11 = 55 parameters that the optimizer would
56
==PAGE:79==
3. Development of Simulink Models
have to estimate at the same time.
This is repeated for both the discharge and charge pulse experiment at each temperature
and all the parameters are then collected into their respective look-up table.
Discussion of optimizer results
While the manually estimated values seen in Table 3.2 gave acceptable results, it was clear
that the Parameter Optimizer did provide much better results. However, in order to assist
the Parameter Optimizer, the manually determined parameters was used as the initial
guesses for the optimizer. This provided better results by ensuring that the optimizer did
not ran into any local optimum. Furthermore, the Parameter Optimizer was supported
even further by placing limits on each parameter. The parameters were limited to positive
values, removing any unrealistic values that the optimizer could have found.
The Parameter Optimizer only had access to the pulse measurements and was only allowed
to adjust the values for the resistances and capacitances. This resulted in a steady state
oﬀset of ±15 mV as the optimizer wasn’t allowed to adjust the OCV.
The oﬀset is due to the OCV-SOC curve not matching the pulse resting voltage. 5 mV of
the oﬀset is due to the c/30 assumption during the OCV-SOC experiment. The remaining
oﬀset is either due to a combination of measurement errors or the cell heating up. The
latter is more likely, as sending 100 A through the cell will generate heat and thereby aﬀect
the cell parameters.
Since the high current is needed for the dynamic responds and the slow heat dissipation of
the cell, it is impossible to completely avoid the heat. An alternative solution was explored,
where the Parameter Optimizer was allowed to modify the OCV-SOC curve during the
estimation in order to compensate for the oﬀset. However, this comes at the high risk
of overﬁtting as it forces parameters to ﬁt this exact experiment. While allowing the
Parameter Optimizer to change OCV-SOC resulted in almost perfect ﬁts, the parameter
performed poorly when it was exposed to new experimental data.
In the end, the results from when the optimizer only was allowed to adjust the resistances
and capacitances was used. In addition, the ﬁnal parameters was found on one individual
cell, which comes at a high risk of overﬁtting the parameters. Since the battery model is
going to be based on this single cell model, the most unbiased method should provide a
better estimation of the cell performance.
As this is the only cell to be modelled the simulated battery performance could be
underpredicted compared to the actual system, if the modelled cell is weaker than the
average cell in the battery. The opposite could also be true. The ideal scenario would
therefore be to have more experimental data for the Parameter Optimizer.
3.1.7
Discussion of the OCV-SOC c/30 current assumption
Earlier in this project, it was assumed that the OCV could be determined using a low
c/30A current (See section 3.1.3) as the low current would not inﬂuence the result. This
assumption is now reconsidered since the pulsing experiment has shown that the steady
state voltage resulted in an oﬀset compared to the experimental OCV-SOC data. A part
of this oﬀset can be explained by the voltage drop due to the small current that was still
57
==PAGE:80==
3. Development of Simulink Models
present in the OCV experiment. Every pulse in the dynamic experiment was followed by a
long rest period which should theoretically converge to the OCV of the given SOC. The
settled voltage was however oﬀset with approximately 12 mV at each SOC level between
10-90% during both charge and discharge.
This oﬀset could be a result of the accuracy at which the SOC was estimated and how
the OCV-SOC curve was extrapolated. But a much more likely explanation is due to the
c/30 current, which for a 100 Ah battery means 3.33 A. However, in practice the c-rate
was 3.5 A due to current source limitations.
The oﬀset observed during discharge is opposite compared to the charge experiment,
indicating the current direction is inﬂuencing the result.
Assuming the optimized cell parameters to be true, the total cell resistance varied between
RDis = 1.2 ± 0.6 mΩand RCha = 1.6 ± 1 mΩfor discharging and charging respectively.
This results in a average measured voltage oﬀset in the OCV test as seen in equation 3.14.
Vc/30,avg = mean( ⃗
RDis) · Ic/30 ≈1.2mW · 3.5A = 4.2 mV
(3.14)
As the resistance of a cell is a function of SOC, so will the voltage oﬀset be a function
of SOC. While the internal voltage drop is in the order of mV, so is the oﬀset, which is
conﬁrmed from the pulse experiment. This means that the c/30 assumption is not true.
The voltage oﬀset due to the c/30 assumption is calculated at each SOC for discharge
and summarized in table 3.3. This improved the steady state of the dynamic parameter
estimation and the compensation is incorporated into the cell OCV-SOC curve.

==PAGE:81==
3. Development of Simulink Models
Figure 3.21: Final EECM cell Simulink model.
Analysis of ﬁnal cell parameters
Starting with the resistance, it is observed from Figure 3.22a how
the general size decreases for each RC branch that is added, with R0 being the largest.
This is to be expected, as the ﬁrst branch covers the initial voltage drop during a change
in the load, while the RC branches are used to explain the charge transfer and diﬀusion
voltages.
Furthermore, it is observed that the resistances are increasing at the lower and higher
end of SOC, while the middle range is somewhat constant. For R1 and some of R0, the
resistance during charging increases at higher SOC. This is likely a result of the cell’s
chemistry. When it is near full capacity, a limited amount of free charges ﬂoats within
the cell while the anodes have a high concentration of charge. The high concentration
creates an opposing force making it more diﬃcult to get the ﬁnal charges back resulting in
this higher resistance. An exception to this increase in resistance is at 5 °C, as it peaks
earlier and then decreases in resistance. This indicates that the temperature does have
some inﬂuence.
Similarly, lower SOC during discharge have a slight increase in resistance. However, the
accuracy of some of the parameters at 10% and 20% SOC is questionable, as the optimizer
at the lower range were quite bad. The poor ﬁt is a result of the OCV having slightly
higher values throughout the experiment.
While the temperature steps are identical, it is observed that the resistance increase from
45 % SOC →25 % SOC is smaller compared to the resistance increase from 25 % SOC →
5 % SOC. This is most visible for R0 in ﬁgure 3.22a. Based on literature, the temperature
dependency should mimic an exponential decay [5], ﬂattening around 15 °C and higher
temperatures.
59
==PAGE:82==
3. Development of Simulink Models
(a) Final estimated resistances; R0, R1 and R2.
(b) Final estimated capacitance; C1 and C2.
Figure 3.22: Final ﬁtted parameters for the EECM cell model at 5 °C, 25 °C and 45 °C.
This behaviour is observed in the resistance; however, it is not possible to conﬁrm based
on only three diﬀerent temperature measurements. Exponential interpolation between
the parameters at diﬀerent temperatures is therefore simply not possible due to the low
amount of available points.
The R0 remains almost constant across each SOC for each individual temperature (except
for discharging at low soc/temperatures).
60
==PAGE:83==
3. Development of Simulink Models
Regarding the capacitance, it seems to be semi random and diﬃcult to interpret. Therefore,
it can be an advantage to look at the time constants τ = R · C, as it describes the speed
at which the diﬀerent branches approach steady state. The derived time constant is shown
in ﬁgure 3.23.
Figure 3.23: Time constant for RC branch 1 & 2.
As seen in ﬁgure 3.23, the time constant τRC,1 for the ﬁrst RC-branch is a factor 10 smaller
than τRC,2 which follows the statement earlier; that the ﬁrst branch describes the fast
part of the polarization (charge transfer) and the second branch describes the slower part
(diﬀusion voltage), since lower time constants indicate a slower climb. τRC,1 is increasing
with higher SOC (with the exception of discharging at 5 °C). This implies that the dynamic
behaviour speeds up as the battery cell is depleted. The second branch does the opposite,
resulting in a fast response as the cell is nearing 100 % SOC. For the second branch it is
observed that the direction of the current also has an inﬂuence, as the faster response is
even higher at low SOC during charging. The main diﬀerence between the low and high
SOC is the "ease" of access for the charged particles to attach to the anode.
3.2
Battery system model
This section will go through a detailed description of the modelling approach and the
development of the BESS.
This includes the following:
• Assumptions.
• Model overview.
• Component losses.
• Final BESS model.
The section starts by describing the assumptions that have been made followed by an
overview of the battery model on system level and how it has been constructed.
The relevant components losses in the system will then be discussed and estimated.
61
==PAGE:84==
3. Development of Simulink Models
Finally, the BESS model developed in Simulink will be described along with the control
logic for the BMS and the Nerve Switch.
3.2.1
Assumptions
Before describing the proposed BESS design in Simulink, a short discussion of the diﬀerent
assumptions will be given.
One cell ﬁt them all
The BESS model in Simulink will be based on the single cell model developed in section
3.1, but in reality the complete BESS consists of 324 unique cells. However, as discussed
in the previous section, modeling one cell require multiple time-consuming experiments at
diﬀerent controlled temperatures. This means that when validating the BESS model, it is
expected to observe some diﬀerences in the output voltages due to the assumption that
the cell model is the perfect average of all cells in the real BESS.
The parasitic eﬀects
It is known that parasitic eﬀects is unavoidable. The parasitic inductance and capacitance
appear from the magnetic coupling induced by current in the system. This can originate
from the ﬂat surface of the inter-connector plates to the grounded chassis of modules or
the inductance induced from the rapidly change in current during cell switching.
These parasitic eﬀects are ignored due to 3 reasons:
• Firstly, the parasitic eﬀects might be found theoretically based on geometry, but
they will require special equipment for validation.
• Secondly, inserting the parasitic capacitance and inductance would increase
complexity of the model and could increase the total simulation time.
• Thirdly, the parasitic is assumed small with minimal inﬂuence.
Communication speed
The communication and data acquisition speed between the BMS, battery and other
components is assumed to be instant. Some dynamics might be missed and underlying
behaviors could disappear from the simulation model, which is not important to get
a general model of the BESS. When comparing the simulation and real battery this
assumption will be visible in short delays between signal and measurement, which will not
be present in the simulation.
The control speed of the BMS becomes important and inﬂuential during PV simulation,
which is discussed in more detail in chapter 3.5. Since the speed of the control system
will determine how fast the battery can react to changes in the PV panel. The faster the
control is, the faster the battery system will be able to perform the MPPT.
Therefore, for the battery system, the following it is assumed:
• Each cell in the battery will be based on the developed EECM cell model.
62
==PAGE:85==
3. Development of Simulink Models
• Parasitic capacitance and inductance will not be included.
• That there is perfect accuracy on measurements with no time delay.
• The speed of the BMS is as fast possible.
3.2.2
Model overview
Figure 3.24: Overview of the BESS model construction.
The battery model was built in steps as seen in ﬁgure 3.24. Starting with the basic EECM
cell that was modelled in section 3.1. The EECM is combined with the Nerve switch PCB
board to create a "Cell + PCB" model which allows for bypassing and engaging of the
EECM (Cell model).
Taking 27 of these cell models, connecting the terminals in series, the module model is
created. The cells in the module is engaged and disengaged by the "module controller logic"
sometimes referred to as slave controller. In reality the module controller is the one to
send a engage/bypass signal to the Nerve Switch on each individual cell. For simpliﬁcation,
this functionality has been moved to the BESS BMS algorithm.
6 Modules are then connected in series to create a string. This string is then duplicated
such the ﬁnal battery system consist of a dual-string with 6 modules per string. The
strings are typically controlled by a master controller, which is called the BESS BMS.
3.2.3
Component losses
This section evaluates the diﬀerent component losses that must be considered within the
battery system in order to capture the dynamics of the real BESS.
The major losses found is then implemented in the Simulink model.
The included resistances are:
• Connectors - copper plates creating a connection between cells or modules.
• MOSFETs at each Nerve Switch.
• Resistance due to interface between two materials.
• The Nerve Switch - PCB board traces.
• The screws that connect the cell terminals with the Nerve Switch.
63
==PAGE:86==
3. Development of Simulink Models

B. MOSFET
MOSFETs are used as switches to bypass or engage a cell as described in section 2.2.3.
The ideal MOSFET blocks current until it receives a signal. When receiving the signal, the
MOSFET will close and allow current to ﬂow freely without any voltage drop. However,
an "on" resistance is present during conduction which is given as RMOS = 0.58 mΩfor the
used "SiRA20DP" MOSFETs datasheet [55].
Note that if a single MOSFET is exposed to the full current, it would incur more than
50% of losses compared to the total resistance caused by all the connector plates.
The PCB boards in the Nerve battery is equipped with four "SiRA20DP" MOSFETs for
bypassing and four more for engaging as shown in Figure 3.25. The reason for having four
in parallel, is to divide the current and thereby minimize the losses and the generated heat.
When calculating the total resistance of the MOSFETs in a module, it does not matter
whether the cells are engaged or not. In both states, 4 parallel MOSFETs will be active.
The total resistance is therefore calculated as equation 3.19.
RModule
MOS,tot = Nmodule
Cell
· Ron
4
= 27 · 0.58mΩ
4
= 3.375 mΩ
(3.19)

3.2.4
Final BESS model realisation
The BESS model developed in Simulink is shown in Figure 3.27. The BESS model consist
of two submodels, the BESS Battery and the BESS BMS.
BESS Battery - Reconﬁgurable battery system
In order to capture the dynamic performance of the entire battery on system level, each
cell in the battery is based on the developed model of a single cell as described in section
3.1. Each cell can be identiﬁed by its cell number i. The submodel BESS Battery has two
inputs, where the ﬁrst input is the battery current Ibatt, and the second input is a vector
containing the binary control signal ΓBESS
cell,i
∈{0; 1} for every cell. This binary signal
determines whether the individual cells should be engaged ("1") or bypassed ("0"). As
67

3. Development of Simulink Models
Figure 3.27: The BESS model including the physical properties and data connections.
with the single cell model, the sign convention for the current is deﬁned such that negative
values correspond to charging. The voltage of the BESS is given as:
V BESS

The internal monitoring of the the cells is realised by feeding back the information of each
individual cell state from the BESS Battery into the BESS BMS.
BESS BMS
The BESS BMS uses the states of each individual cell to determine the optimal connection
scheme according to the following objectives 1) fulﬁll the voltage/current request sent by
the connected component, and 2) balancing the state of charge of each individual cell. The
control algorithm is based on the previous work [27].
The algorithm works as follows [27]: During each iteration, the BMS will estimate the
predicted battery voltage using:
V BESS
Batt
= Vref + IBatt · RBESS
Batt
(3.32)
The algorithm then sorts the predicted voltages which has been stored in one single vector
in a decreasing order, according to each cell’s respective SOC. Starting from the cells with
68
==PAGE:91==
3. Development of Simulink Models
the highest level of SOC, the appropriate amount of cells is calculated such that the sum
of all the selected cell voltages is the closest to the given reference voltage. The BMS will
then forward a binary signal ("1") to the Nerve Switch to engage the selected cells and
bypass the remaining cells, as shown in Figure 3.28.
This method ensures that the engaged cells always have the highest SOC and thereby an
"active" cell balancing has been achieved across the battery pack while still being able to
fulﬁl the request. Furthermore, the switching events have been reduced by introducing a
deadband to keep the SOC of all cells within a given tolerance.
Figure 3.28: Nerve Switch controller logic implemented in Simulink.
Figure 3.28 shows the logic of the BESS BMS model which determines whether a cell
should be engaged or bypassed based on the binary signal from the BESS BMS as described
in subsubsection 2.2.3.
The logic is realised through an if statement which compares the binary signal ΓBESS
cell,i .

Current or voltage reference
The original control system that was provided was only able to use a voltage reference
signal to control the BESS system. Therefore, a small number of additions has been added,
which makes the control system more ﬂexible. The control system now have two control
modes that determine whether it should control the total voltage output of the BESS in
reference to a voltage setpoint or if it should control the voltage output in order to achieve
a speciﬁc current output based on a current reference (Figure 3.29).
Control based on Voltage Reference
The internal voltage drop is needed to calculate the voltage reference point for the BESS
based on a given voltage setpoint Vset. The internal voltage drop in the BESS can be
predicted from the resistance, described in Equation 3.28 and the measured current.
69
==PAGE:92==
3. Development of Simulink Models
Figure 3.29: Subsystem in BESS BMS model to calculates voltage reference, based on
either a current or a voltage setpoint.
The internal voltage drop is calculated as:
V BESS
internal = Ibatt · RBESS
Batt
(3.33)
The voltage reference for the BESS can then be calculated as:
Vref = Vset + Vinternal
(3.34)
Control based on Current Reference
For the current control mode, it needs a current setpoint, the internal voltage drop in the
BESS, the resistance from the connected load, and the measured load voltage.
The current setpoint can be turned into a voltage setpoint based on the load resistance,
the current setpoint, and the resistance of the link between the BESS and the load. This
is expressed as:
Vset = (Rload + Rlink) · Iset
(3.35)
Finally, the voltage reference for the BESS can be calculated as:
Vref = Vset + V BESS
internal
(3.36)

3. Development of Simulink Models
3.3
Inverter model
This section will provide a brief description of the assumptions made and the development
of the inverter model.
It should be noted that it have been diﬃcult to get some proper information on this inverter,
which means that the proposed inverter model only includes a RMS representation for the
AC side and losses as a function of power at 800 V.
The section will include the following:
• Assumptions
• Model overview
• Characterization of inverter model
3.3.1
Assumptions
High frequency noise The inverter model is made without considering any harmonics
noise. This is done such that faster simulation times can be achieved, since modelling a
4 kHz noise will require smaller simulation steps. In addition, the inﬂuence will be minimal
as most modern inverters are built with ﬁlters to absorb most the disturbance.
Additionally, it will be diﬃcult to validate, as the inverter is mounted within the cabinet,
which makes it tricky to measure the DC directly.
This assumption therefore allow for a simple current source representation of the inverter
and a RMS representation of the AC side, as the individual switching- and grid noise are
neglected.
The power factor angle is assumed to be 0.
Inverter eﬃciency Inverter eﬃciency is known to depend on both power and voltage. An
experiment was performed at DTU in an attempt to characterize the eﬃciency as a function
of power at diﬀerent operating voltages (Appendix C.2 on page 151). The accuracy of the
BMS controller was however ineﬃcient, which resulted in unrealistic eﬃciency estimations.
Instead NSS provided an eﬃciency characteristic at 800 V, which is included in appendix
C.2 on page 155.
3.3.2
Model overview
Figure 3.30: Inverter model overview.
The inverter model consists of an electrical current source and a voltage sensor as electrical
equivalent and a mathematical calculation, calculating current setpoint based on power
71
==PAGE:94==
3. Development of Simulink Models
reference and measured voltage. Both can be seen in Figure 3.30. The DC side of the
model is represented by a current source, while the AC-grid connection is represented as a
RMS power value. The inverter takes a P Inv
Ref signal as a setpoint of power on the AC-side.
The AC power is then transformed to DC power using the eﬃciency ηInv of the inverter.
3.3.3
Static characterization of inverter
The inverter model in Simulink has two physical connections; the positive and negative
terminal which can be directly coupled with the BESS model. It has two input data
connections: the requested power P Inv
Ref and the inverter operating voltage. The sign
convention of the requested power is designed such that positive power means discharging
the BESS and negative power means charging the BESS.
In addition, the inverter has one single output which is the voltage reference Vref that is
sent to the BESS BMS.
In order to determine which direction power ﬂows, the following logic is used to determine
how the current is calculated:
IDC =
P Inv
Ref
VDC
· x



x = ηInv,
if Discharging : P > 0
x =
1
ηInv ,
if Charging : P < 0
(3.37)
The IDC current is used in a controllable current source which is directly coupled to the
battery. The voltage measurement is used as the voltage reference Vref which is being fed
to the BMS described in 3.2.4. The battery is set to maintain VDC = 800 V when it is
connected to the inverter, as this is the ideal operating voltage with the highest inverter
eﬃciency. Due to the cell switching dynamics, there will always be an oﬀset, and the model
will therefore have a voltage sensor for measuring VDC.
The inverter works such that the it requests a power. A lookup table, then uses the
requested power to determine the conversion eﬃciency. It then measures the voltage across
the two terminals and calculate a current based on equation 3.37. Finally, it uses the ideal
current source to output the calculated current setpoint.
By modelling the inverter like this it makes it faster to simulate and much easier to
handle. The disadvantage is that the higher order harmonics will not be modelled and
disturbances on the AC grid will be perfectly ﬁltered out. While this provides a less accurate
representation, it also allows isolation of the dynamic behaviour of the battery without the
harmonics interfering with the fast response of the cell switching. The simpliﬁed model was
also chosen as any other inverter model would require a dedicated control of the switching
with PLL, DQ-transformation and other functionalities that will make the models model
more complex that ultimately will increase the simulation time.
3.4
Thermal model
This section provides a detailed description of the modelling approach for the thermal
model, including all considerations and assumptions that have been made.
The section will go through the following:
72
==PAGE:95==
3. Development of Simulink Models
• Assumptions and simpliﬁcations.
• Model overview.
• Experiment to determine the thermal time constant.
• Thermal model: module level.
• Thermal model: cell level.
In order to model the thermal characteristics of the BESS, two diﬀerent thermal models will
be designed and built in Simulink: a thermal model describing the thermal characteristics
of one module and a thermal model on cell level. The thermal cell model will then be
scaled up to represent a module.
To create these two models, a thermal experiment is needed to estimate the thermal time
constants for a complete module and two individual cells such that the diﬀerent thermal
resistances in the model can be determined.
3.4.1
Assumptions
Thermal modelling, like modelling the electrical battery equivalent, can be highly
sophisticated if every detail is to be considered.
Considering all thermal paths and minor details will make the model too complex and
challenging to validate, while making the thermal model too simple will result in poor
accuracy.
Thus, for this project the following assumptions were made in order to simplify the thermal
model as much as possible without compromising the overall accuracy.
The cooling air is a perfect heat sink at 25 °C
modelling the airﬂow, especially with the hexagon cooling screws, would be extremely
complex due to the ﬂuid-like behaviour of air. Hence, modelling the behaviour of air is not
considered.
Instead the cooling air is assumed to be a perfect heat sink at 25 °C. This simpliﬁes the
mathematical equations used to describe the thermal dynamics. The 25 °C is based on the
equipped cooling system on the actual battery system, which aims to maintain the battery
system at 25 °C during operation to protect the cells from overheating. The cooling system
is designed such that the fans are not turned on before the battery system’s temperature
reaches 30 °C. However, this is not considered in the thermal model as the heat sink is
assumed to be at constant 25 °C.
This assumption results in a thermal barrier between modules, as the thermal sink will
remain at 25 °C and thereby prevent any heat exchange between modules. This isolates
each module from the next, as a channel of cooled air will always ﬂow between them.
The generated heat originate from electric losses
Even though losses due to magnetization and other phenomena are known, the losses is
minimal compared to the heat losses. This is further true for DC current, as magnetization
losses is depending on changes in current. Despite the cell switching and the high frequency
noise from the converter, the current in the battery still provides a highly stable DC signal.
Neglecting these eﬀects will not aﬀect the thermal behaviour signiﬁcantly. Therefore it is
73
==PAGE:96==
3. Development of Simulink Models
assumed that the heat losses are only originating from the electric losses in the battery
system, which simplify the calculations as seen in 2.4.
Only resistive losses contribute to heat losses
The generated heat calculations will be based solely on the diﬀerent resistances that has
been discussed throughout the previous sections. This means that the capacitors from
the RC branches are not included in the cell when calculating the losses. By doing this it
is expected that the calculated heat losses will be higher than they actually are. During
switching of the cells, the losses will depend on the state of the capacitors. If a cell is
engaged with an "empty" capacitor, some of the current applied to the cell will be absorbed
by the capacitor, which will result in lower heat losses.
When calculating losses, the ideal method is to measure the simulated current and terminal
voltage and then multiply them with the binary control signal. This is something which
can be done physically as well, and it would be a valuable comparison. However, this will
include the hysteresis losses which can not be directly connected to heat losses, as it is a
chemical reaction within the cell. The reactions inside the cell could result in byproducts
that simply stores energy but do not contribute to the processes when charging/discharging.
The compromise would then be to measure the voltage across the RC branches with a
voltage sensor. While this is not possible to recreate in the real system, it will provide
information on the generated heat for each RC branch.
However, this is not doable in the complete battery system consisting of 12 modules as it
would make the model too complex.
Thermal radiation
Any thermal radiation is neglected as this is diﬃcult to model and validate.
3.4.2
Model overview
(a) Thermal module model.
(b) Thermal cell model scaled to a module.
Figure 3.31: Overview of the thermal models.
74
==PAGE:97==
3. Development of Simulink Models
There were built two diﬀerent thermal models. A simple thermal model which treats a
single module as a single thermal object using equation 2.19, with experimental thermal
time constant and calculated thermal mass. This model will henceforth be referred to as
thermal module model and sketched in ﬁgure 3.31a.
The second model is based on modelling the individual cell as a thermal mass and then
scale up to a single module, modelling the heat transfer path between cells and through
module walls. This model is based on Simulink thermal modelling blocks. This model is not
part of the original scope and is therefore not as ﬂushed out. This model will henceforth
be refereed to as thermal cell model. The naming refers to the cells in the module being
modeled as individual thermal objects. This model is seen in ﬁgure 3.31b.
3.4.3
Experiment to determine the thermal time constant
The main purpose for this experiment is to determine the thermal time constant for one
complete battery module and two individual battery cells. The thermal time constant
described in 2.4.4 is important for characterising the heat dissipation.
The cooling/heating of any object is known to follow an exponential decay towards the
ambient temperature as described in section 2.4.4. The exponential progress is described
using the thermal time constant as seen in equation 2.8.
Therefore the thermal experiment is designed such that an object is heated up to a known
temperature T1. As the object reaches thermal steady state it will be moved to a new
temperature T2. During the experiment the temperature will be monitored as a function
of time t.
The remaining unknown in equation 2.8 is the thermal time constant. As this is an
exponential decay, the expected temperature change is shown in Figure 2.15.
The thermal time constant is then found by measuring the time at which the temperature
has changed by 63.2% of its initial temperature T1 towards T2 from equation 2.10.
Thermal time constant experiment setup
The experiment is split into two phases: Heating phase 1) Heating the components in the
climate chamber. Cooling phase 2) monitored cooling, as shown in Figure 3.32.
Firstly, the temperature sensors have been set up as shown in the conﬁguration in
Figure 3.33.
There are used a total of 14 thermocouples for the thermal experiment. The sensors has
been placed such that:
• There are 10 sensors for the module, where three are in the front, three in the middle,
three in the back, and 1 inside the centre of the module.
• There is one sensor for each of the two individual cells (Cell A and B).
• There are 2 sensors to measure the ambient temperature.
75
==PAGE:98==
3. Development of Simulink Models
Figure 3.32: Thermal time constant test setup.
(a) Complete overview of thermocouple placement.
(b) Placement of thermocou-
ple at the cells in the side.
(c) Placement of thermocou-
ples in the middle column.
Figure 3.33: Placement of thermocouples.

==PAGE:99==
3. Development of Simulink Models
Heating phase
During the heating phase, the module and the two cells are placed inside the climate
chamber provided by NSS, see Figure 3.34. The three components stay in the chamber
until the temperature of every sensor has reached a steady state. This is done to make
sure that the temperature is evenly distributed. The temperature inside the chamber has
been set to approximately 40 °C which was the maximum allowed.
(a) Climate chamber.
(b) Module/cell setup inside the cli-
mate chamber during the heating
phase.
Figure 3.34: Experimental setup during the heating phase.
Cooling phase
As the components reach a thermal steady state after approximately 18 hours as shown in
Figure D.1, the cooling phase can begin. This means that the module and cells are moved
to an undisturbed room with a constant ambient temperature. The module and cells are
then placed separately in the conﬁgurations shown in Figure 3.32, while the temperature
is constantly monitored.
The three conﬁgurations as shown in Figure 3.32 are the following:
• Module - with thermal isolating material at the top and bottom.
• Cell B - with thermal isolating material at the top and bottom and open space to
the sides.
• Cell A - with thermal isolating material at the top and bottom while being surrounded
by other non-heated cells.
These 3 conﬁgurations will provide suﬃcient experimental data which can be used to
estimate the thermal time constants, which are necessary for designing and building the
thermal model of a single module and a single cell. The actual setup during the experiment
is shown in Figure 3.35.
For the single module, thermal isolating material is placed at the top and the bottom in
order to prevent any heat transfer. This is due to the assumption that there are no heat
transfer between modules (see 3.4.1). Thereby the time constant for the module will be
based on the heat transfer occurring from the sides of the module.
77
==PAGE:100==
3. Development of Simulink Models
(a) Module setup and ambient sensor placement.
(b) Cell A setup.
(c) Cell B setup.
Figure 3.35: Experimental setup during the cooling phase.
For simplicity, the fans will be turned oﬀ, as the airﬂow from the fans would inﬂuence
the time constant. However, by repeating the experiment with the fans on, a thermal
time constant for the forced convection could be found. Then the two time constants
could be modelled as the time constant at 0% and 100% fan speed respectively, and a
linear interpolation between the points could be made. By doing this it would allow the
simulation model to consider a simple for airﬂow cooling and thereby make the model even
more accurate since the forced convection has been proved to have a signiﬁcant impact on
the cooling as mentioned in [49].
For the cells, thermal isolating material has been placed above and below. However, there
is a need for two cells where one of them (cell A) is surrounded by other non-heated cells,
and the second cell (cell B) is standing by itself. By doing this it is possible to ﬁnd a
thermal time constant for the conduction between two cells and the thermal time constant
for the convection between the cell and the surrounding air.
The reason for doing this, is that when designing the thermal model for a single cell, 27
cells are going to be placed inside a module. In this case most of the cells will be adjacent
to other cells where conduction is assumed to take place. However, a few cells will be
adjacent to the sides of the module, where convection from the cell to the surrounding air
is assumed to take place. The two diﬀerent thermal time constants can then be used to
determine the heat ﬂow.
78
==PAGE:101==
3. Development of Simulink Models
Thermal time constant experiment results
Figure 3.36: Temperature progressions during the cooling phase of the thermal experiment.
The thermal measurements for the experiment are shown in Figure 3.36. From this it is
observed that the temperatures start at around 40 °C. However, since the measurements
of the cooling was started a short while after they the components were taken out of the
chamber, some of the thermocouples were already measuring temperatures below 40 °C.
Otherwise the temperatures were following the expected exponential decay starting from
the initial temperature and then moving towards the ambient temperature which was
measured to approximately 21 °C. The complete time duration of the cooling phase was
measured to be around 28 hours.
One thing to notice is the change in the ambient temperature in the beginning and
during the end of the experiment. In the beginning of the experiment the three heated
components were placed in a room, which had access to a large garage door. This garage
door was opened 30 minutes after the cooling phase was initialised and wasn’t close until
approximately 45 minutes had passed. This resulted in the rapid temperature decrease
shown in the beginning of the experiment.
The small increase in the ambient temperature towards the end of the experiment is due
to the sun heating up the room.
The temperature change from the initial temperature T1 in % is then calculated and
plotted as a function of time, as shown in Figure 3.37.
The time constant for cell A and cell B has been estimated to be 6123 seconds and 3159
seconds respectively.
For the thermal time constant of the single module, all the diﬀerent temperature
measurements inside the module have been considered. This is done in order to get
a realistic approximation of the thermal time constant, since the sides of the module most
likely will cool down faster than the centre of the module.

3.4.5
Thermal model - cell level
It should be noted that this model was not initially included in the project plan and still
is a work-in-progress as it requires further development. However, the model is included in
order to describe how the thermal module model could be expanded to be more accurate
and simultaneously describe how the heat transfer is occurring within the module itself.
The thermal cell model is built in the Simscape environment using its build-in simulations
blocks for heat calculations instead of mathematical equations. This allows for a better
overview of the heat paths and an easier scalability. The disadvantage is the increased
diﬃculty in validating that the model is operating as intended.
82
==PAGE:105==
3. Development of Simulink Models
Figure 3.40: Heat paths considered for the simulation model on cell level.
Firstly, the major heat transfer paths are identiﬁed within a single cell:
• Cell Conduction

−−−−−−−→Air.
• Cell Conduction
−−−−−−−→Neighbouring cell.
• Cell Conduction
−−−−−−−→Aluminium cage (Module)
Convection
−−−−−−−→Air.
Each individual cell is considered as one thermal mass, which means the internal heat
distribution within a cell is neglected.
The heat transfer paths are then implemented in the cell model as shown in Figure 3.41
and 3.42. Figure 3.41 shows how the electric loss, Ploss, is transferred through the screws
(Upwards path) or to the neighbors (Right path).
From Figure 3.41 the marked area, called "Thermal conduction via cell walls", the heat
conducted through the cell walls is raised to module level.
The module level is shown in Figure 3.42, where the heat can either be transferred between
the cells or to the surrounding air through the aluminium cage. On module level, a heat
sink is connected to the aluminium casing. In Figure 3.42 the surrounding heat sink is
referred to as the "Heat-sink rail".

The following parameters must be determined for the thermal cell model:
• The generated heat.
• Conductive heat transfer from a cell to a screw.
• Convective heat transfer from screw to the surrounding air.
• Convective heat transfer from the top of the cell to the surrounding air.
• Conductive heat transfer from a cell to a neighboring cell.
• Conductive heat transfer from a cell to the side of aluminium case.
• Convective heat transfer from the sides of aluminium case to the surrounding air.
The generated heat is calculated by multiplying the cell resistance with the current as
expressed in Equation 2.4.
The calculations of the diﬀerent parameters that is needed to determine the heat ﬂows for
convection and conduction, is included in appendix D.2.
