3.5
The photovoltaic system
This section provides a detailed description of the modelling approach and the development
of the photovoltaic (PV) system model.
This include the following:
• Assumptions.
• Model overview.
• Final realisation of PV system model.
• A description of the developed MPPT algorithm.
85
==PAGE:108==
3. Development of Simulink Models
The PV system is based on the system that is installed at Bornholm, which consists of 10
parallel-connected PV strings, each consisting of 24 series-connected PV panels. The PV
panels are based to the type that is included in section E.1.
3.5.1
Assumptions
Communication speed
As described in section 3.2.1, the communication speed of the BMS, battery and the other
components were assumed to be instant.
However, in order to provide a realistic estimation of the overall MPPT eﬃciency, the
command execution speed of the BMS controller must be considered. This is done in order
to simulate how fast the actual system can calculate new setpoints for the battery.
Based on [8] the execution speed of the controller was estimated to be approximately 10 Hz.
The angle of the PV panel
The tilt angle and orientation of the developed PV panel is not considered in the model.
This means that the PV panel is assumed to always have the optimal angle and orientation
towards the sun.
This is not a problem since the objective of this project in terms of the PV system, is
to investigate whether it is possible to develop a MPPT decision algorithm that takes
advantages of the reconﬁgurable BESS.
Therefore only the MPPT eﬃciency is of interest, as in how well can the developed MPPT
algorithm track the maximum power point by engaging and bypassing cells in the BESS
for any given irradiance.
3.5.2
Model overview
An overview of the PV model is provided in Figure 3.43. The PV model consist of an
electrical current source, a voltage sensor and a mathematical function calculating the
current setpoint based on inputs shown in Figure 3.43. A detailed description how the
model was realised will be given in the next section.
Figure 3.43: PV system model overview - The PV function block and current source.
86
==PAGE:109==
3. Development of Simulink Models
3.5.3
Final realisation of PV system model
The PV panel itself was built from the ground up as a MATLAB function block.
There are two reasons for building the PV panel model from the ground up:
Firstly, the built-in PV panel in Simulink belongs to a completely diﬀerent environment
called Specialized Power Systems (SPS). Since the rest of the models are built in the
Simscape environment, the interconnections of the two environments made the simulation
times incredibly slow.
Secondly, the existing PV panel was not able to provide the values for voltage and current
which would provide the maximum power point (MPP) at any given irradiance. Hence, by
building the PV panel from the ground up it is possible to extract the MPP, which makes
it more straightforward to calculate the MPPT eﬃciency.
The MATLAB function block uses the described equations in subsection 2.5.1, and the
developed MATLAB PV function block is included in appendix section A.1.
The setup of the PV system is shown in Figure 3.43 and has been built such that it consists
of:
• The MATLAB function block (PV panel) containing all the necessary calculations to
simulate the characteristics of a PV panel.
• Controllable current source which takes the calculated current from the MATLAB
function block.
The PV panel uses 6 inputs in order to determine the output current, which include:
• The solar irradiance.
• The temperature in ℃.
• The measured voltage across the PV panel.
• The panel type.
• The number of panels that are in series and parallel.
Regarding the panel type, the function block has been designed such that it is possible to
register your own type of panel based on the standard speciﬁcations that can be found in
any PV panel datasheet.
In addition, the PV panel has 4 outputs which include:
• The output current which is used in the controllable current source.
• The three maximum points for both the current, voltage and power.
Finally, the PV system have two terminals that physically connects the PV panel to the
battery system.
The PV system’s current and voltage are monitored by the submodel MPPT Algorithm,
realised by feeding back the information from submodel PV into submodel MPPT Algorithm.
Based on the measured current and voltage, the MPPT Algorithm then determines the
optimal voltage, which provides the MPP.
87
==PAGE:110==
3. Development of Simulink Models
3.5.4
Design of the MPPT control algorithm
This section will focus on developing the MPPT control algorithm by adapting and
optimizing the original P&O original algorithm for a reconﬁgurable battery system. The
objective of this algorithm is to engage and bypass the individual battery cells in order to
maximize the power output of the connected PV system.
The major disadvantage of the original P&O algorithm described in section 2.5.4, is the
constant oscillation around the MPP, as reported in [8]. In most cases, a great alternative
is a variable step size P&O algorithm described in [57]. However, this requires the ability
to vary the voltage with high precision, which is not possible in the current battery control
system, where the change in voltage could only be in steps of 3V or above based on the
current SOC.
Perturb and observe - Steady State
To the best of our knowledge there is no alternative MPPT control algorithm which takes
full advantage of a reconﬁgurable BESS. Therefore, a new algorithm based on the principle
of the original P&O is proposed, which allows for steady state voltage operation at the
optimal voltage point. This algorithm is henceforth referred to as P&O-SS for its steady
state capabilities.
As shown in Figure 3.44 the P&O-SS still uses the original P&O algorithm, however the
objective of the P&O-SS is to ﬁnd a steady state for the BESS system such that the
oscillations can be removed.
To make sure this is achieved the following additional functions are needed:
• Update-Function.
• Steady-State Hook.
• Maintain Optimal Steady-State.
• Release Steady-State.
A major part of P&O-SS extension is the handling of historical values. To explain this, the
concept of iteration "K-n" is used. Values labelled K refer to measured or calculated values
in the current iteration, while "K-n" are values from the "n" prior iteration. Running the
algorithm at 10 Hz means that Vmeas,K is the voltage at current time step (t = t0), and
Vmeas,K−1 was measured in prior iteration (t = t0 −0.1 s). Vmeas,K−2 is then two iterations
ago (t = t0 −0.2 s) and so on.
"K-n" is thereby deﬁning the value from nth iteration back (t = t0 −n/f).
88
==PAGE:111==
3. Development of Simulink Models
Figure 3.44: Flowchart of P&O-SS algorithm which is used with the BESS.
Update-function
The update function, marked as the green blocks in Figure 3.44, is a simple method of
keeping track of recent values. It takes the newest value K and insert it into the ﬁrst
element of the corresponding cell array, thereby shifting all prior values to the next element.
Such that the ﬁrst element in the old array becomes the second element etc.
Since the array have a set length, the last element of the array is removed/forgotten. An
illustration of the update function is shown in Figure 3.45.
In this algorithm ﬁve arrays are updated to keep track of the history. The following
deﬁnitions were used for the diﬀerent arrays:
89
==PAGE:112==
3. Development of Simulink Models
Figure 3.45:
Illustration of "Update-function" changing the CellArray with a new
observation.
• CellArray: Array containing the recorded cell states.
• CellSum: Sum of the previous iteration of CellArray.
• Error: Array containing the last 3 measured absolute power values before steady
state.
• PowerArray: Array containing the measured PV power history.
• Vref-array: Array containing the reference voltage setpoints for the battery.
Before returning to the start of the ﬂowchart the sum of the CellArray, which include the
current number of engaged cells, is saved in a new a parameter called CellSum as shown in
Figure 3.44.
Steady-State hook
With the update function storing the history of the previous iterations, it is possible to
deﬁne a steady state criterion, which if met, will freeze the reference output.
In order to understand the principle behind the "Steady state hook" it is important
to understand the term "Wave". Figure 3.46 shows the results from the original P&O
algorithm where the reference voltage is increasing towards the optimal voltage point. As
the optimal voltage point is increased and reaches the optimal voltage, it will begin to
oscillate around that point. Since the battery operates in steps of cells, the oscillation will
become a repeating pattern of active cells (square pattern). In this case every 4th step in
the voltage is deﬁned as a "Wave". The length of the described arrays must therefore be
deﬁned as 4, 8, 12 etc.
The steady state condition is then deﬁned as a comparison between the sum of the CellArray
and the CellSum. This will capture if the number of engaged cells is repeated.
X
(CellArray[K]) == CellSum
(3.44)
90
==PAGE:113==
3. Development of Simulink Models
Figure 3.46: P&O oscillating around optimal, with repeating "Waves" marked along with
past iteration K-n.
Release Steady-State criterion
This if-statement is implemented in order to release the steady state hook. The given
condition dictates that the power change, |∆P|, during steady state operation must be
larger than a deﬁned dead-band, max(Error). This dead-band is automatically updated
through the Error every time a cell is engaged or disengaged through the "Error-update"
block on the right path of the ﬂow chart. If the change in power during steady operation
is smaller than the given dead-band, it means that the change in the irradiance isn’t
signiﬁcant enough and the algorithm should therefore maintain the current operating point.
If the change in power is larger than the dead-band, then the algorithm must release the
steady state and ﬁnd a new operating point in order to reach the new MPP.
The release steady-state function is given as:
|∆P| > max(Error)
(3.45)
Maintain optimal steady-state
To maintain steady state, the 4 elements in the CellArray is replaced with the optimal
voltage reference signal. This means that:
CellArray(:) = CellArray(K)
(3.46)
The PowerArray is used in order to maintain this steady state. The optimal voltage
reference is then chosen by taking the element index of the PowerArray which has the
highest measured power. That index is then used to locate the element in the voltage
reference array (Vref-array) which corresponds to the MPP. This is achieved by the following
expression:
V ref −array(:) = V ref −array(LOC(Max(PowerArray)))
(3.47)
91
==PAGE:114==
3. Development of Simulink Models
Clear history
As the steady state is released, the elements in the CellArray are replaced with an impossible
setpoint (example: "-1" active cells) to ensure that the controller performs at least one
complete "Wave" before steady state is allowed again.
If this is not performed, the control system might lock onto the same (wrong) voltage level,
as seen in Figure 3.47. This happens whenever the control system begin to change the
voltage reference in the wrong direction. This will result in the controller choosing the
same steady state as before (Red line in Figure 3.47).
By replacing all the elements in the CellArray with an impossible value, it will prevent
this and allow a full "Wave" to be performed (Blue line in Figure 3.47).
The P&O-SS function implemented in MATLAB as a function block can be found in
appendix section A.3.
Figure 3.47: Settling point if history is not cleared (Red) compared to cleared history
steady state (Blue) after radiance change.
92
==PAGE:115==
3. Development of Simulink Models
3.6
Summary of developed models
Through this section, 6 models have been designed and implemented in Simulink.
Firstly, the LFP cell that is used in the reconﬁgurable battery system was modelled. It
was decided to build the cell model based on the EECM described in section 3.1.4 by
including a voltage source, a series resistance and two RC Branches as a compromise
between accuracy and model complexity. The EECM model has 6 variable circuit elements,
which all are functions of the operating conditions such as the SOC, temperature, and
state (charge/discharge). This was realised by implementing 3D-lookup tables for each
circuit element which uses the above mention conditions as input.
The operating conditions and the resulting parameters for the circuit elements were
characterised by using the Simulink Design Optimizer. During the development it was
discovered that the Parameter Optimizer provided the best results when the initial guesses
given to the diﬀerent parameters was manually estimated compared to random or constant
initial values.
A great eﬀort was put into developing a method to describe the hysteresis eﬀect, since this
eﬀect is signiﬁcant in LFPs. The ﬁnal method to determine the OCV vs. SOC hysteresis
eﬀect involved a speciﬁc experiment that was designed to help capture the hysteresis eﬀect.
The result from the ﬁnal method, was a hysteresis ratio constant κhyst, which is a ratio
between the charge and discharge curve as a function of the change in SOC. κhyst = 1
means that the charge curve is fully utilized, while κhyst = 0 means that the discharge
curve is fully utilized.
Secondly, the complete BESS system was built by scaling up the developed battery cell
model as this project focuses on a single dual-string consisting of 12 modules, which sums
up to 324 cells in total. During the development of the battery system, particular focus
was on the losses of diﬀerent components, such as terminals, MOSFETS, connectors etc.
In addition the battery system’s BMS was upgraded such that it can be controlled based
on either a voltage or a current reference.
The third model was a simple inverter model. The DC side of the inverter was represented
by a single current source, while the AC side was represented as an RMS power value.
The inverter uses the requested power to estimate the actual power output based on its
eﬃciency curve as shown in ﬁgure C.5. The operating voltage, the requested power, and
the eﬃciency is then used to estimate a current which is directly used as input in the
current source on the DC-side.
The fourth and the ﬁfth models are the thermal module model and the thermal cell
model, respectively. In order to create these models, a thermal experiment was designed to
estimate the thermal time constants for a complete module and two individual cells such
that the diﬀerent thermal resistances in the model could be determined.
For the thermal module model, a single module was considered as one thermal mass and
the generated heat losses were based on the diﬀerent components losses described in section
3.2.3.
The cell model was a bit more sophisticated, as it considers a single cell as a thermal mass.
It is then scaled up to represent a single module, meaning that 27 thermal cell models have
93
==PAGE:116==
3. Development of Simulink Models
been placed in series. The thermal cell model considers each individual heat transfer path
within the cell and the module. However, as mentioned earlier, this model was initially not
included in the project plan and still is a work-in-progress as some of the heat transfer
paths are yet to be determined.
For both thermal models only passive cooling through the surrounding air has been
considered, which means that any active cooling hasn’t been modelled yet.
Finally, the PV system model was built from the ground up using the literature described
in section 2.5 instead of using the built-in PV panel component from the Specialized Power
Systems environment in Simulink. This resulted in a PV system which is much more
manageable and ﬂexible, while the simulation time got reduced signiﬁcantly.
Furthermore, a MPPT decision algorithm, P&O-SS, which utilizes the unique features of
the reconﬁgurable battery system was proposed. It aims to ﬁnd the voltage reference, that
provides the highest possible power output from the PV system. As the P&O-SS starts to
oscillate around the optimal voltage point, the algorithm will lock the optimal amount of
engaged cells such that the optimal voltage point is achieved. The algorithm does that by
keeping track of the number of engaged cells during each iteration. The amount of engaged
cells that achieves the optimal voltage point is then maintained until changes occur in the
irradiance proﬁle. Should a large change in irradiance occur, the P&O-SS algorithm will
release the steady state such that a new optimal voltage can be found and the new MPP
can be reached.
94
==PAGE:117==
4
EXPERIMENTAL MODEL VALIDATION
This chapter will focus on validating the developed cell- and BESS model, by characterizing
a test procedure that can provide the necessary experimental data for the two models.
This chapter will include the following:
• Measurement equipment (4.1).
• General description of the Parker proﬁle (4.2).
• Test procedure, results, and discussion of Parker proﬁle on a single cell (4.3).
• Test procedure, results and discussion of Parker proﬁle on a single module (4.4).
4.1
Measurement equipment
Throughout all the experiments, data was acquired in one of three ways. Either by the
Nerve Switch, the DAQ or the HBM machine.
Nerve Switch When the BESS system was in use, all the relevant signal states was
recorded and later exported from the Nerve Switch. The Nerve Switch could record SOC,
cell voltage, engagement signal etc. with 10Hz and a limited accuracy of ±30 mV.
DAQ The DAQ equipment is a stand-alone measurement equipment, which measured at
10Hz and at a very high resolution. This equipment was primarily used by NSS during
testing of diﬀerent cells.
HBM The "Gen7ta" HBM machine is owned by DTU and it can sample between 50kHz
and 2MHz at a 16bit resolution resulting in highly accurate measurements. The equipment
was set up with 3 "GN610B" cards allowing for 18 simultaneous measurements (current and
voltage). The datasheet for the HBM machine and cards can be found through [58, 59].
By using the HBM it is ensured that the measured data has the exact same timestamps,
and that it is measured with as high accuracy as possible compared to the measurement
equipment on the Nerve Switch.
4.2
Parker proﬁle
To validate the Simulink models, a load proﬁle will be applied on both the developed cell
model as well as a single module model and their physical counterparts. The load proﬁle
is an adapted version of the developed Parker proﬁle from [60].
Figure 4.1 shows the Parker proﬁle that is going to be used at both the single cell model
as well as a real cell.
95
==PAGE:118==
4. Experimental model validation
Figure 4.1: Parker proﬁle - current proﬁle for validation of the developed cell model.
The diﬀerent phases during the Parker proﬁle has diﬀerent purposes:
• The gradual ramps in current are designed to evaluate how well the models are
following the constantly increasing/decreasing setpoint.
• The pulses that instantly changes the setpoint are designed to test how well the
dynamic performance of the models are predicted.
• The polarity switch is designed to catch any behaviour the models are experiencing
when instantly switching between charging and discharging.
• Finally the sinusoidal wave will be used to investigate whether any capacitive or
inductive eﬀects are not taken into account in the cell model. This was not a part of
the original Parker proﬁle.
The Parker proﬁle was chosen as the load proﬁle, as it includes diﬀerent current patterns
that makes it possible to validate the accuracy of the performance characteristics and
dynamical behaviour. Additionally, the Parker proﬁle includes behaviour not included
in the characterisation experiments of the cell. Finally, the Parker proﬁle is a short and
precise test as the time duration only is 17 min.
4.3
Parker proﬁle on a single cell
This section will present the designed test setup that has been used to perform the Parker
test at cell level. The results will then be presented and analysed, followed by a discussion.
The purpose of this Parker test is to gather reference data for validating the simulation
accuracy of the cell model.
4.3.1
Experimental setup
During the experiment, the cell voltage and current was measured by the HBM equipment.
The measured current was then applied to cell model to replicate the exact current
behaviour.
Then, the measured voltage was compared to the simulated voltage in to order to validate
96
==PAGE:119==
4. Experimental model validation
the accuracy of the cell model. The input and output as well as setup is shown in ﬁgure
4.2. The Parker proﬁle supplied to the cell is shown in Figure 4.1 and the simulation was
performed with a ﬁxed time-step of 100ms.
Figure 4.2: Experimental setup of Parker proﬁle at cell level. Including the controlled
inputs and measured/simulated outputs
Procedure
A step-by-step procedure is described as follows:
1. Insert cell into thermal chamber.
a) Set chamber temperature to 25 °C
b) Measure initial SOC and voltage
2. Charge cell to approximately 50 %
a) Note down the initial SOC and voltage.
3. Let rest for approx. 1.5 hours.
4. Apply the Parker cell test to the cell.
a) Use the Parker proﬁle as input to the current source.
b) Monitor the voltage, current and temperature with the HBM
The complete test procedure including duration, description, scope etc. can be found in
appendix section C.3 on page 156.
4.3.2
Results
At the beginning of the Parker proﬁle, the cell was balanced with SOCCell
init = 0 %. A 200 A
charging current was applied in the beginning to charge the cell to approximately 40 %.
The cell was then rested for 10 min before the Parker proﬁle was applied, since 1.5 hours
was not possible. During the Parker proﬁle the current was oscillating between 100 A and
−100 A as this corresponds to 1C. This c-rate was chosen as it was discovered in section
3.1.5 that the cell behaved slightly diﬀerent at c-rates higher than 1C.
The test was designed such that the ﬁnal SOC will be the same as the initial SOC by
having the exact same charge and discharge patterns.
The analysis of the results is split into two parts:
The ﬁrst part evaluates the dynamic behaviour and the performance of the Simulink model
97
==PAGE:120==
4. Experimental model validation
when comparing the predicted voltage with voltages measured by the HBM. Particular
focus will be given to the accuracy of the voltage as well as the dynamic response.
The second part will focus on the hysteresis ratio constant κhyst.
It should be noted, that the HBM machine had a 6 mV oﬀset in measurements during cell
testing. This 6 mV oﬀset was corrected after the experiment.
Voltage prediction and dynamic behaviour
Figure 4.3: Completed Parker proﬁle for a single cell including the voltage, voltage error,
current and SOC: comparison of experimentally measured and simulated voltages, and the
simulated SOC and current progression
Figure 4.3 provides an overview of the complete Parker proﬁle and the progression of
the voltage and SOC during the simulation. It should be noted that the voltages are a
comparison between the measured and the simulated voltages, while the SOC and current
progression are simulated.
As mentioned in the test procedure the cell has been charged up to 40 % SOC and then
rested for 10 minutes before initializing the Parker proﬁle.
Firstly, it is noted that there sometimes is a slight oﬀset, which is expected as discussed in
subsubsection 3.1.6. The ﬁnal result showed that the cell model have an average absolute
error of 8 mV and a mean error of −3.74 mV meaning it has a slight tendency to under
predict.
The errors are summarized in table Table 4.1.
Absolute Mean
Mean
Max
Min
VError [mV]
7.99
-3.74
15.36
-17.18
Table 4.1: Overview of the error between simulated and measured voltage.
98
==PAGE:121==
4. Experimental model validation
Looking at the top graph in Figure 4.3 the dynamic behaviour is observed to be accurate.
Firstly, the ﬁrst triangle-wave is depicted with suﬃcient accuracy as the shape of the
simulated voltage is similar to the measured voltage. Especially the points where the state
is changed from charge to discharge is showing that the cell model accurately predicts the
dynamic behaviour. This is primarily due to the implemented hysteresis function that
adds a smooth transition between charging and discharging and vice versa.
The simulated dynamic response can be observed to follow the physical cell during instant
polarity change. However, there is a constant oﬀset of 10 −15 mV. This oﬀset is similar to
the measured steady state voltage oﬀset in the pulse experiment described in section 3.1.6.
The polarization (charge transfer process and diﬀusion voltage) is well depicted, as the
exponential decay/growth is well matched with the measured voltage. However, at the
rest between the initial pulses the model is having some issues completely replicating the
voltage behaviour. Since the measured voltage has a slightly softer decay compared to the
simulated voltage.
The same is observed from the pulses without rest.
The predicted and measured sinusoidal wave are very similar. However, as the end of the
sinusoidal is reached it is observed that the simulated and the measured voltage is lagging
slightly behind the applied current signal. This is to be expected due to the capacitance
included in the RC branches in the developed cell model.
The phase shift can be calculated based on the diﬀerence in peak between the voltage and
current as seen in equation 4.1 - 4.2.
ΘSimulated = 360 · ∆t · f = 360 · 7.5 s · 3.3 mHz = 18.43°
(4.1)
ΘMeasured = 360 · ∆t · f = 360 · 7.5 s · 3.3 mHz = 8.9°
(4.2)
The mismatch between the measured and simulated phase shift of 10° is highly likely to
originate from the neglected parasitic inductance in the cell which has not been modelled.
Another factor inﬂuencing the phase shift was that the cell only experienced one wave.
The cell was therefore not in equilibrium which can clearly be observed in the diﬀerent
phase shift during positive and negative peaks of the sine wave.
Hence, the phase shift diﬀerence should therefore decrease slightly if the cell had experienced
a longer sinusoidal.
Finally, it is observed that the SOC is starting at the expected 40 % SOC. It then oscillates
between 43 % and 40 % SOC, and ﬁnally it ends at 40 % SOC. By keeping the SOC around
this area it is ensured that the error is kept as small as possible, due to the lower model
accuracy at below or above 10 % and 90 % SOC respectively. As discussed in section 2.1.5,
the OCV vs. SOC between 10 % and 90 % SOC can be linearly described. While below
10 % or above 90 %, the OCV vs. SOC can be exponentially described.
Hysteresis ratio
Figure 4.4 shows the simulated voltage, current, and hysteresis ratio constant κhyst. As
mentioned in section 4.3, the state before initializing the Parker proﬁle was a full charging
99
==PAGE:122==
4. Experimental model validation
process. Therefore, the hysteresis ratio is recorded as 1 in the beginning, which means that
the charging OCV curve is fully utilized. The ﬁrst few minutes of the Parker proﬁle (the
slow ramping) is charging and therefore, the hysteresis ratio is not changed. However, as
the polarity changes and the current are ramped down, the hysteresis ratio slowly converges
towards 0, as this indicates the discharging OCV curve. Thereafter, it is observed that the
overall hysteresis ratio oscillates between 0.2 and 0.7, meaning the duration of each state
isn’t long enough to reach the fully stage of either the discharging or charging OCV curve.
Thus, it is conﬁrmed that the hysteresis eﬀect is very important to take into consideration,
as observed with this load proﬁle, the complete OCV curve for the discharge is never fully
utilized, while the OCV curve for charging is only fully used in the beginning.
Note that during the sine-wave in Figure 4.4, the κHyst increases slightly more than
compared to the previous current pulses. This is due to the simple fact the change in SOC
is larger during the sine-wave as observed in Figure 4.3. As mentioned in section 3.1.5 the
hysteresis ratio is a function of ∆SOC.
Figure 4.4: Completed Parker proﬁle for a single cell including the voltage, current and
hysteresis ratio constant.
4.3.3
Discussion
The results of the Parker proﬁle on a single cell suggest that the developed cell model will
be able to accurately describe the performance of the cells that are going to be used in
the reconﬁgurable battery system with an average error of 8 mV. By including the two
RC branches and the hysteresis eﬀect to determine the OCV behaviour, it is possible to
describe the static and the dynamically behaviour for diﬀerent kinds of load patterns, such
as slow ramps, pulses etc.
The small constant oﬀset of 8 mV can be due to many small factors which haven’t been
implemented during the model development. First of all, one way to improve the model
100
==PAGE:123==
4. Experimental model validation
performance would be to perform the OCV experiments in a slightly diﬀerent way, which
includes 4 steps:
• 1. step: The fully charged cell is soaked at the test temperature for at least 2 hours
to ensure uniform temperature. The cell is then discharged at C/30 until lowest
voltage Vmin (manufacturer-speciﬁed) is reached.
• 2. step: Soak the cell at 25 °C for at least 2 hours. If the voltage is below Vmin then
charge it to Vmin and vice versa if the voltage is above Vmin.
• 3. step: Soak the cell at test temperature. Then charge the cell until voltage is Vmax.
• 4. step Soak the cell at 25 °C. If the cell voltage is below Vmax then charge the cell
with C/30 until it is. And the opposite if the cell voltage is below Vmax.
By doing this it is ensured that the correct maximum and minimum voltages has been
reached at each testing temperature, which will make the OCV curves more accurate at
temperatures diﬀerent from 25 °C. As discussed in section 3.1.3 the temperature does have
a small impact on the overall OCV.
Secondly, the pulsing experiment that was performed makes it diﬃcult to determine the
parameters of the cell at high and low levels of SOC. The parameters at low and high
SOCs are unreliable and will get a lower prediction accuracy compared to the parameters
at middle SOCs. As suggested earlier this could be solved by performing the pulsing
experiment with smaller pulse intervals at below 10 % SOC and above 90 % SOC. This
would ensure that the exponential relationship between the voltage and SOC would be
described with much higher accuracy allowing for better approximation of the parameters.
However, the oﬀset will not have that much of an impact due to the accuracy of the
measurement equipment that is used on the actual system. The accuracy is said to be
±30 mV which means that the cell model’s accuracy is acceptable.
In order to validate whether the temperature dependency that has been implemented is
accurate at the two remaining temperatures, the Parker proﬁle would have to be repeated
at the desired temperatures.
Regarding the cell parameters being current dependent, it is not clear whether this is
the case. Therefore, the Parker proﬁle would have to be adjusted such that there is a
proﬁle with higher current rates and a proﬁle with lower current rates. This would make it
possible to evaluate the cell model and analyse if the current also has an impact on the
performance.
Furthermore, some experiments performed by NSS showed that when the cell is operated
above 1C the cell resistance would deviate a lot compared to what would be expected
based on the results from 1C and below. This could mean two things: Firstly, the current
does have an impact on the cell parameters and the OCV or secondly, due to the high
currents more heat is being generated resulting in high temperatures which is proven too
has an impact. However, this would require more experiments to be performed on the
same cell at diﬀerent c-rates.
The cell model could be expanded even further, by considering the ageing and SOH of the
cell. One way to do this would be to use a more sophisticated method to determine the
SOC and SOH such as the Extended Kalman Filter [61]. This would allow the BMS to
control each cell more eﬃciently.
101
==PAGE:124==
4. Experimental model validation
A ﬁnal, but much more demanding method, would be to utilize the physical modelling
techniques which would allow the model to describe each individual process inside the cell.
The physical model is good a extrapolating, meaning it will provide reliable results beyond
the scenarios that was used to build the model.
4.4
Parker proﬁle on a single battery module
This section will ﬁrst present the designed test for the Parker proﬁle on a single battery
module, which include the experimental setup and procedure. The results of the Parker
test will then be presented and evaluated, and ﬁnally, a discussion of the results.
The objective of the Parker proﬁle on a single battery module, is to validate the accuracy
of the dynamic performance of the developed module model when switching cells.
4.4.1
Experimental setup
Figure 4.5 illustrate the input and output during the experiment and the simulation. For
the experiment, the Parker proﬁle was supplied to the module as a voltage. During the
experiment, the cell states were recorded by the module controller. The recorded cell states
were then used as input for the simulation in order to replicate the exact same behaviour of
the module controller. By doing this, it is ensured that the same cells are getting engaged
in the simulation and thereby exclude any inﬂuence from the controller.
Figure 4.5: Overview of the Parker proﬁle for both the experiment and simulation, including
the input and output.
Since it was a single battery module, it was possible to ﬁt the module inside the climate
chamber.
This ensured that the cell parameters are not inﬂuenced by the changing
temperature. However, by doing this it was only possible to use the DC-power supply
as a resistive emulator. Hence, the Parker proﬁle on module level was designed only to
discharge the battery through a resistor. The resistor was set to 1 Ωsuch that the current
and voltage of the module has a one-to-one relationship. Since it was only possible to
apply a voltage proﬁle to the module controller, the one-to-one relationship made it simple
to design the Parker proﬁle.
Due to the limited amount of voltage sensors, it was decided to scatter the voltage sensors
across diﬀerent cells. The voltage sensors have been placed as seen in Figure 4.6. There
102
==PAGE:125==
4. Experimental model validation
was a total of 6 voltage measurements on the module and these are placed on cell 1, 9, 14,
18, 23, and on the module terminals.
In addition the placement of the thermocouples from the thermal time constant experiment
described in section 3.4.3 was kept.
Figure 4.6: Placement of sensor during the Parker proﬁle at a single battery module.
Parker proﬁle on module level
Figure 4.7: Parker proﬁle - current proﬁle for validation of module model.
The Parker proﬁle on a single battery model is shown in Figure 4.7 and it contains similar
patterns as the Parker proﬁle on cell level. The only diﬀerence is the new values for the
103
==PAGE:126==
4. Experimental model validation
current. The max 60 A was speciﬁcally chosen so as not to blow the fuses in the equipment
that was used. The Parker proﬁle was also oﬀset compared to the original, as it was only
possible to discharge the battery module.
Procedure
The step-by-step procedure can be described as follows:
1. Pre-test.
a) Measure resistance between module and dump load (Connection resistance)
b) Measure/Note down “Dump load” resistance value (Or test emulator).
2. Insert module into thermal chamber.
a) Set chamber temperature to 25 °C
3. Charge battery system to approximately 85 % SOC
4. Let rest for approx. 1.5 hours.
5. Connect battery module to dump load.
a) For dump load, use the power-circuit/electronics/supply to emulate a constant
b) Set dump load resistance to Rload = 1 Ω
6. Apply the Parker proﬁle on a module through BMS.
a) Since the BMS only controls voltage, divide the Parker proﬁle with Rload to
convert current to voltage proﬁle.
b) Monitor following signals:
i. Measure cell voltage with HBM on selected cells.
ii. Measure module current and voltage with the HBM.
iii. Measure temperature on selected cells with DAQ.
iv. Record the cell engagement signal from the BMS.
v. Record SOC of each cell from BMS.
The complete test procedure including duration, description, scope etc. can be found in
appendix section C.4.
4.4.2
Results
This section will present the simulation results from the Parker proﬁle that is used to
validate the single battery module model. The simulation was performed with a ﬁxed
time-step of 100 ms.
At the beginning of the case study, all cells in the BESS was balanced with approximately
SOCBESS
init
= 50 %. This SOC has been chosen since it is the average SOC during normal
operation of the BESS, and due to the model accuracy is much better at the middle SOC
levels. The maximum SOC diﬀerence between the engaged cells that are tolerated is set to
5 % and the simulation is executed with a variable time-step and a maximum step-size of
0.01 s.
It should be mentioned that to get the simulation to run an inductance had to be included
in the module model. The solver crashed in speciﬁc scenarios, where multiple cells were
engaged at the same time. The abundant of capacitance required the solver to backwards-
calculate a large current spike to model the capacitive eﬀect. This spike became so large
104
==PAGE:127==
4. Experimental model validation
that the system became unstable. Adding a small inductance of L = 3.8 pH based on the
dimensions of the copper connectors reduced the spike. The inductance was calculated
using [62].
Ability to predict output voltage and the dynamic behaviour
Figure 4.8: Simulated vs. measured voltage, current outputs from a single module, and
error of voltage and current as well.
Figure 4.8 shows the module voltage and current from the simulation and the measurements.
The voltage and current can be observed from the ﬁrst and second plot respectively, while
the third plots show the error.
Firstly, the switching of cells is clearly observed from the plots, since the small steps in
voltage are due to the cells being engaged or bypassed.
Secondly, it should be noted that the spikes in the error are due to diﬀerent sampling
rates of the HBM and the Nerve Switch. The Nerve Switch and HBM have a sample rate
of 10 Hz and 50 kHz respectively. The slow sampling rate of the state vector occasionally
results in the recording timestamp being rounded to the nearest 10Hz, which is not the
case for the HBM measurements. This means a slight shift between the simulated and
measured voltage can occur. This is shown in Figure 4.9.
Thirdly, the diﬀerence between the measured current and simulated current is due to the
logic of the module controller. It was designed such that whenever the module voltage
would attempt to go below 12 V the module controller automatically changes it back to
12V. However, since the experiment was designed with a 1 Ωresistance and a current
proﬁle which went below 12 A, the resistance emulator was forced to cancel the resistive
mode and automatically adjust the resistance such that the applied current would result
in the desired voltage. As the voltage reach 0 the module controller would disconnect
everything, which is the reason for the voltage drop from 12 V to 0 V.
Regarding the model’s ability to predict the voltage and dynamic behaviour, it is observed
from Figure 4.8 that the simulated voltage accurately describes the measured voltage
105
==PAGE:128==
4. Experimental model validation
Figure 4.9: Illustration of the slight delay that can occur between the simulated vs.
measured voltage and current due to the diﬀerence in sampling rate.
during the complete Parker proﬁle. The depiction of the dynamic switching of cells is
following the measurements with acceptable accuracy.
The simulation had an accuracy within ±270 mV while the current is predicted with an
accuracy of ±1 A. The model seems to under predict the current by 10 mA. This is due to
the resistive emulator which is not able to keep a constant resistance of 1 Ω, but instead
overestimates as seen in Figure 4.10.
Figure 4.10: The emulated resistance based on the measured current and voltage. The red
dotted lines is the period where the resistive emulator deactivated due to lower voltage
limit violation

4.4.3
Discussion
The results have proven that the developed module model is able to predict the voltage
and the dynamic behaviour of the real module used in ﬁnal reconﬁgurable BESS with a
high accuracy of ±273.25 mV. In addition, the model suggests that the single cell model is
unlikely to be biased, since it was able to predict the cell voltages within 4.12 mV.
The analysis revealed that engaging and bypassing individual cells only make it possible to
get a rough control of the voltage. This can lead to scenarios where the requested voltage
is only partially fulﬁlled.
One way to avoid this would be to have a more precise control of the voltage. Two ways
to achieve this would be to use cell types with a lower nominal cell voltage or to have a
new method that could perform pulse width modulation (PWM) on each individual cell
voltage.
In addition, the BMS control algorithm could be further improved by considering the state
of health for each cell, hence making it possible for the BMS to consider ageing eﬀects.
Next step regarding the model would be to consider the communication and data acquisition
speed to make the model a more realistic depiction of the actual system. An example
could be when the module controller determined which cells to engage/bypass, it must
wait until each individual Nerve Switch has performed the measurements and are ready to
deliver them to the module controller.
To achieve this, it would require investigations of the module controller, the Nerve Switch,
and the communication scheme.
Finally, the Parker proﬁle should be applied to the BESS system in order to validate BESS
model consisting of a dual-string.
108
==PAGE:131==
5
SIMULATIVE STUDIES
This chapter will focus on using the validated models to evaluate their eﬃciency and
performance when interacting with other components of the energy system.
Especially three diﬀerent scenarios are of interest:
Firstly, the battery system eﬃciency and loss distribution between the various components
included in the battery system model.
Secondly, the thermal characteristics of the two developed thermal models. This will
include a validation of the thermal time constant, as well as a comparison of the thermal
distribution between the thermal module model and the thermal cell model scaled to a
module.
Finally, the developed P&O-SS MPPT algorithm (Section 3.5.4) will be investigated by
using the PV system (section 3.5) and the validated battery module model, which has
been scaled to represent a single dual-string.
The chapter is split up into these three studies:
• Battery system eﬃciency test (5.1).
• Thermal cooling test (5.2).
• PV MPPT test (5.3).
5.1
Battery system eﬃciency test
The goal of this test is to evaluate the eﬃciency of the battery system model containing 12
modules in series as described in section 3.2. This section has been split up into four parts:
• 1) The investigated scenario.
• 2) The assumptions that have been made regarding the calculations of losses.
• 3) An analysis of the results.
• 4) A short discussion.
5.1.1
Investigated scenario
The estimation of the system eﬃciency will be achieved by performing a complete charge
of the battery system from 20 % to 80 % SOC, and then discharged again to 20 % SOC at
40 A. During the test, the total power injected into the battery system will be compared
to the total power that is withdrawn from the system in order to calculate the eﬃciency.
The ideal load proﬁle is shown at Figure 5.1. However, due to losses in the system it is
expected that the total time duration of the experiment will be a bit longer.
At the beginning of the test, all cells in the BESS are balanced with SOCBESS
init
= 20 %. It
will then be charged with the maximum allowed current (40 A) until SOCBESS = 80 %.
109
==PAGE:132==
5. Simulative studies
Figure 5.1: Load proﬁle applied to the battery system to determine the eﬃciency.
Thereafter the battery system will be discharged with 40 A until SOCBESS = 20 %. The
maximum SOC diﬀerence between the engaged cells that are tolerated is set to 5 %.
During the test, the simulated BESS model containing 12 modules in series was set to
operate at 800 V. This voltage reference is chosen as this is the optimal operating voltage
for the inverter that is used together with the battery system at Bornholm.
5.1.2
Assumption for loss calculation
To calculate the energy losses from the diﬀerent components, two assumptions was made
which are:
• Hysteresis: Instant switch between OCV.
• Neglecting the capacitance.
This is followed up by a short comment regarding the compensation for the two assumptions.
Hysteresis losses
Calculating the hysteresis loss is challenging, as it depends on the voltage diﬀerence when
charging and discharging. To simplify the calculation, it is assumed that the cell voltage
is either using the discharging or charging OCV. This eﬀectively removes intermediary
voltages between the curves, making the voltage diﬀerence solely dependent on SOC.
This will give an overestimate since the intermediate voltage values would have given a
smaller voltage diﬀerence between charging and discharging.
Neglecting the capacitance
Similarly calculating losses due to the internal cell resistance is tricky, as each RC branch
splits the current.
As tracking current in each path for all 324 cells is unrealistic a
110
==PAGE:133==
5. Simulative studies
simpliﬁcation was made; the cell is assumed to only consist of the internal resistances.
This assumption can be made, as the capacitors in the cell are charged within seconds of
engagement and operates most of the time as an open circuit. Neglecting the capacitors
will result in overestimating the cell losses since the capacitance in the RC branches would
split the current until it is fully charged. Hence, if the capacitance’s is not considered the
cell losses will be overestimated.
The alternative method of ﬁnding cell losses is by measuring the voltage over each individual
cell. Doing this would increase the simulation signiﬁcantly.
Loss distribution calculation compensation
All equations used to calculate the losses from the diﬀerent components are included in
appendix D.4 on page 175. To validate the accuracy of the loss calculations, the total
calculated losses is compared to the simulated losses. When the capacitance is neglected and
the hysteresis is simulated by instantly switching between the OCV curves, the calculated
losses match the simulated losses.
To compensate for the two assumptions during this system eﬃciency test, the calculated
hysteresis and cell resistance losses was corrected.
The correction factor which was
subtracted from the calculated losses was based on the diﬀerence in the power losses when
performing a simulation with and without the given assumption. Regarding the neglected
capacitances, the simulation of the battery pack was run with and without capacitors C1
and C2. The power loss diﬀerence between the two simulations were then subtracted
from the calculations of the internal cell losses. This compensation was repeated for the
calculations of the hysteresis losses.
5.1.3
Battery system eﬃciency results
The results from the eﬃciency test will now be analysed. The result is divided into two
parts:
Firstly, the overall system eﬃciency.
Secondly, the energy loss distribution between the various simulated components.
Overall system eﬃciency
The simulated injected and extracted power are calculated using the measured voltage and
current at the battery terminals:
Q = ΣP = Σ

V BESS
Batt
· IBESS
Batt

(5.1)
During the test a total energy of 41.09 kWh was injected into the battery and of this energy
a total energy of 62.69 kWh was able to be withdrawn from the battery. This means that
3.29 kWh was dissipated as heat. Hence, the eﬃciency of the battery system was estimated
to be 95 %.
95 % eﬃciency is high, but it is to be expected from this type of battery. Additionally, it
should be noted that the estimated eﬃciency is the worst-case scenario since the inverter
111
==PAGE:134==
5. Simulative studies
is limited to a maximum current of 40 A. If the charging current were set to 10 A the
eﬃciency would be 97.8 %.
The energy yields is summarized in table 5.1.
Charge Energy [kWh]

62.69
