Table 5.1: Overview of energy yields and system eﬃciency.
Loss distribution between the various components
Figure 5.2: Contribution of diﬀerent system components and eﬀects to the total losses in
the system based on the system eﬃciency test.
The pie chart in ﬁgure 5.2 illustrates the losses caused by each component in the battery
system based on the calculations in appendix D.4 and after the described compensation for
the assumptions has been applied (section 5.1.2). This pie chart only considers the losses
caused by the diﬀerent components that have been implemented during the modelling of
the battery. Therefore, minor eﬀects such as the inductance and losses incurred when
switching are neglected.
First noticeable loss is due to the hysteresis eﬀect, which has been simulated to be 19 % of
the total losses. The hysteresis loss is especially interesting, as this loss is not necessarily
converted directly to heat losses. The energy is instead lost through the diﬀerent chemical
reactions happening within the cell during a charge and discharge. The chemical reactions
could result in by-products that absorbs and store a small amount of energy, which is then
not able to be used during the charge or discharging state.
Additionally, it was discovered that the hysteresis losses are depending on the change in
SOC, and which part of the OCV-SOC curve that was used during the cycling through
112
==PAGE:135==
5. Simulative studies
diﬀerent ranges of SOC. This means that the hysteresis losses are not aﬀected by the
current applied to the cell.
Next it is observed that 48 % of the total losses is due to the internal resistance in the cells.
Similarly, to the hysteresis, the resistances is highly temperature dependent, which means
that the losses will increase even further at lower temperatures.
As both the hysteresis and the internal cell losses are dependent on the cell speciﬁcations,
it is important to source quality cells.
Next major source of loss is the copper connectors, which are responsible for 11 % of the
total losses. This could be minimized by shortening the diﬀerent connectors as much as
possible or by using other material.
Looking at the PCB losses, Figure 5.2 shows that the majority of the losses occurs when
cells are engaged. This is to be expected as most of the cells are engaged in order to
maintain the operating voltage of 800 V. It was found that the Nerve Switch during an
engaged state has more resistance than during bypassed.
Regarding the MOSFETs, having four in parallel as suggested in [27], has reduced the
losses. However, the loss caused by the MOSFETs is still responsible for 8 % of the total
losses.
Finally, the screws which are connected at each terminal are responsible for 1 % of the
total losses. This could be a problem since the screws are essential for creating a distance
between the PCB and the cell. In addition, the screws act as a tab cooling component on
the cell terminals. The shape and the amount of material used can be improved, as this is
a very rough prototype.
A ﬁnal method to reduce the losses would be to split the string of 12 series connected
modules into two parallel strings of 6 modules in case the required voltage levels are below
500 V. By running them in parallel the two strings will only be subjected to half the
current, which would reduce the total losses by a signiﬁcant amount.
5.1.4
Discussion
The results from the battery system eﬃciency test suggests that the major sources of
loss are due to the internal resistance in the cells and the hysteresis eﬀect, with a loss
contribution of 48 % and 19 % respectively.
The cells internal resistance can be tricky to improve. However, as described in section
2.1.5 the hysteresis eﬀect is signiﬁcant in LFP cells, which probably is the reason for the
relatively high losses. Therefore, a solution could be to choose other lithium-ion cells that
do not have a nearly as great hysteresis eﬀect as a LFP cell.
Next is the connectors which has a loss distribution of 11 %. The connectors are made of
copper which is the best compromise between price and how eﬀective it is at conducting.
Therefore, an idea could be to investigate whether it is possible to reduce the length of the
connectors to reduce losses as much as possible.
Further investigation into the Simulink solvers might also uncover methods to improve the
accuracy of the calculated losses, as the diﬀerence between the calculated and simulated
113
==PAGE:136==
5. Simulative studies
losses is ﬂuctuating by 0.3 % when 2 battery modules are used.
As discussed in section 4.3.3 an improved cell characterisation at higher and lower SOC
levels will allow for a complete simulated battery cycle eﬃciency to be determined. The
none-linearity in the cell characteristics will most likely increase the losses, as the internal
resistance tends to increase in these areas of SOC.
Finally, it would be interesting to perform the battery system eﬃciency test on the real
battery system to capture the system eﬃciency while including the inverter. Especially
in regard to the harmonic eﬀects caused by the inverter or the reactive power losses due
to resonance between the capacitances found in the cells and the small inductance which
wasn’t included in the 12 module simulation.
5.2
Thermal cooling test
The purpose of this thermal test is to evaluate the behaviour of two thermal models and
to validate the calculated thermal time constant from section 3.6.
This section has been split up into four parts:
• The investigates scenarios.
• Analysis of the results.
• A discussion of the results.
During the test the measurements from the thermal experiment described in section 3.4.3
will be used to initialise and compare the thermal module model (Section 3.4.4) and the
thermal cell model (Section 3.4.5).
The thermal module model will provide a single temperature for the complete module, while
the module constructed by 27 individual thermal cell models will provide 27 measurements.
To capture a general picture of the module temperature, an average of all 10 thermocouples
as seen in Figure 4.6 will be used to describe the complete module temperature.
5.2.1
Investigated scenarios
There will be investigated a total of three scenarios.
The ﬁrst scenario investigates a simulated cooling phase similar to the thermal experiment
described in section 3.4.3. The scenario is designed such that the initial temperature
for the two models will be the average start temperature of all the 10 thermocouples
shown in Figure 4.6. The ambient temperature has been set to the ﬁnal measured ambient
temperature during the experiment, which was measured to be approximately 21 °C.
Nothing else is applied during the simulation to ensure that only the ambient temperature
determines the temperature of the modules. The idea is then to run the simulation until
the temperatures of the modules reach the ambient temperature. This makes it possible
to evaluate whether the temperatures of the two simulated modules decrease within the
expected time frame.
The two remaining scenarios will be simulated to showcase the thermal distribution within
the thermal module, that has been constructed by 27 thermal cell models.
114
==PAGE:137==
5. Simulative studies
Second scenario is designed to illustrate the thermal distribution from the inner cell (cell
23) to the outer cells. This will be achieved by having cell 23 start at 40 °C while the
remaining cells are started at 25 °C which is the same as the ambient temperature.
By doing this it should be possible to capture how the heat is transferred throughout the
module.
Third scenario is designed to illustrate the thermal equilibrium when the inner cell (cell
23) is loaded. In this case all the cells are initialized with a temperature of 25 °C and
SOCinit,cell = 20 %. A 40 A current is then applied to cell 23 such that the cell SOC
remains between 20 % < SOC23 < 80 % until all temperatures ﬁnd an equilibrium.
By doing this it is possible to capture the thermal behaviour inside the module, when a
constant load current is applied to the inner cell.
5.2.2
Thermal simulation results
This section will now present the simulation results from thermal tests by splitting it into
two separate parts:
• Scenario 1: Validation of the thermal time constants.
• Scenario 2 & 3: Analysis of the thermal distribution.
Scenario 1: Validation of thermal time constants
Figure 5.3: Simulated temperature progression during the cooling phase.
Figure 5.3 shows the temperature from the experimental data and the simulation results
of the thermal module and the thermal module constructed by 27 thermal cells.
• The orange plot represents the mean temperature of all the thermal measurements
during the thermal experiment described in section 3.4.3.
• The blue plot is the simulated thermal module temperature.
• The yellow is the mean temperature of all 27 thermal cell models.
115
==PAGE:138==
5. Simulative studies
The initial temperature for both models were set to the starting temperature of the
experiment, while the ambient temperature had been set to the ﬁnal measured temperature
of the ambient thermocouples.
It is observed as the module is cooled from 38.6 °C to around 21 °C, that the thermal
module model is accurately following the measured temperature throughout the complete
simulation. The measured temperature in the beginning is declining faster than expected
and then return to its original path. This is due to the fact, as described in section 3.4.3,
that during the ﬁrst 30 minutes of the experiment, the large garage door was opened
thereby changing the ambient temperature.
The thermal time constant for the module was estimated to be 10.21 hours based on the
thermal experimental data. This is also true for the thermal module model, as it is observed
that at t = 10.2 hours the temperature has declined with approximately 63.2 % from its
initial temperature. Based on Equation 2.10 described in section 2.4.4, a temperature
decrease of 63.2 % means that the temperature should be approximately:
38.6 °C −(0.632 · (38.6 °C −21 °C)) = 27.5 °C
(5.2)
The sudden change in the ambient temperature could have resulted in a thermal constant
that is slightly wrong. This would explain the reason behind the small oﬀset between the
measured and the module temperature.
Another reason could be that the measured temperature is an average of all the 10
thermocouples within the modules. Each thermocouple was measuring a slightly diﬀerent
temperature as seen in Figure 3.36 which indicates that the temperature is depending on
the location inside the module.
Therefore, the thermal cell model was developed, and a module was built using 27 individual
thermal cell models. By simulating each cell as its own thermal mass and the heat transfer
paths within the module, it is possible to capture the thermal dynamics and the thermal
behaviour in more detail. The thermal cell model was described in section 3.4.5.
This leads on to the ﬁnal curve (yellow) in ﬁgure 5.3. The average temperature of all the
cells within the module seems to be decreasing much slower than expected. This can be
due to several diﬀerent cases:
First of all, by considering the thermal dynamics for each individual cell in the module,
the accuracy is more sensitive to errors as the module consist of 27 unique thermal masses.
Therefore, the unnatural change in the ambient temperature during the experiment could
have had a signiﬁcant impact on the results. Hence, it is very important to have a correct
thermal time constant for the cell.
Secondly, the thermal cell model is based on multiple diﬀerent paths that the heat can
be transferred. This means that in order to get the best possible accuracy, each thermal
path must be considered. However, as mentioned the thermal cell model is still a work-in-
progress meaning that not every thermal path has been considered yet. A major heat path
which is still to be implemented is the bottom surface of the aluminium case on which cells
is placed. It is known that aluminium is a great thermal conductor, and since the surface
is rather large it could mean that the average temperature of all the cells would become
more accurate.
116
==PAGE:139==
5. Simulative studies
Temperature distribution
Figure 5.4: Comparison between with the maximum and minimum measured temperature
and the simulated module temperature.
Figure 5.4 looks more in-depth on the temperature spread. The graphs are based on the
thermal time constant experiment as well. The dotted lines indicate the maximum and
minimum measured temperature and the module temperature throughout the experiment.
Firstly, the ﬁgure shows that the module temperature is a great approximation as it is just
between the maximum and the minimum measured temperature.
Looking at the diﬀerence between the maximum-module and the minimum-module, the
largest diﬀerence is 1.84 °C and −3.04 °C respectively. This means that the thermal module
model can capture the temperature with a decent accuracy without making the model too
complex.
However, it also shows that there is room for improvement if a precise thermal management
system is to be developed. Two diﬀerent scenarios showing this will be described in 5.2.2
and 5.2.2.
Scenario 2: Thermal distribution from inner cell to adjacent cells
Figure 5.5 shows the results of the ﬁrst of the two scenarios, which was designed to capture
the thermal distribution between cells within the module. The temperature was measured
at 9 diﬀerent cells as shown in Figure 5.1. The inner cell (cell 23) was initialized with a
temperature of 40 °C while the remaining was initialized with the ambient temperature of
25 °C. As expected, the inner cell instantly experiences a decrease in temperature as it
transfers heat to the adjacent cells. The remaining cells receives the heat from the inner
cell resulting in a brief increase in temperature. Cell 14 and cell 5 are closest to the inner
cell, which means that these will experience the highest temperature increase, followed by
cell 27 and 19. Finally, the corner cells (cell 1, 9, 10 and 18) will experience the smallest
temperature increase as they are furthest away.
Scenario 3: Thermal equilibrium when the inner cell is loaded with 40 A
117
==PAGE:140==
5. Simulative studies
Figure 5.5: Thermal distribution between cells in the module during the cooling of the
inner cell (cell 23).
Figure 5.6 shows the results of the last of the two scenarios. The inner cell (cell 23) was
subjected to a 40 A charge/discharge cycle for 150 hours. To avoid any overcharge and
deep discharge of the cell, the state was switched such that 20 % < SOC < 80 % was true.
First of all, cell 23 is experiencing an increase in the temperature as expected. Furthermore,
the change between charge and discharge and the SOC level can be observed as the ripple
eﬀect.
Similarly to the 40 °C study, cells that are closer to the hot cell will increase in temperature
faster, while cells placed against walls is heated less and dissipate the heat faster. Cell
23 stabilizes at the highest temperature (32.1 °C) compared to the remaining cells, which
is to be expected since this is the only cell getting charged/discharged. The further the
distance from cell 23, the lower the steady state temperature is.
The highest recorded steady state temperature is 32.1 °C on cell 23, while the lowest
temperature was measured to be 25.4 °C on the corner cells (1, 9, 10 and 18). This is a
temperature diﬀerence of 6.7 °C, showing that there is a signiﬁcant temperature diﬀerence
between the cells.
Based on these two experiments describing the thermal distribution, it is clear that the
thermal module model has some ﬂaws which will show if a simulation is run over a longer
period of time. As illustrated in the two experiments in ﬁgure 5.5 and 5.6 the temperature
of the cells will begin to deviate from each other, and this will not be captured by the
thermal module as it is treated as a single thermal mass.
An additional diﬀerence between the two thermal models is the method for calculating
the generated heat. For the thermal module, the heat losses included every resistance as
described in Table 3.4, while the thermal cell only consider the internal cell resistances.
This means that the thermal module will experience more heat losses than the thermal
118
==PAGE:141==
5. Simulative studies
Figure 5.6: Thermal distribution between cells in the module during 40 A charge/discharge
cycle.
cell. Due to the natural temperature grading between the cells, the cooling of the complete
module will be slower. But since the thermal module is considered as a single thermal
mass the cooling will be faster.
5.2.3
Discussion
The results of the thermal experiments on the thermal module suggest that the developed
thermal module model is able to accurately describe the thermal dynamics of the module
with an accuracy of approximately 1.8 °C and −3 °C. However, the results showed that
there could be a signiﬁcant diﬀerence between the temperatures of each cell of up to 6.7 °C
which is not being considered in the module model.
To be able to describe this diﬀerence and the thermal dynamics within the module, a
thermal cell model was developed. By considering every heat transfer path within the
module, it should be possible to capture how the thermal dynamics are behaving within
the module by monitoring the temperature of each individual cell.
As the thermal cell model is a work-in-progress, the simulation results from the module
(built on 27 thermal cell models), suggest that the heat dissipation need some work. The
obvious solutions would be to include the bottom surface of the aluminium case, since
aluminium is very eﬀective at dissipating heat it should result in better passive cooling
of each individual cell. Secondly, would be to connect each of the aluminium case walls,
which are shown in Figure 3.42. This would allow the aluminium to transfer heat between
the aluminium walls and thereby increase the overall eﬃciency of the heat dissipation.
A feature which is not included in either of the models is active cooling. NSS has designed
each module with 3 fans that actively cool each individual cell by pushing cold air through
119
==PAGE:142==
5. Simulative studies
each cell below the installed Nerve Switch. This is called forced convection and has proven
to increase the eﬀective cooling by a factor of 2 when going from natural convection to
airspeeds of 1 m/s [49]. The fans are only activated if the temperature is rising above 30 °C.
To model the eﬀect of force convection, further experiments is needed to characterise the
airﬂow’s and the air pressure’s inﬂuence on cooling.
A proposal would be to design a thermal experiment that considers 100 % fan speed. The
thermal time constant could then be estimated in a similar manner as described in section
3.4.3. By doing this, two thermal time constants would have been estimated. One at
0 % fans speed and one at 100 % fan speed. A linear interpolation for the thermal time
constant between 0 % and 100 % fans speed could then be made as a lookup table. However,
this method would only work for thermal module model as its thermal characteristics are
directly related to the thermal time constant.
Regarding the thermal cell model, airﬂow can be incorporated directly into the convection
based on literature. It can be achieved based on the experiment discussed in "ISSN 2319 -
4847" [49]. The challenge is to determine the airspeed from the fan, which can be diﬃcult
due to the in-homogeneous airﬂow around the hexagon shaped cooling screw.
Alternatively, ﬁgure 5.6 and 5.5 have both proven the diﬀerence in the passive cooling
between the corner cells and inner cells. This could mean that the inner cells would require
a more sophisticated cooling method in order to keep the temperatures at a level similar to
the outer cells. As mentioned in [63] air cooling is simply not able to remove suﬃcient heat
from the battery. Instead, the cooling should be carefully targeted in order to prevent any
damage and preserve life cycle performance. In this case [64] concludes that tab cooling is
the best method to cool the battery as it ensures that a thermal gradient is not developed
across the diﬀerent chemical layers inside the cell, and at higher c-rates the tab cooling
results in three times lower loss of usable capacity under load.
However, since the cooling is already based on tab cooling (cooling screws at each cell
terminal, one solution to maintain a safe temperature on the inner cells could be to install
a water cooled system. This could be cooling passages going through the terminals at the
inner cells.
Since the corner cells, and to some extent the cells in the sides suggests that they are easier
to cool oﬀ, another method to improve the thermal management of the BESS could be to
update the BMS control such that is prioritizes the cells which are the most eﬀective at
heat dissipation.
Finally, moving forward the next step in the development of the models would be to
design a proper case study in order to validate the thermal time constants as well as the
two thermal models. A proposal would be to design a load proﬁle and then apply it on
a module. During the experiment, all temperatures for each individual cell should be
monitored. It is very important to perform the experiment without any active cooling, as
this hasn’t been considered yet.
5.3
PV MPPT test
This test aims at evaluating the MPPT eﬃciency of the proposed MPPT algorithm P&O-SS
with respect to the deﬁned control objective, which is to reach the optimal voltage points
for any given irradiance to achieve the maximum power point by engaging the correct
120
==PAGE:143==
5. Simulative studies
number of cells in the battery.
The section has been structured as follows:
• A description of the simulation model.
• A description of the investigated scenario.
• An evaluation of the results.
• A discussion of the results.
5.3.1
Overview of simulation model
Figure 5.7: Overview of the BESS and the PV model including their physical linkage and
the data connections.
As shown Figure 5.7 the simulation model will include the PV system described in 3.5
coupled together with the BESS system that was described in section 3.2. This is done to
determine how well the BESS system provides MPPT functionality on the PV system.
Figure 5.7 provides an overview of the models and how they are linked. The PV model
consist of one PV submodel, while the BESS model will have an additional submodel
called the MPPT Algorithm.
The submodel in the PV system is representing the PV system which include the physical
properties of the panel itself and the current source as shown in Figure 3.43.
The two submodels in the BESS system that was described in section 3.2: the BESS
Battery and BESS BMS, are still present. The third submodel is representing the MPPT
control algorithm from section 3.5.4.
The PV system’s current and voltage are monitored by the submodel MPPT Algorithm,
realised by feeding back the information from the PV submodel into the MPPT Algorithm
submodel.
Based on the measured current and voltage, the MPPT Algorithm then
determines the optimal voltage, which provides the MPP.
Linkage of the PV system and BESS models
The connection between the PV system and the BESS system are represented by the
submodel Model Linkage. The inputs to this block are the total battery OCV V BESS
OC,batt, the
resistance RBESS
batt
, and the PV system voltage V PV . The provided current to the battery
is based on the PV system output current IPV .
121
==PAGE:144==
5. Simulative studies
MPPT algorithm
The submodel MPPT Algorithm uses the measured voltage and current from the PV array
to calculate a reference voltage Vref for the BESS BMS. The control objective of the MPPT
algorithm is to perform MPPT and thereby maximize the power output of the connected
PV system.
The MPPT control algorithm will be explained more in depth in the following section.
5.3.2
Investigated scenario - irradiance sample
The introduced simulation model shown in Figure 5.7 will be investigated by applying
a sample of measured irradiance to the PV system. To provide a realistic estimation
of the performance of the developed algorithm, the irradiance is based on a sample of
measured global horizontal (GHI) irradiance between the 1st of October 2018 and the 14th
of November 2019. The irradiance is measured by DTU Risø Campus with a frequency of
1Hz.
The measured irradiance is given as the Global Horizontal Irradiance (GHI). GHI is the
irradiance received by a panel in the horizontal plane and can be found by the Direct
Normal Irradiance (DNI) times cosine of the zenith angle Θz plus the Diﬀuse Horizontal
Irradiance (DHI) expressed as [8]:
GHI = DNI · cos(Θz) + DHI
(5.3)
Figure 5.8 shows the 24 hours segment of measured irradiance data from the 11th of August
2019. The measured irradiance has been ﬁltered such that it only shows the time between
sun set and sun rise.
Figure 5.8: 24 hours segment of measured irradiance from the 11th of August 2019.
The irradiance will be varying constantly as seen in Figure 5.8, which means the MPPT
algorithm must calculate new reference voltages Vref in order to maintain the maximum
power point.
122
==PAGE:145==
5. Simulative studies
During the experiment, the following three techniques will be used to provide MPPT:
• P&O MPPT algorithm
• P&O-SS MPPT algorithm
• A constant voltage reference for the battery
The last "constant" voltage scenario was added to create a baseline for comparison and to
ensure that the P&O-SS provides any beneﬁt. For this test scenario, the battery control
system was fed a constant setpoint throughout the day of Vref = SI669.67V . This setpoint
was chosen as it is the average of the optimal voltage for the given irradiance data.
In order to evaluate the performance, three parameters will be used as a performance
metric:
• The MPPT eﬃciency.
• The number of switch events.
• The ability to follow the MPP.
MPPT eﬃciency is deﬁned as the ratio of extracted power to the maximum available power
throughout the simulation.
A switch event is deﬁned as the moment a cell is engaged or disengaged. The number of
switch events should be as small as possible to get a stable voltage and minimize MOSFET
switching losses.
Furthermore, it would be interesting to investigate whether the reconﬁgurable battery
system can replace an inverter performing MPPT.
Simulation boundaries
The initial SOC of the battery is balanced with SOCBESS
init,cell = 20 % and the tolerated
maximum SOC diﬀerence between all the cells is set to 5 %.
Finally, the control frequency of BESS BMS was set to 10 Hz to simulate the actual speed
of the Nerve Switch controller as described in section 3.5.1.
In addition, a "SOC reset" logic is applied, which reset the SOC of all cells to the initial
values if the cell which has the lowest SOC exceeds 90 %. This feature has been implemented
in order to simulate a switch from a charged dual-string to an empty dual-string. In addition,
if this feature were not implemented it would result in the SOC hitting the 90 % limit.
This means that the BESS BMS would start blocking cells from charging, decreasing
BESS voltage output until no more cells is available, resulting in 0 battery voltage for the
remainder of the simulation.
5.3.3
PV system model results
This section will evaluate the BESS’s ability to perform MPPT on the developed PV
system, based on the performance metric that was described previously in section 5.3.
Figure 5.9 shows the results from the complete simulation of the 24 hours of measured
irradiance.
From the ﬁgure, the two ﬁrst graphs shows the simulated voltage and power for both the
123
==PAGE:146==
5. Simulative studies
original P&O algorithm (P&O), the new steady state P&O algorithm (P&O-SS), and the
optimal voltage as well as the maximum power point (MPP).
The third graph shows the measured irradiance and the last graph shows the maximum
and minimum SOC in the dual-string. The jump from 80 % symbolises the switch from a
charged string to a discharged 20 % SOC string. From the ﬁgure it is observed that through
the whole day, the PV system at Bornholm is able to charge approximately 3.5 dual-strings
from 20 % to 80 % SOC. Furthermore, it is observed that the maximum tolerated SOC
diﬀerence between the cells is achieved while the battery performs MPPT. Thereby, it is
ensured that every cell in the BESS is used as eﬃciently as possible.
Figure 5.9: Results of complete simulation of the 24-hours irradiance.
PO: The original P&O MPPT algorithm;
POSS: The new P&O-SS MPPT algorithm;
MPP: The optimal and maximum point for the PV voltage and power respectively
Ability to follow MPP
Figure 5.10 shows a speciﬁc period of 9 seconds at around 13:30 PM. From this it is possible
to get a much better idea of the diﬀerences between the two MPPT algorithms. it is
observed from the voltage graph, that the P&O algorithm is able to follow the voltage point
which provides the maximum power. However, one signiﬁcant issue is that the algorithm
is not able to achieve a steady voltage. Instead, the algorithm is constantly oscillating
around the optimal voltage point, creating an unstable power output from the PV.
Looking at the P&O-SS algorithm, described in section 3.5.4, it is observed that is able to
follow the optimal voltage point as well. However, the new algorithm does not oscillate
around the optimal voltage point. Instead, the algorithm ﬁnds the best possible voltage
point that the battery is able to achieve by engaging and bypassing the correct number of
cells. This voltage point is then maintained as long as the change in irradiance is within
the dead-band of the "Release Steady State"-function as described in 3.5.4.
124
==PAGE:147==
5. Simulative studies
In the beginning of Figure 5.10 it is observed that the P&O-SS ﬁnds a steady state for the
voltage at around VPOss = 718 V while the the P&O oscillates between VPO = 722 −715 V.
The optimal voltage point is VMPP = 717.14 V. Therefore, the P&O-SS algorithm is
only 0.86V from reaching the optimal voltage point for the given irradiance, which was
measured to be 705.4 W
m2 .
Shortly after the algorithm has found a steady state the irradiance changes, but the
algorithm doesn’t respond which is expected. This is due to the fact that the change
in irradiance is within the dead-band of the algorithm and therefore the "release steady
state"-function is not activated.
Just before 13:30 PM the irradiance changes again, which continues to be within the
dead-band. But soon after, the irradiance changes enough to trigger the release function.
As the steady state is released just before 13:30 PM, the P&O-SS algorithm must run two
waves before it can ﬁnd steady state again.
The new steady state voltage point that is found is the same as the initial steady state.
This is due the minor change in the irradiance, which result in a small change in the
optimal voltage point. Thus, the best possible voltage points that the battery can achieve
is the same.
Figure 5.10: Zoomed in on results of complete simulation showing a 9 second period at
around 13:30 PM

algorithm, the total number of switch events that each cell experience has been reduced.
As expected the original P&O algorithm result in considerably more switch events compared
to the new P&O-SS algorithm.

MPPT eﬃciency
The MPPT eﬃciency is calculated by using the simulated power output for the MPPT
algorithms and then divide them by the extracted maximum power point, P MPP . It is
assumed that the extracted MPP is the maximum power that is able to be extracted from
the system.

5.3.4
Discussion
The results of the MPPT eﬃciency test on one complete dual-string indicates that the
developed MPPT algorithm is able to perform MPPT functionality by using the developed
BESS model. This includes the fact that the command execution speed of the BMS
controller was set to 10 Hz to simulate the execution speed of the actual system.
The P&O-SS algorithm had a ηMPPT = 99.36 % which is an improvement of 10.24 %
compared to the original P&O algorithm. But keep in mind that the MPPT eﬃciency of
99.287 % for the P&O algorithm is by no means bad. Furthermore, the P&O-SS algorithm
has improved the power output by ﬁnding a steady state for the voltage and thereby
removing any oscillations. Both the P&O and the P&O-SS MPPT algorithms outperforms
having a constant voltage setpoint (Vref = Vavg,MPP ).
The P&O is designed to have small voltage steps, to reduce the oscillations as much as
possible. This is not possible in the BESS due to the very nature of the reconﬁgurable
cell topology, where the voltage steps are directly related to each cell being engaged or
bypassed.
126
==PAGE:149==
5. Simulative studies
The standard method of performing MPPT of a PV system is to use a commercial PV-
inverter. For the commercial PV-inverter the typical MPPT eﬃciency is ηMPPT > 99.9 %
[65].
One advantage of the reconﬁgurable BESS compared to a commercial PV-inverter, is
that the BESS is able to be directly connected to the PV system without the need
for a DC-DC converter. A traditional storage system requires a DC-DC converter to
perform the MPPT and to transfer the energy from the PV system into the storage system.
This result in conversion losses which means that the total MPPT conversion eﬃciency,
ηInv
tot = ηInv · ηMPPT [65], is lower than the reconﬁgurable BESS. Since the reconﬁgurable
BESS is able to perform the MPPT and is directly connected to the PV system, the
conversion losses are avoided. Based on the highest achievable inverter conversion eﬃciency
at 800 V DC which is ηInv = 98.1 %, the total MPPT eﬃciency of the inverter is:
ηInv
tot = 0.981 · 0.999 = 0.98 = 98 %
(5.5)
This means that the BESS theoretically is better than the traditional inverter and hence,
the proposed P&O-SS algorithm is capable of performing MPPT at the same levels as
what is commonly used today making it a competitive solution. However, a proper case
study at the real system is needed to make the ﬁnal conclusion.
Furthermore, the reconﬁgurable BESS is much more ﬂexible since it can adjust the voltage
by dynamically changing how many of the available cells should be engaged.
Regarding the P&O-SS algorithm it was chosen that it would perform two waves before
ﬁnding steady state. If one wave were chosen, the settling time would have increased
according to other experiments that have been performed. However, this would result in
the P&O-SS ﬁnding a wrong steady state as seen in Figure B.1 in appendix B.1. One
wave only allows the change in voltage reference to go in one direction, which is due to the
hill-climbing eﬀect as described in section 2.5.4. Then as the change in irradiance slows
down, the algorithm will automatically start to perform 1 wave and ﬁnd a steady state
that provides a much lower power output than what is possible. Therefore, by using more
than one wave allows the algorithm to determine whether the voltage reference should
be decreased or increased. Therefore, two waves are chosen as the best trade-oﬀbetween
settling-time and MPPT accuracy.
For the future work, a change that possibly can improve the existing P&O-SS algorithm
would be to directly control the number of engaged cells instead of giving the BESS BMS a
voltage reference. This would allow for much better control of the states of each individual
cell in the reconﬁgurable BESS. Instead of adjusting the voltage reference in steps, the
battery voltage would be changed by increasing/decreasing the number of engaged cells in
steps of 1. The algorithm would then just use the element with the highest value in the
PowerArray to locate the element in the CellArray as described in section 3.5.4, which
contains the number of engaged cells.
Again, a more precise control on the battery voltage could be achieved by the following
two methods, which also was discussed in section 4.4.2.
Firstly, an idea would be to use cell types that have a lower nominal voltage. This would
allow the voltage reference to change in smaller steps, hence increasing the accuracy of
127
==PAGE:150==
5. Simulative studies
steady states that the P&O-SS algorithm is able to achieve.
Secondly, would be to control the voltage with higher precision by performing PWM on
each cell in order achieve much more accurate voltage outputs. However, this would mean
an increase in losses due to the constantly switching caused by the PWM control. This
trade-oﬀwould have to be investigated in order to conclude whether this is worth it.
Besides the simulation studies, the next step would be to implement the P&O-SS algorithm
in the actual reconﬁgurable BESS and then run it up against the real PV system to
experimentally validate the developed MPPT algorithm and PV system model.
128
==PAGE:151==
6
CONCLUSION
The aim of this thesis is to characterize both performance and dynamic behaviour of an
innovative reconﬁgurable battery system. The complete system consists of three dual-
strings with 12 modules per dual-string and 27 battery cells per module, capable of
providing up to 312 kWh. However, during this project, only a single dual-string was
modelled, as the focus was on the reconﬁgurable design and the interaction/coupling with
other components.
The characterisation was achieved by building a cell- and a battery system model in
MATLAB & Simulink®, and then validate them against experimental data. The validated
model was then used to explore the features of the reconﬁgurable battery system when
interacting with other components of the energy system, more speciﬁcally the inverter and
the PV system.
Finally, the battery model has been further expanded so that it includes the thermal
characteristics of the battery system.
First, a single LFP cell is modelled and validated against experimental data. Based on
the experimental data, it was decided to build the cell model using the EECM (Section
3.1.4). This included a voltage source, a series resistance and two RC-branches as a
compromise between accuracy and model complexity. It resulted in six variable circuit
elements, all functions of the operating conditions such as the SOC, temperature and state
(charge/discharge), realised through 3D-lookup tables. The operating conditions and the
resulting parameters for the circuit elements were characterised by using the Simulink
Design Optimizer.
Signiﬁcant eﬀort was put into developing a method to describe the hysteresis eﬀect, since
this eﬀect is signiﬁcant in LFP cells. In order to determine it, a speciﬁc experiment was
designed to capture the hysteresis dynamics. The solution was a hysteresis ratio constant
labelled as κhyst, which is a ratio between the charge and discharge curve as a function
of the change in SOC. The results from a dynamic validation procedure, labelled as the
Parker proﬁle (Section 4.3 and 4.3.2), were used to validate the cell model. The modelled
cell is able to accurately describe the performance and dynamic behaviour of the cell, as it
had an absolute average error of 8 mV when exposed to the Parker proﬁle. Furthermore,
the cell model accurately predicted the instantaneous- and diﬀusing voltages as well as
the charge transfer process. Additionally, the results of the Parker proﬁle conﬁrmed that
the hysteresis eﬀect is important to consider, since the charge and discharge OCV curve
was never fully utilized. The cell was then scaled up to represent the complete battery
on system level, with particular focus on the component losses within the system, which
included resistances such as the connectors, PCB boards, terminals etc.
The Parker test was performed on a single battery module as well. This made it possible to
capture the performance characteristics and dynamic switching of cells. The results from
the Parker proﬁle suggests that the module model can accurately predict the switching
129
==PAGE:152==
6. Conclusion
dynamics and was able to predict the voltage with a suﬃcient accuracy of ±273.25 mV.
Furthermore, the average error of four diﬀerent cells inside the module was 4.12 mV.
Additionally, the losses of a battery system consisting of 12 module was investigated
by performing a charge eﬃciency test, which included a charge/discharge of the battery
between 20 % and 80 %. The simulation results showed a battery system eﬃciency of 95 %
at 40 A. The cell itself accounted for almost 67 % of the total losses, whereas the internal
cell losses and the hysteresis eﬀect accounted for around 48 % and 19 %, respectively. PCB
traces and connectors combined contributed for around 25 % of the losses.
The remaining losses were distributed between the diﬀerent resistances (Section 3.2.3).
The hysteresis loss has great interest, since in the literature the charge and discharge OCV
curves are often approximated as a single curve, thereby ignoring this eﬀect. This loss is
not necessarily converted directly to heat. The chemical reactions inside the cell could
result in other by-products that absorbs and store a small amount of energy, which is
not able to be used during charge or discharge. Additionally, the results suggest that the
hysteresis loss is only dependent on the change in SOC, and which part of the OCV-SOC
curve that is used during the cycling process.
A static characterization of the inverter was developed, which implies that the DC side of
the inverter was represented by a single current source and the AC side was represented by
an RMS power value. Based on the operating voltage and the requested power, the inverter
will then calculate a current, which either charges or discharges the battery. The inverter
model contains a loss characteristic at 800 V, which is the nominal operating voltage.
The thermal dynamics was implemented as two separate models. The thermal module
model and the thermal cell model, which both do not consider any active cooling. To
characterise both models, a thermal experiment was designed to estimate the thermal time
constant. The experiment included a module and two individual cells. The experiment
resulted in a thermal time constant of 10.21 hours for a single module and 1.7 or 0.8 hours
for the cells depending on whether they were surrounded by other cells or isolated.
In order to validate the estimated time constants, the thermal module model and a thermal
module created by 27 thermal cell models was compared against a cooling experiment. The
results showed that the thermal module model accurately follows the measured temperature
progression during the cooling. The thermal cell model still requires some calibration, as
the predicted temperature progression was inaccurate. However, the thermal cell model
did show more potential during the thermal distribution tests compared to the thermal
module model, since the cell model describes the heat distribution and ﬂow within the
module in more detail. Additionally, the results of thermal distribution tests suggest that
the inner cells may have problems dissipating heat compared to the outer cells. However,
before making a ﬁnal conclusion on this issue, more experiments are needed to validate
the thermal models.
Finally, to evaluate the BESS system model’s ﬁdelity when interacting with other
components of the energy system, a PV system was developed from scratch. This resulted
in a PV system which is much more manageable and ﬂexible, while the simulation time is
reduced signiﬁcantly. In addition to the PV system, a MPPT algorithm called P&O-SS
was speciﬁcally designed to enable the validated battery system model to perform MPPT
at the developed PV system. The P&O-SS is an extension of the well-known perturb and
130
==PAGE:153==
6. Conclusion
observe P&O algorithm but adapted to utilize the unique features of the reconﬁgurable
battery system. It aims to ﬁnd the voltage reference, that provides the highest possible
power output from the PV system. As the P&O-SS starts to oscillate around the optimal
voltage point, the algorithm will lock the optimal amount of engaged cells so that the
optimal voltage point is achieved.
Based on the simulations, which involved a sample of 24 hours of measured irradiance, the
P&O-SS algorithm was able to achieve an MPPT eﬃciency of 99.36 % which is a relative
improvement of 10.24 % compared to the common P&O method. As a reference, supplying
a constant voltage setpoint through the day resulted in 95.55 %. However, due to the
nature of the reconﬁgurable battery system the voltage control is achieved in relatively
large steps, which means the optimal voltage point cannot be fully achieved in many cases.
A comparison of the MPPT eﬃciency to a converter performing MPPT, which has a total
MPPT conversion eﬃciency of 98 %, suggest that the BESS system is a very competitive
solution in regard to MPPT functionality.
In conclusion, a complete BESS system model that considered the transients inside a cell
has successfully been modelled, so that the dynamic cell switching, and corresponding
voltage output, are accurately described. Thermal dynamics on module level was able to
be modelled with expected thermal behaviour and potential for further expansion. Finally,
a substantial potential using the BESS system’s unique feature to be directly connected to
any DC component has been shown in this thesis. Even though the BESS only allows for
coarse control of the voltage, the BESS can outcompete the traditional converter when
performing MPPT.
131
==PAGE:154==
7
FUTURE WORK
Further work and investigations could be carried out to improve the developed models,
and to analyse more in depth the BESS model’s ability to interact with other components
of the energy system.
Cell model
The cell model could be improved even further by performing experiments that allows for
better estimations of the OCV at higher and lower SOC levels.
It still is not clear whether the cell parameters (resistances and capacitances) are current
dependent. Based on discharge experiments at diﬀerent c-rates performed by NSS, it was
found out that whenever the cell was operated above 1C the total voltage resistance for the
cell would deviate compared to the estimated resistances at 1C and below. An experiment
to further investigate this could therefore be carried out.
The developed cell model does not consider ageing and SOH, which also could be an
interesting feature to investigate.
Investigation on the computational impact by having an additional RC branch in the
EECM compared to the gained accuracy. The models so far have used 2 RC branches,
which could indicate that the dynamic response accuracy might beneﬁt by having a third
RC branch.
A ﬁnal point regarding the cell mode, would be a complete rework of the cell by utilizing
the physical modelling techniques. It would allow the model to describe each individual
process inside the cell, as proposed in section 2.1.4.
Battery system model
Regarding the ﬁnal BESS model, it would be interesting to incorporate the communication
and data acquisition speed in order to make the model more realistic. The model could
then be validated against the actual system. If the speed of the system can improve, it
can have a large impact on the system, especially when it comes to directly coupled to a
PV, performing MPPT.
This thesis have had focus on modelling the BESS itself and not the environment it is
placed. Investigating the grid connection and grid codes is therefore a natural next step.
As only a single module model was validated, a Parker proﬁle applied to the real BESS
system could be performed in order to validate the battery system model. Especially to
validate that the resistances between each module in the BESS has been correctly estimated
and implemented in the model and investigate if the parasitic inductance becomes a problem
when modules are stacked.
Regarding the calculations of the eﬃciency, a test on the real BESS system would provide
132
==PAGE:155==
7. Future work
insight regarding the accuracy of the simulation. Especially the harmonic eﬀects caused by
the inverter or the reactive power losses due to resonance between the capacitance and
small inductance is of interest, as these are neglected in the current model.
Inverter model
Regarding the inverter model, the eﬃciency as a function of power was only estimated
at one operating voltage, 800 V. The experiment trying to estimate the eﬃciency at
other operating voltages failed and was never ﬁnished during this project. Therefore, the
experiment should be performed again in order to ﬁnish the inverter model. Additionally,
further investigation on how to model the inverter switching noise and its interaction with
the BESS cell switching can be further explored.
Thermal model
Since the thermal model on cell level never was fully developed, it could be interesting
to ﬁnalize the thermal cell model. Firstly, the bottom plate of the aluminium case would
have to be implemented into the models, as this should have a large impact on the total
heat dissipation. Secondly, each side of the aluminium case should have a heat transfer
path built. Any additional heat transfers that may have an eﬀect of the thermal dynamics
would have to be identiﬁed.
By having the thermal cell model and the thermal module model, it would be possible to
investigate whether the more complex thermal dynamics in the thermal model would be a
better choice compare to a simple thermal module. Additionally, it would be interesting to
incorporate thermal management into the BESS BMS.
This thesis have been focusing on creating test and modelling a single module, but the
battery is often placed in racks of multiple modules. Further investigation can therefore be
performed on how heat travels between modules within a cabinet.
Further investigation can also be done on the cell thermal behaviour. While the cell is
modelled at three diﬀerent temperatures, all validation test were performed near 25 °C.
There is therefore room for further exploration temperature inﬂuence on the cells.
PV system and the developed P&O-SS algorithm
Finally, there is the developed PV system and the P&O MPPT algorithm. The next
interesting step would be to validate the simulations results against the actual system.
And regarding the algorithm it would be relevant to implement a feature which would
allow to directly control the number of engaged cells in the BESS, which should allow for
better and more reliable MPPT when using the BESS.

V_ref = VrOld + Step;

C EXPERIMENTS
C.1
Torque-resistance bolt experiment
Introduction
The goal of this experiment is to determine the inﬂuence of the tightness of the cell
connections in relation to resistivity. This is performed to evaluate if this have signiﬁcant
inﬂuence on the simulation accuracy and if the torque at each connection should be
monitored
Procedure
(a) Electrical diagram setup.
(b) Physical setup.
Figure C.1: Torque-resistance bolt experiment 4-point setup. The numbers in both ﬁgures
represent the same connection point.
Since the resistance is expected to be small in the range of µW, direct multi-meter ohmic
measurement will be insuﬃcient. Instead a 4-point resistance measurement is performed.
A 4-point measurement setup is based on the current being supplied independently from
the measurement equipment, such as to not measure the probe wire resistance.
The setup can be seen in Figure C.1. Note that separate current source and voltage
measurement equipment is needed. The accuracy of both inﬂuence the end result accuracy,
the current source should therefore able to provide a constant none ﬂuctuating current
while the voltage sensor should measure µ.
147
==PAGE:170==
C. Experiments
When connecting the test as seen in Figure C.1, the exact placement of the current source
clamps does not matter, as long as the resistive part of interest is the only current path
between the current source clamps. The voltage sensors should on the other hand be as
close to both side of the contact points as possible or at the same potential path.
The experiment is performed by inserting the bolt to the connection and measure voltage
and current as the bolt is tighten in steps. The resistance is calculated with R = V
I . In
addition, a torque wrench is used to tighten the connections in steps.
Experimental results
As each connection between cell and PCB is unique due to diﬀerent mounting equipment,
all three terminals on PCB board and screw to cell have been tested. The resulting test
and connection points can be seen. The PCB board is rate to 12 N*m torque setting the
upper limit. The torque wrench lower limit is 2.5 N · m, which is rounded to 3 as the lower
limit of the test sets.
Since the negative terminal does not have a connector for easy current clamp connection
and the PCB copper surface is smaller, the positive terminal was used for the negative
connection measurement. The connection setup was done with the bolts and washers as if
it was negative terminal. The tables below includes all measurement results from the bolt
experiment.
The measurement tables can be seen in Figure C.2 along with ﬁgure illustrating connection
conﬁguration and probe points.
Discussion
Figure C.3: Torque-resistance experimental result plot.

C. Experiments
In Figure C.3 the experimental resistance can be seen as a function of torque (tightness
of the bolts). Note that both the Cell-screw (Upper left) and Middle terminal (Lower
right) in Figure C.3 decays until 8N · m before the increased force do not improve the
results. This is to be expected as the surface is completely pressed together preventing
microscopic gaps. For the Positive (Upper right) and Negative (Lower left) this decays
is still ongoing and is yet to ﬁnd the optimal pressure. Further force might ﬁnd a lower
minimum of resistance, but this puts the PCB in risk of breaking.
The biggest diﬀerence between the Cell terminal and Middle PCB compared to
positive/negative PCB terminals is the contact area. For Both cell-screw and middle-
connector connection, the pads are solid material, unlike the Positive/negative terminals,
which have " sprayed" copper layer above the PCB board as contact. This might give a
more uneven and less complete contact result in further improvement to be had. Even
though the copper contact is yet to ﬁnd the optimal torque level, it is a better contact
compared to the PCB middle terminal. This is seen in Figure C.3 as these sprayed copper
contact reach 27µW and 9.1µW where as the solid middle contacts has already settled on
69µW. The Cell-Screw contact results in the lowest resistance of 3.2µW which is most likely
a result of larger surface contact, when bolt is threaded to the cell terminal. The diﬀerence
between positive and negative terminal is the included connector on the positive terminal,
which is not present on the negative terminal (But is on the middle terminal).
Conclusion
All solid terminals Cell-Screw and middle PCB terminal have an ideal contact resistance for
8 N · m and above. The Positive and negative terminals improve the results with increased
torque up until the PCB max mechanical rating of 12 N · m.
The minimum resistance for each contact was found to be - Middle:69 Positive:27
Negative:9.1 and Cell:3.2 µΩ. The main reason for the diﬀerent results is the connection
area (Sprayed copper/solid material) and whether the connection has a connector attached.
150
==PAGE:173==
C. Experiments
C.2
Inverter eﬃciency test
Background
Common bi-directional AC-DC converters is based on switching gates, as to build the ac
wave and reversely for the DC. The switching creates losses due to the gates on-resistance,
higher order harmonic, the control system and signaling power consumption. These losses
are none linear with the power transfer and the converter voltage setpoint. This experiment
aim to determine the eﬃciency graph as a function of Power setpoint and DC-voltage. As
the converter is directly connected to the AC-grid, the AC-voltage will be none controllable
around 230VRMS nominal grid voltage.
Test procedure & Setup
To measure the eﬃciency, the in-out power needs to be measured. As the converter
and battery system is a closed package, it is not possible to measure the DC bus.
The DC-voltage/current/power is therefore gathered through the battery system own
measurement system. The AC side is easily accessible and will be measured using stand
alone measurement equipment provided by DTU, connected between Lab-cell and grid.
This system can measure all AC-phases voltage, current and power.
The complete setup can be seen in ﬁgure C.4.
Figure C.4: Inverter test setup at DTU.
The procedure was rather simple, as it consisted of setting DC-voltage and power setpoint
for the battery and inverter and then write down the measurements.
Before taking measurement, the system was given 1 min to ﬁnd steady state. The battery
power set-point was reached quickly and the grid is so stiﬀsuch that the system found
steady state within the time-frame.
151

in table C.2. Note that only measurement at the rated DC voltage was performed, due to
poor result.

C. Experiments
Discussion
As seen in the eﬃciency calculations, something is clearly wrong, as the lower power transfer
indicate that more power is coming out of the inverter, than coming in. The experimental
results can therefore be discarded as invalid and not useful for the simulation. As the
result was so poor, the measurement at other voltages besides the rated VDC = 800V was
cancelled.
Then comes the question "Why" is the resulting eﬃciency not correct. The most likely
reason behind this is the accuracy of the measurement equipment. Since the eﬃciency is
expected to be around 95.5 −98.5% based on similar inverter(Mail seen in ﬁgure C.5),
the slightest error or oﬀset in the measurement could lead to high deviation in accuracy.
To ensure the validity of the measurement equipment, a second measurement was taken.
For the AC-side, the lab-cell connecting the battery to the grid is equipped with its own
ABB-measurement equipment. It was therefore possible to compare the AC measurement
with DTU measurement box to the Lab-cell ABB measurements. These two measurement
were in agreement, making the AC-measurement unlikely to be the cause of error.
The DC side on the other hand was trickier, as there were no access to the DC bus bar
between battery string and inverter. The measurement them-self was however in very
limited resolution, which could be seen in the DC voltage jumping in steps of approximately
0.5V and the current was measured with ﬂuctuating 2 decimal (Unlike the AC side which
provided 4+ decimal).
In addition, it was pointed out that the current measurement equipment within the battery
was made to measure up to 300A, the limits of the cells. The accuracy of the current
sensor where therefore inaccurate at low current. This seems to be the main source of the
inaccuracy, as the DC power would likewise by in accurate.
As the setup is very simple, very few other eﬀects could inﬂuence the result. The ﬂuctuation
of grid voltage and current could have some inﬂuence, but the grid power was monitored
to be semi stable within the short time-frame it took to write down the measurements in
table C.1.
154
==PAGE:177==

==PAGE:178==
Cell test – Parker Profile with Current Source 
Introduction 
Purpose 
The purpose of this test is to gather reference data for validating the simulation accuracy of the cell model. The experiment is 
performed to capture the dynamic performance of a single cell when current parker profile is applied. The goal is to capture the 
dynamics and the static behaviour to ensure that the steady state voltages is acceptable. 
Intended audience. 
Internally and DTU 
Scope 
 
How well does the cell follow a ramp? 
 
How fast can the cell follow the current and voltage? 
 
Measure thermal behaviour of cell during operation. 
Test description 
One cell will be used in the thermal chamber to ensure that constant 25℃ is achieved. 
Estimated time duration. 
 
Prep: approx. 4 hours 
 
Resting period: 1.5h 
 
Experiment: 1 hour 
 
Disassembling: 1 hour 
Devices under test 
 
A single Lithium-Iron-Phosphate cell 
Experimental setup 
 
Procedure  
1. Insert cell into thermal chamber.  
a. Set chamber temperature to 25C  
b. Measure initial SOC and voltage 
2. Charge cell to approximately 50%SOC 
a. Measure SOC and voltage and save the measurement. 
3. Let rest for approx. 1.5 hours. 
4. Play back load profile, see appendix 1 
a. For current, play back CSV file. 
b. Monitor voltage, current and temperature with the HBM 
c. Measure the final SOC and voltage and save the measurement.

Module Test – Parker Profile with Resistor 
Introduction 
Purpose 
The purpose of this test is to gather reference data for validating the simulation accuracy of the model created by the DTU students. 
The experiment is performed to capture the dynamic performance of one battery module when switching cells and to capture the 
static performance to ensure that steady states at each level of SOC is at an acceptable level.  
Intended audience. 
Internally and DTU 
Scope 
 
How well does the battery follow a ramp? 
 
How fast can the battery follow the current and voltage? 
 
Measure thermal behaviour of battery during operation. 
Test description 
1. The second test will use the dump load to discharge the system with the Parker Load Profile. This is done in order to capture 
the static- and dynamic performance when switching cells. 
Estimated time duration. 
 
Prep: approx. 4 hours 
 
Resting period: 1.5h 
 
Switch inverter setup to dump load: 2 hour. 
 
Experiment: 1 hour 
 
Disassembling: 1 hour 
Devices under test 
 
DC BESS – The battery system which is going to be used on Bornholm.  
Experimental setup 
Setup using the dump load.

Test procedure: Parker proﬁle - single module test

==PAGE:181==
Procedure - Dump Load 
1. Pre-test 
a. Measure resistance between module and dump load (Connection resistance) 
b. Measure/Note down “Dump load” resistance value. 
c. Measure initial SOC of all cells and save the measurement. 
2. Insert module into thermal chamber.  
a. Set chamber temperature to 25C  
3. Charge battery system to approximately 85%SOC 
a. Measure SOC of all cells (Again) and save the measurement. 
4. Let rest for approx. 1.5 hours. 
5. Connect battery module to dump load. 
a. For dump load, use the power-circuit/electronics/supply to emulate a constant resistance (As discussed in meeting) 
b. Set dump load resistance to Rload = 1Ω 
6. Play back load profile, see appendix 2 
a. For current, play back CSV file. 
b. For voltage: Play back CSV after dividing by Rload 
c. Monitor all signals from Appendix 1. 
d. Measure the final SOC of all the cells and save the measurement.

C. Experiments
160
==PAGE:183==
System Test – Parker Profile with Inverter 
Introduction 
Purpose 
The purpose of this test is to gather reference data for validating the simulation accuracy of the model created by the DTU students. 
The experiment is performed to capture the dynamic performance of the battery when switching cells and to capture the static 
performance to ensure that steady states at each level of SOC is at an acceptable level.  
Intended audience. 
Internally and DTU 
Scope 
 
How well does the battery follow a ramp? 
 
How fast can the battery follow the current and voltage? 
 
Measure thermal behaviour of battery during operation. 
Test description 
The test is split up into two parts (two procedures). 
1. The first setup will use the inverter to perform the Parker-Charge profile. This ensures that the static and dynamic 
performance and the cell switching when charging and discharging is captured. 
Estimated time duration. 
 
Prep: approx. 4 hours 
 
Experiment: 1 hour (+1.5h resting time) 
 
Disassembling: 1 hour 
Devices under test 
 
DC BESS – The battery system which is going to be used on Bornholm.  
Experimental setup 
Setup using the inverter. 
 
1. Procedure - Inverter 
1. Pre-test 
a. Measure resistance between battery/system and Inverter (Connection resistance) 
b. Measure initial SOC of all cells and save the measurement. 
2. Charge battery system to approximately 50%SOC 
3. Let rest for approx. 1.5 hours. 
4. Connect battery to Inverter. 
5. Play back load profile, see appendix 2, by setting either current or voltage setpoints for battery. 
a. Monitor all signals from Appendix 1. 
6. After test 
a. Measure the final SOC of all the cells and save the measurement.

C. Experiments
162
==PAGE:185==
System test - Effeciency 
Introduction 
Purpose 
Determine the columbic efficiency of the battery system.  
Intended audience. 
Internally and DTU 
Scope 
 
Determine the Coulombic efficiency constant by charging/Discharging the battery. 
 
How much of the power that is injected into the system can be withdrawn from the system? 
Test description 
The battery system will be charged to 100 % SOC by using the inverter and then discharged through the dump load to 0 % SOC. It is 
very important to make sure that before starting this experiment, that the SOC of all cells in the battery are starting at 0 % SOC. As to 
ensure no residual power will be present. 
The power injected can then be compared to the power extracted to determine the columbic efficiency. 
Estimated time duration. 
 
Prep: approx. 4 hours 
 
Experiment: 3.5 hours  
 
Switch inverter setup to dump load: 2 hours  
 
Experiment: 3.5 hours 
 
Disassembling: 1 hour 
Devices under test 
 
DC BESS – The battery system which is going to be used on Bornholm.  
Experimental setup 
Setup during charging. 
 
 
C. Experiments
C.6
Test procedure: Eﬃciency test - system level
163
==PAGE:186==
Setup during discharging. 
 
Procedure 
1. Pre-test 
a. Measure resistance between battery and inverter/dump load (Connection resistance) 
2. Discharge the battery system completely. 
a. Ensure all Cells SOC is 0% (Reference point) 
b. Note down actual Cell SOC for all cells. 
3. Charge the battery till max at maximum allowed current (40A) using the inverter. 
a. Measure Current & voltage at battery terminal (To determine power in) 
b. Monitor/Record signals from Appendix 1 
4. Switch the inverter with the dump load  
5. Begin discharge at same 40A until 0% SOC (Initial reference point). 
a. Measure Current & voltage at battery terminal (To determine power in)

165
==PAGE:188==
Test procedure: Thermal Time Constant 
Introduction 
Purpose 
Determine the thermal time constant for one complete battery module and individual battery cell. 
Intended audience. 
Internally and DTU 
Scope 
 
To determine the thermal time constant of a single cell. 
 
To determine the thermal time constant of a module. 
Test description 
By heating the module or cell and consequent monitoring of the temperature change as it settles back to ambient temperature, the 
time constant can be determined. From this and the material list, the heat transfer can be determined. 
Estimated time duration. 
 
Preparation time: 1 Hour 
 
Heating process: 24 hours (In thermal chamber) 
 
Cooling process: Approx. 24 hours resting in room temperature (Outside thermal chamber!) 
Devices under test 
 
DC BESS – The battery system which is going to be used on Bornholm.  
Experimental setup 
Module and cell in thermal chamber 
 
C. Experiments
C.7
Test procedure: Thermal time constant
166
==PAGE:189==
 
 
 
Module during cooling process 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
C. Experiments
167
==PAGE:190==
Cell during cooling process: Cell 1
 
Cell during cooling process: Cell 2 
 
Procedure 
The cells and module will NEVER be powered during this test. 
1. Place the module and the 2 cells in thermal chamber. 
a. Module contains 27 cell, screws and PCB control boards. 
b. The individual cell will NOT have PCB or screws attached. 
i. The cell and module may touch each other. If space is limited 
2. Set chamber to 55C (or Max allowed) and let the temperature settle (Approx. 24 hours) 
a. Monitor the temperature of cells and module. 
3. Move Module and cell to ambient temperature (Room where the temperature is undisturbed. No doors shall be opened) 
a. Place heated cell 1 and cell 2 as seen in Experimental setup with thermal isolating material. 
i. Cell 1 (Without screws) surrounded by ambient temperature cells (with cooling screws). 
ii. Cell 2 without neighbour cells. 
b. Move the heated module (With screws and PCB) from chamber to ambient room. 
c. Place thermal isolating material above below the module as seen in experimental setup. (With closed casing) 
4. Let module and cell rest without Cooling fans. 
a. Record the temperature during cooling period (With time stamps)

D CALCULATIONS
D.1
Heat capacitance calculation
The heat capacitance is found using speciﬁc heat capacitance Cρ, component list and mass.
For an object consisting of diﬀerenet component, the heat capacitance is the sum of all
components heat capacitances.
CTh = Cρ · m
(D.1)
Screw
The simplest calculation is the screw, as it consist only of aluminum. Looking up aluminum
speciﬁc heat Cρ = 903
J
kgK and measuring the weight to Mscrew = 17g. The heat capacity
CTh is then calculated using equation D.2.
CTh = Cρ · m
(D.2)

(D.4)
Module
Like the cell calculations, the heat capacity of a complete model can be described by
summing up its components. For the module, the main heat capacitance originates from
the cells them-self and the aluminium used for casing. Since the heat mass of the screws
is already calculated, they will be included. The module thermal mass including the
aluminium casing is calculated in D.6. There are other components contributing to the
thermal mass which is neglected. These components is PCB-boards, the isolating plastic
sheet between PCB and casing as well as the air in the module. These components is
neglected for simplicity. Especially air is a tricky component, as its conductivity changes
depending on the ﬂuid behavior.
Which means the heat dissipation is a function of
temperature diﬀerence and fan speeds.
CTh,Module = CTh,cell · 27 + CTh,Screw · 27 · 2 + cρ,Al · mCase
(D.5)
CTh,Module = 3950.27 · 27 + 15.35 · 54 + 903 · 11.7 = 118051.29 J
K
(D.6)

==PAGE:193==
D. Calculations
D.2
Calculation of heat ﬂows for the thermal cell model
Convection
To calculate the convection heat transfer, the2.6 and 2.7 described in section 2.4 must be
used.
Screw to air
The heat transfer coeﬃcient for the screw to air is calculated:
hscrew = 2.5 ·
0.45
0.0110.25 = 3.47[W/(m2 · K)]
(D.7)
Where C and L is given by table 2.1 assuming it is a vertical cylinder
The surface are of the screw is assumed to be a solid cylinder in order to simplify the
calculations. This means the surface area of the screw is: 968 · 10−6[m2]
The ﬁnal calculation of the heat transfer convection for the screw is:
Pconv = 3.47 · 968 · 10−6 · ∆T 1.25
Top of cell to air
The heat transfer coeﬃcient for the cell to air is calculated:
htopCell = 2.5 ·
0.71
0.04270.25 = 3.9047[W/(m2 · K)]
(D.8)
Where C and L is given by table 2.1 assuming it is a rectangular surface with horizontal
(topside) orientation.
The surface are of the cell is: 743 · 10−6[m2]
The ﬁnal calculation of the heat transfer convection for the cell is:
Pconv = 3.9047 · 743 · 10−6 · ∆T 1.25
Aluminum casing to air
The heat transfer coeﬃcient for the aluminum case to air can be calculated in two diﬀerent
ways. This is due to the fact the case has a side surface area and a top/back surface area.
These two surface areas will result in two diﬀerent convection heat transfer calculations.
hAlu = 2.5 ·
0.55
0.2210.25 = 2.0054[W/(m2 · K)]
(D.9)
Where C and L for the top is given by table 2.1 assuming it is a rectangular surface with
horizontal (topside) orientation.
171
==PAGE:194==
D. Calculations
The surface area will be depending on whether is convection from the sides or from the
front/back of the aluminum case.
The surface area of the sides is: 0.031[m2].
The surface area of the front/back is: 0.013[m2]
This ares is based on the cells because it is assumed that all the cells in module will be
adjacent to the sides of the aluminum casing. Each cell will conduct heat from to the
aluminum through their surface area, and then that speciﬁc area on the aluminum casing
will transfer the heat to the air through convection. By adding all the areas together from
each individual cells, the total area of the module is obtained.
The ﬁnal calculation of the heat transfer convection for the aluminum case is:
Pconv = 2.0054 · 0.031 · ∆T 1.25
Sides
Pconv = 2.0054 · 0.013 · ∆T 1.25
Top/back
Conduction
To calculate the conductive heat transfer, the2.5 and 2.15 described in section 2.4 must be
used.
Cell to screw
For the conduction between the cell and the screw, it is assumed that the surface area is
7.06cm2.
The length of the heat transfer path is assumed to be the length of the screw which is
11mm
In addition the screw is constructed by aluminum which has a thermal conductivity of
205W/(m · K)
This means the the heat transfer conduction from the cell to the screw can be calculated as
Pcond = 205 · 7.06 · 10−4
11 · 10−3 · ∆T
Cell to cell
For the conduction between a cell and a cell, the surface area depends on whether the cell
is adjacent to an other cell in the front/back or in the sides.
The front/back surface area of a cell is 0.031m2
From the sides the surface area is a bit smaller: 0.013m2
The length of the heat transfer path is depending on whether it is the sides or the front/back
that is considered. The length is assumed to go from the middle of the cell, where the heat
is generated, to the side or front/back of the cell
172
==PAGE:195==
D. Calculations
From the middle of the cell to the front/back of the cell, the length is measured to be
0.0305m
From the middle of the cell to the sides of the cell, the length is measured to be 0.071m
The thermal conductivity is a bit more tricky to calculate as the thermal conductivity of
all the materials in the cell can’t be conﬁrmed. Instead the thermal conductivity is used:
σth = 1/ρth
(D.10)
where ρth is the thermal resistivity which can be calculated as:
ρth = Rth · A
L

σth = 1/1.538 · 0.0897
0.05
= 0.03624W/(m · K)

Pcond = 0.03624 · 0.013
0.071 · ∆T
Sides
Pcond = 0.03624 · 0.031
0.0305 · ∆T
Front/back

of the aluminum case. Therefore, the conduction from the cells to the aluminum case can
be calculated in two diﬀerent ways.
The front/back surface area of a cell is 0.031m2
From the sides the surface area is a bit smaller: 0.013m2
The length/thickness of the aluminum case is 3mm
Finally, the aluminum has a thermal conductivity of 205W/(m · K)
This means the the heat transfer conduction from the cell to the aluminum can be calculated
as

SECTION 4 – FIRST AID MEASURES
Eye Exposure:
In case of contact with eyes, flush with copious of water for at least 15 minutes. Assure adequate flushing by
E. Data-sheets
183
==PAGE:206==
Report No.: ZJ20160425MSDS01
Page3 of 7 Pages
separating the eyelids with fingers. Call a physician.
Skin Exposure:
If the internal battery materials of an opened battery cell some into contact with skin, immediately flush with plenty
of water.
Inhalation Exposure:
If inhaled the internals of battery vomiting. Seeking Immediate medical attention.
Ingestion Exposure:
If swallowed, do not induce vomiting. Seek immediate medical attention.
SECTION 5 – FIRE FIGHTING MEASURES
Danger characteristic:
Exposure to excessive heat can cause venting of the liquid electrolyte. Battery may burst and release hazardous
decomposition productswhen exposed to a fire situation.
Hazardous combustion products:
Corrosive gas may be emitted during fire.
Fire-Fighting method& media
The stuff must equip with filtermask (full mask) or isolated breathing apparatus. The stuff must wear the clothes
which can defense the fire in the upwind direction. Remove the container to the open space as soon as
possible .Spray water on the containers in the fireplace to keep them cool until finish extinguishment Media: plenty
of water, dry chemical powder or carbon dioxide .
SECTION 6 – ACCIDENTAL RELEASE MEASURES
Emergency treatment:
If the battery material is released, remove personnel from area until fumes dissipate. Provide maximum ventilation to
clear out hazardous gases. The preferred response is to leave the area and allow the batteries to cool and vapors to
dissipate. Provide maximum ventilation. Avoid skin and eye contact or inhalation of vapors. Remove spilled liquid
with absorbent and incinerate waste.
SECTION 7 – HANDLING AND STORAGE
Handling:
1. Do not allow battery terminates to contact each other, or contact with other metals.
2. Do not put the cell or battery into a fire or heat it. Do not solder the cell directly. Do not use or leave the cell or
battery in a place near fire or heaters.
3. Do not expose the battery to excessive physical shock or vibration.
4 Do not immerse, throw, and wet a battery in water.
5 Short-circuiting should be avoided. Short circuit will reduces the life of the battery and can lead to ignition of
surrounding materials. Physical contact with to short- circuited battery can cause skin burn.
6. The batteries should not be opened, destroyed or incinerate, since they may leak or rupture and release to the
environment the ingredients that they contain in the hermetically sealed container.
7. Place the cell beyond the child packing and container.
8. Do not connect the battery directly to an electric outlet or cigarette socket in a car.
9. Be sure to use the specified charger for battery, and follow the charging instructions correctly.
10. Do not mix old and new batteries together, neither with Ni-Cd, dry batteries or another manufacturer batteries or
product.
Storage:
1. Batteries should be separated from other materials and stored in a noncombustible, well ventilated,
sprinkler-protected structure with sufficient clearance between walls and battery stacks.
E. Data-sheets
184
==PAGE:207==
Report No.: ZJ20160425MSDS01
Page4 of 7 Pages
2. Keep the sample in the cool, dry and well-ventilated place(temperature:-20~30degree C humidity:45~85%). Do
not exposure to direct sunlight for long periods.Keep away from fire and heating sources. Don’t keep the samples
with oxidizer and acid.
3. charge the battery every 6 months to the amount specified by the manufacture, even if the battery is not used.
4. Equip with relevant types and quantities of the extinguishment instruments. The storage place should be equipped
with suitable shelter materials for divulgence handling.
SECTION 8 - EXPOSURE CONTROLS, PERSONAL PROTECTION
Engineering Control:
Keep away from heat and open flame. Supply with sufficient partial air exhaust. Store in a cool, dry place.
Respiratory Protection:
Not necessary under conditions of normal use. Wear self-contained breathing filtermask if the density exceed in the
air. Wear breathing apparatus under the condition of emergency rescue or evacuation.
Eyes Protection:
Not necessary under conditions of normal use. Wear protective glasses if handling a leaking or ruptured battery.
Skin and Body Protection:
Not necessary under conditions of normal use. Wear fireproofing, gas defense clothes in case of handling a leaking
or ruptured battery.
Hands Protection:
Not necessary under conditions of normal use. Wear chemical resistant rubber .
Other Protections:
No smoking, dining and drinking water in the workplace. Keep good habit of hygiene.

Solid

SECTION 10 – STABILITY AND REACTIVITY
Stability:
Stable under normal temperature and pressure.
Distribution of Ban:
Strong oxidizer, strong acid and corrosives
Conditions to Avoid:
Fire source, heating source, disassemble, external short circuit, crushes, deformation, high temperature above 100℃,
direct sunlight and high humidity, immerse in water or overcharge.
Hazardous Polymerization:
Will not occur.

SECTION 11 – TOXICOLOGICAL INFORMATION
Acute Toxicity:
N/A
Sub-acute and Chronic Toxicity:
N/A
Irritation Data:
The internal battery materials may cause irritation to eyes and skin.
Sensitization:
The liquid in the battery may cause sensitization to some person.
Mutagenicity:
No information is available.
Carcinogenicity:
No information is available.
Others:
Since the materials in this battery are sealed in the can, the potential for exposure to the components of the battery is
negligible, when the battery is used as directed. However technical or electrical abuse of the battery may result in the
release of battery contents.

Other harmful effects:
Don’t abandon the battery into environment, may cause water or soil pollution.
SECTION 13 - DISPOSAL CONSIDERATIONS
Appropriate Method of Substance:
The battery should be completely discharged prior to disposal in order to prevent short circuit. The battery contains
recyclable materials. It is suggested recycle. Refer to National or Local regulations before handling. Disposal of the
battery should be performed by permitted, professional disposal firms knowledgeable in National or Local regulations
of hazardous waste treatment and hazardous waste transportation.
SECTION 14 – TRANSPORT INFORMATION
Note:
Lithium batteries shipped as "Lithium batteries", "Lithium batteries packed with equipment", or "Lithium
batteries contained in equipment" may not be classified as "Dangerous Goods" when shipped in accordance
with " PI965-967 section II of IATA-DGR" or "special provision 188 of IMO-IMDG Code"
